THE RED PAGE.
Parnell and His Power.
When Mr. Parnell was deposed, the backbone
of Home Rule was extracted. Deprived of his
qualities, the Celtic temperament was abandoned
to its faults. Unsuppressed by his alien will, its
incendiary temper and its passion for replies have
been fatal again as before. It is not very deeply
said that no man is indispensable. Pew men are.
One man was. Abandoned to itself, Celtic
emotionalism alternates between internecine an-
archy and moody quietism. It is under the
organic impress of great leadership alone that this
inchoate mass becomes incalculably formidable,
and gathers a disciplined force of an almost irre-
sistible impact. With the withdrawal of the
integrating ascendency of a man, the imposing
phenomenon of solidarity relaxes, separates,
vanishes; an extraordinary coherence falls back
io irrevocable confusion and the original chaos.
There is no continuity. It takes another man to
make another movement. That is the inexorable
aw of Irish politics. The Celt knows his need.
He is rebellious to symbolic title. He never
acknowledged succession through minors and
women. He craves for actual discipline and a
*eal dictator. The only political institution of the
3elt is the Chief. Mr. John Hill Burton observes
tin his “History of Scotland.” Mr. Bodley
maintains it in his book on France. Mr. Barry
s’Brien proves it in his “ Life of Charles Stewart
Parnell. ”
* * * *
This book leaves the portrait of a memorable
igure to be realised and the problem of a mysteri-
es nature to be elucidated. It remains for some
Froudeof thefuture to put Parnell upon the literary
sanvas—to make intelligible the compelling aspect
>f command ! the atmosphere of tacit sovereignty
that there was about the man. From that canvas
his resting eyes, observing and impenetrable, ought
to follow the mind like the rememberable eyes of
Philip IV. in the most solemn portrait m the
world. In later years his shapely and deceptive
beard concealed a-jaw that recalled at once the
remorseless masses of bone indicated in Holbein’s
drawings of so many of the Tudor nobles as well
as of their sovereign. Sooner or later, Parnell
must be the subject of one of the great pictures of
literature.
* * * *
The power and the tragedy of Parnell’s career
require great Comparisons. His fate was as tragic
as that of Antony or Mary. Among his contempo-
raries, Bismarck only was like him in epic cast of
ch&racter. In' English politics the character of
Parnell in simple solidity, deep craft, conquering
force, and the ruling ascendency of sheer non-
scrupulous strength, takes us back to Cromwell.
* * * *
Parnell as a racial product was curiously enough
the most typical representative of the English-
speaking world that has yet been seen. His stock
was of strong blood and strong bend. The family
of Parnell oame from Cheshire, and matured
through two centuries in that Irish environment
which seems to develop the highest governing
capacity of English blood. On the other han'd,
Mr. Parnell’s mother represented a name as Scot-
tish as Stewart and one as Welsh as Tudor, both
brought through the American medium. English
extraction, Irish atmosphere, American maternity
deriving from Scotch and Welsh blood—the squire
of Avondale wag the microcosm of the English-
speaking races.
* * * *
Until he was nearly thirty he was as mere a
country gentleman as Cromwell himself ; satur-
ated with his mother’s typical American
“notions” —typical up to yesterday —of British
dominion ; and living in a silent and gathering
dislike of English rule in Ireland. It was his
fixed idea that Irishmen were despised by the
ruling race ; that the system of government in
Ireland was fundamentally a caste-system ; and it
was not a bit out of any philosophic conviction of
the wisdom of Mr. Butt’s scheme of Home Rule,
but with the determination tp break the arrogance
and ascendancy of the British caste in Ireland,
that Parnell entered politics. He appeared in
them out of the abrupt machine. It was thought
by the fluent and genial types Who accepted this re-
served and inarticulate recruit that he would be a
respectable nullity, able to pay his own expenses.
He was destitute of demagogic arts; he was worse
than what is understood by a wretched speaker.
He was inarticulate, capable only of painful
efforts to be otherwise. He had the repellent
insularity of an iceberg. It seemed impossible to
conceive a type less adapted to a political career
or more completely lacking in manner and mind
of all qualifications for acquiring the smallest fol-
lowing in Celtic politics. But he had the rare
touch to which the Irish, whenever they feel it,
respond by immediate instinct. He was a
phenomenon among the eloquent politicians. It
was not only that he learned in the end to speak
as he desired, in sentences of clear steel—he could
do something. He could do more than demon-
strate :he could prevail. He could put an end to
confusion and ineffectuality ; he could employ all
forces, stimulate all talents, combine all interests
and all passions—the Church, the Fenians, the
farmers and the towns —he could direct, rule them.
He could lead and he could win. It was the touch
of supreme leadership that began to send the fusing
current through the country, and to set up the
process of electric welding that made five-sixths of
Ireland one in a massive discipline that was with-
out precedent and will probably remain without
parallel. It would be as useful to speculate upon
what France would do under another Napoleon as
upon what Ireland would do under another Par-
nell. Some things history does not repeat.
t * * * •
Sir Charles Cavan Duffy has frequently pointed
out that Mr. Parnell had not the slightest claim
to hare invented the policy of Independent
Opposition. There is nothing very recondite
about the idea of Independent Opposition. The
only difficulty about that policy is to find the
masterful hand to work it. The dangerous prece-
dent set by Mr. Parnell’s Parliamentary method
is, after all, not so easy to imitate. When he
entered Parliament he found Mr. Butt’s party,
with the rugged exception of Mr. Biggar, subject
to the Irish desire to please. They stroked the
shell of the tortoise. Parnell began to break the
shell. That is what attracted the attention of
Ireland. It was realised that here was a man
whom even the whole House of Commons could
not break, but who might break the whole House
Of Commons. That was the secret of his power.
m * » *
We know what he did. He was thirty when he
began, and he died at forty-five. He disorgaiused
the House of Commons; reversed the traditional re-
lations of the races by making Englishmen furious
while he remained calm; wrested all constitutional
forms to revolutionary ends; made Ireland un-
governable except by himself ; extorted more
valuable concessions for Ireland ungovernable than
Ireland submissive would ever have received;
paralysed the great Liberal majority of 1880;
overthrew Mr. Gladstone's government; put Lord
Salisbury and the Conservatives into power, and
persuaded the Constitutional party to hold re-
markably civil language towards treasonable agi-
tators ; threw the Irish vote in Great Britain lor
the first time against the Liberal party ; attained
the balance of power at a General Election —was
for a moment the Warwick of the Empire ; forced
Mr. Gladstone to capitulate ; placed Mr. Glad-
stone again in power ; saw a Bill that would have
made him autocrat of Ireland rejected by a majo-
rity of thirty only in the House of Commons;
drove the most respectable of great journals to
the exotic course of attacking him on charges of
condoning assassination by fac-similes of letters
that were forged ; defeated the Times in the last
and most dramatic of his victories —only to bo
ruined by the divorce-oase, as completely as was
Queen Mary by the Casket Letters ; to be excom-
municated by Mr. Gladstone ; deposed by tlui en-
thusiasts who had re-sworn eternal allegiance 24
hours before ; hounded by his own pack ; crushed
by the blind forces hurled upon him by the
Catholic Church ; and to die very darkly, leaving
his party to irremediable anarchy, and his cause to
certain extinction.
* * * *
Mr. Gladstone said: “Parnell was the most
remarkable man I ever met. I do not say
the ablest man; I say the most remarkable,
and the most interesting. . . . He was a most
extraordinary man.” Mr. Chamberlain ac-
cepts him decisively for “a great man,” and
adds that he was unscrupulous, like Napoleon.'
Sir Robert Edgeoumbe expresses a common
thought: “Of all the men I ever met, Mr. Cecil
Rhodes alone equals Parnell in possessing that in-
definable quality, the power to lead men.” Mr.
Rhodes reports, for his part, that “ Parnell was
the most reasonable and sensible man that I ever
met.” Sir Charles Dilke gives his feeling happily :
“ Dealing with him was like dealing with a foreign
power,”
* * * •
To these tributes elicited by Mr. Barry O’Brien
we may add others. After Parnell’s death, Mr.
Frederick Greenwood hazarded the opinion that,
in the fatal precedent he had set of the perversion
of Parliamentary machinery to revolutionary pur-
poses, the career of Parnell was perhaps the most
significant Parliamentary event since the Whig
Revolution of 1688. Mr. John Morley compared
Parnell with Cavour in his realistic power of view.
The Times, the other day, compared him with Bis-'
marck, and gave it that his “ruinous force of
will” was even greater than Bismarck’s.
* • * •
With all this, it might still be possible not
to understand why Parnell was indispensable.
Mr. Justin M‘Carthy, in a passage of admirably
candid and damaging confession, puts his finger
upon the exact essential of the truth —
He was not liked by thejparty as a whole. But like or
dislike, all bowed to him, because all felt that he was the
one man who knew what to do in moments of difficulty,
and that he was always right. He had the genius of a
Commander-in-Ohief. It was that which gave him his
power. Others of us might be useful in fixing lines of
policy in advance. But when a crisis arose, when some-
thing had to be done on the instant which might have a
serious effect in future, we were no good. We were para-
lysed. Parnell made up his mind in an instant, and did
the thing without doubting or flinching.
• * * *
That explanation goes to the root. All the in-
genious plans of the excitable Celt will go to
pieces in a crisis. At the psychological moments
upon which the success of great enterprises de-
pends Parnell alone had the cold and devising
brain, the competence in emergency. We are re-
minded of the pregnant remark of Lord Roberts :
“ I have known many natives whose gallantry
and devotion could not be surpassed, but I have
never known one who would not have looked to
the youngest British subaltern for support in time
of difficulty and danger.”
• * • *
Ireland, in short, was the island of Delos, and
Parnell was the peg, the point of stability. ' To
pull away that peg was to pull that island to
pieces and to strew tLe wave. When Mr. Glad-
stone abjured Parnell he did what no mere enemy
could have done—he mined the Irish-party,
destroyed Home Rule, and was the fatal
agent of Unionism. When the Irish mem-
bers preferred Mr. Gladstone to Mr. Parnell
they did exactly what Mr. McCarthy declares
them apt to do; they took the wrong step in
an emergency. It was the power of coping with
crises possessed by Parnell alone that made the
characteristic cleverness of his followers’ inexpres-
sibly trivial by comparison. The one central
reason for holding by the leadership of Parnell, at
any hazard, was simply that Parnell was indis-
pensable. With Parnell everything might have
been retrieved. Without him nothing could be.
* * * *
The formidable fighting power of the Celt, when
dominated by a great personal force, and the im-
potent confusion of the Celt when released from
strong leadership, are a very curious problem. The
Irish have an extraordinary passion and aptitude
for politics. In organic enthusiasm, concerted in-
telligence, persuasive exposition, irritating criti-
cism, tactical instinct, Mr. Parnell’s party was
certainly the most effective ever seen in a Parlia-
ment. But where is that party now? It has
vanished no less completely than Hans Breit-
mann’s. Except Mr. Parnell, its items are what
they were. Without Mr. Parnell, the same men
are not the same. The once irrepressible and
effective items are lax, dull, contemned. Some
inspiration has evaporated. Mr. Gladstone in the
latter days saw their condition, and, in his con-
versation with Mr. Barry O’Brien, spoke of it as
something disastrous and inexplicable :
Healy was very clever ; he made very clever speeches.
I do not know what has become of him now, but under
Parnell he was admirable. . . . He (Parnell) succeeded in
surrounding himself with very clever men, with men
exactly suited for his purpose. They have changed since ;
X don’t know why. Everything seems to have changed.
This is a pathetic passage. Everything is changed,
indeed. Why is everything changed ? It is, let
us repeat it, because the Irish temperament, de-
prived of Mr. Parnell’s qualities, is abandoned to
its faults.
• * * •
Mr. Healy may be accepted as the .accurate
antithesis of Parnell and as the essential type of
the Celt in politics. Mr. Healy is an embodied
intelligence. Beside his preternatural brightness
of mind Mr. Parnell appeared a dull person. In
the days when a split in the Irish party seemed
unlikely and only desirable, it was a dear specula-
tion, often to bo seen in the Parliamentary corre-
spondence of the newspapers, that Mr. I lealy
might one day depose Mr. Parnell and lie
king. But Mr. Mealy is a leader who would
gesticulate with his sceptre and hurl the orb at a
demurring head. Than Mr. Healy there neverwasa
more Celtic illustration of the fatal incompatibility
between temper and policy. He is a most shrewd
and inventive politician ; but he is possessed by
the genius of retaliation. Whatever becomes of
his salvation he will shoot his arrow. Ho is a
born lieutenant. One of the saddest results of
Mr. Parnell’s disappearance was the waste of Mr.
Healy- “I do not know what has become of
him now,” said Mr. Gladstone to Mr. Barry
O’Brien, “ but under Parnell he was admirable.”
That is the cut of most subtle unkindness, which
Mr. Healy must have felt more than he bas felt
anything since Mr. Parnell died, and it leaves very
little to be said.
* * * *
Recrimination, the passion for replies, is the real
genius of Celtic anarchy. The Irish instinct is
never to conciliate an opponent who has made one
angry, but to throw one’s whole soul into the work
of replying to him in such terms as shall make
him an enemy for ever. It is by the passion for
replies that Mr. Parnell’s party has been broken
up into insensate factions equally barren in them-
selves, equally injurious to each other, and equally
ridiculous to their common opponents. How ine-
radicable is the passion for replies of the Celt in
politics has recently been shown by a most signifi,-
oant instance. Mr. Healy is merely the charac-
teristic type of the Celt in politics, in him the
I'age for recrimination would be expected. Sir
Charles Gavan Duffy is absolutely the highest
type of the Celt in politics, and lie has out-
lived all the controversies of Ills career. It
was profoundly suggestive to observe that a con-
spicuous feature in Sir Charles Gavan Duffy’s
autobiography, “My Life in Two Hemispheres, ”
was an unsparing reply to John Mitchell, who has
been a very long while dead.
• * * *
The lock-mouthed master of loose-lipped men,
Mr. Parnell, amid the volatile talents and passion-
ate levities of his followers; had the absolute fixity
of purpose with which feeling had never any more
to do than emotion with the laws of gravity. He
was equally beyond the contagion of enthusiam
and the provocation of hate. He was unexcitable.
There never was a more complete incarnation of
the Carlylean man, a more _ remarkable exponent
.of the power of silence. His imqaobility and his
' silence were deeply related. As he neither was
touched by devotion nor irritated by dislike, and
as the exercise of words was in itself distasteful,
he had no impulse to respond to supporters or to
waste reply upon opposition which he intended to
crush and did not care to answer. Mr. Parnell
was exempt from the passion for replies.
*' * * * *
He first appears to have made a profound im-
pression upon Mr. Gladstone when the latter
Fastened upon him a quotation “outrageous in its
lawlessness,” but never spoken by Parnell. The
Irish leader neither winced nor protested. He did
not object, and did not reply. He spoke privately
to Mr. Gladstone, and pointed out the error. It
is exactly such incidents which usually set the
whole Irish benches in a tumult, and produce the
Parliamentary “ scenes, ” in which some Irishman
is certain to injure his reputation in vindicating
his innocence. The famous indictment of Mr.
Forster was deeply designed to make the Irish
benches mad. But Parnell did not reply. When
the Lord Mayor of Dublin appeared at Morrison’s
Hotel with a cheque for £40,000, the civic dig-
nitary commenced a speech that would probably
have been adequate to the occasion. He began,
“ Mr. Parnell ” Mr. Parnell at once replied,
“I believe you have got a cheque for me.” The
Lord Mayor.had a cheque. Mr. Parnell put it in
his waistcoat pocket. There was no more function.
When shown for the first time the forged letter
charging him with an infamy, Mr. Parnell did not
invoke the gods nor apply epithets to the Timet.
He observed, putting a meditative finger on the
document, “ I did not make an ‘s ’ like that sinoe
1878.” When Mr. Cunynghame infonned him of
the findings of the Special Commission, half of
which were hostile, Parnell remarked, “ It is al-
most what I would have said myself.” Without
any change of expression he could look down
mutiny. It is needless to multiply proof, that while
the talent of his followers was subversive and de-
structive, his power was controlling and structural.
The lack of the architectonic faculty which Mat-
thew Arnold notes as the essential weakness of
the Celt in literature is also his weakness in poli-
ties. Mr. Parnell was the only great building in-
fluence that Nationalist politics have possessed.
His party was full of men who possessed the com-
mon kinds of cleverness in very considerable
degress, lie alone had distinction. Amid their
versatility ho alone had mass.
« * * «
Parnell was more important than Home Rule.
Home Rule had to be worked as well as won, and
he must have been a very credulous entli tiast
who did not know that only Mr. Parnell could
have worked it to the end of establishing the solid
framework of a new and successful order of things
in Ireland. The pity is that the first Home Rule
Bill did not pass. With its provision for the
exclusion of the Irish members it would have made
Mr. Parnell aut<x;rat of Ireland. He would have
become at ot\ce an Imperial force as strong as Mr.
Rhodes. He had a deep idea for the settlement of
Ireland. All Nationalist Ireland he had united
—priests, Fenians, tuid farmers. There remained
Unionist Ulster and the Irish gentlemen. His
first work under a Home Rule system would have
been to bring them in. Parnell was far too
great a man to pronose to govern I reland against
Ulster and the Irish gentry, or in any other way
except with them and through them. We see in
Mr. Barry O’Brien’s book the depth of his scheme;
it determined his opposition to the Plan of Cam-
paign. The land agitation he seems to have re-
garded as having secured for the tenant as near an
approximation to perfect justice as any class of
mankind can hope to enjoy in an imperfect world.
Parnell held that work to lie sufficiently done. It
had embittered that separation of classes which
was itself the social curse and the political weak-
ness of Ireland. To bring the Trish gentry and
Unionist Ulster into the new system in sup-
port of an Irish Parliament one thing was
necessary—-to drop the Land Agitation. This
is what Parnell proposed to do, and from
this point of view he never showed profounder
statesmanship than in his opposition to the Plan
of Campaign. Under a Home Rule Parliament
he would have dropped the land agitation. Within
six months he would have shifted his base, and
Belfast and the landlords would have been the
support of his power. Ireland would have become
a whole. The most piquant and pregnant passage
of Mr. Barry O’Brien’s two volumes is the con-
versation upon this subject between Mr. Michael
Davitt, essentially a democrat, not a Nationalist,
and Mr. Parnell, essentially a Nationalist and no
democrat
Mr. Davitt: “ But, Mr. Parnell, how on earth could you
resist attacking- the land system as a whole after all your
speeches? If you were Irish Secretary in an Irish Parlia-
ment', how could you defend yourself in the face of these
speeches? What would you do?" Mr. Parnell: “The
first thing I should do would be to lock you up.”
And he would have done it. He was of that
calibre. * >
-* • - • *
So much Mr. Parnell might have made of Home
Rule. No one else could have made anything
more with a Parliament than the fiasco they have
made of the movement. Parnell without Home
Rule gave the country what Home Rule without
Parnell neVer could have given—union, discipline,
“victory and law,” some perception of the nature
of political solidity, a new structure and a new
spirit. Parnell was as much more important to
Ireland than Home Rule, as Napoleon was more
important to France chan a Constitution. The
possession of Parnell was more important than the
hypothesis of a Parliament. It was because of the
enormous and characteristic failure to appreciate
that fact, in the moment of a crisis, that Ireland
has no Parliament and no Parnell. — Condensed
from Louis Gaevin, in the Fortnightly Review.

course of home treatment especially prepared to suit each case. Nervous Debility is not a chance or
natural affliction. In its early stages it is not attended by a great amount of discomfort. It is an
affliction that begins as a mole-hill and ends as a mountain. At first, the only symptom noticeable
is a feeling of unrest, attended by gloomy forebodings of the future. Your sleep at night is not
peaceful. By and bye you have an aversion for society —you are troubled with stupid ness, bashfulness
and loss of energy. You soon become ambitionless, and your purpose in life is aimless. You find
little enjoyment in life, and just at this stage your thoughts run more or less to suicide. Ihe cemetery
looks beautiful, and you think of the peaceful slumber of those beneath the sod. You grow melan-
choly. Life becomes visionless and tiresome. You become troubled with an aching back, and if you
indulge in excesses there is likely to develop a breaking-out of pimples. It is the broad and certain
road to PREMATURE DECAY. Stop for a moment. Call together your better nature. Don’t
despair. Think the matter over coolly and calmly. You need treatment for Nervous Debility.
Consult us, either at our office or by mail, and learn the extent of your affliction. We can cure you
speedily and permanently. We can regain your lost hopes—bring back the lustre to your eyes, and
make of you a perfect man —with an object in life—ambitious —successful. Don’t delay. Immediate
action is far the best.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Prospect.
I am the last of all my race,
My race, that hath no goodly sons,
They live like devils in their place
Lame, barren, festered, foolish ones.
I know I am not such as they—
Some strong strain flows my blood within,
And I can read the bitter way
That men have travelled for their sin.
I tired of their inhuman wiles
And built myself a secret lair,
I found old books within the piles
That rise in ruin everywhere ;
And there I read how men had been
Great, glorious, godlike, wise, and strong,
With minds alert and bodies clean,
With hope for Right and hate for Wrong,
Until an evil thing began
To taint their flesh with rot and rust;
A poisoned garment grew to man
Of city life and city lust.
And city into country grew
Utxm one evon plan designed,
Until the poison filtered through
The farthest races of mankind.
Then many died, nnd some were slain,
And some made Death’s desire their own ;
And all were weary in the brain
And most were sterile as a stone.
All women wandered harlotwise,
And no man living said them nay :
Of such a few were born, whose eyes
Foresaw what mine have seen to-day.
I, ’mid these madmen sane alone,
I see the wheel of Fate complete,
See man’s fair flesh n carrion grown
For lusts to lash and sores to eat.
His fairer soul that Nature gave
To love her well with sight nnd sense,
A charnel-house, an opened grave,
Filled full of frantic iini>otonce.
They goad me with their evil hate,
They mock me in their brutish play :
Lord, thou hast kept the world too late —
Come quickly with the Judgment Day !
The hour speeds on ; no child is born ;
Their loins are barren with their shame;
The veil of Destiny is torn,
And man is nothing but a name.
I am the last of nil my line,
My line, that bore such goodly fruits;
These others are not kin of mine
But born of Satan and the brutes.
Via
THE PAGAN.
On tbe River.
Beneath the bridge's span of arch
I hear the rattle of the cars,
And note the never-ending march
Of tram-lights like a flash of stars.
They stain with orange and with red
The smoke of factory and train,
And purple mists from river-bed
And glistening silver of the rain.
A new moon through the rags of cloud
Peeps coyly in a mist of tears ;
The wind that yesternight blew loud
Whistles a space and disappears.
And o’er the river’s peaceful tide
I poise awhile my dripping oars,
And see before me, spreading wide,
The silent waters, lonely shores.
Of all the gifts to days and nights,
There is one gift for you and me—
The promise that, beyond the lights,
Some night of rest there yet may he.
Melbourne.
E.A.V.
The Incarnation.
I kneel within the temple of my love.
Tn solemn silence, through the twilight blurring
Thine image looks upon me from above.
No doubt is racking me; no breath is stirring.
And as in ecstasy I turn my gaze
Upward to thine, half waking and half dreaming,
I see upon thy brow, white through the haze,
The halo of divine perfection gleaming.
But presently there shudders in ray brain
A consciousness of subtle desecration,
The presence of some element profane,
The impulse to rebellious violation.
A thrill, that sends an angry flush of shame
Into my cheek, is trembling through my semes
Deliciously, and—whispering a name,
The name my soul adores and reverences—
Is gone again, has vanished into nought:
A pang scarce palpable, a vision fleeting,
A hint, a flash, the fragment of a thought,
A faint suggestion— but my heart is beating !
Uneasily I rise ; my eyes grow blear ;
There courses through my veins some magic potion.
A new aroma fills the atmosphere
And turns to red desire my pale devotion.
The transport of austerity has flown ;
The breathless spell of worshipping is broken.
I feel a longing I have never known ;
I hear a language I have never spoken.
I throw the panels open, and a flood
Of rhythmic song, that times my heart’s vibration,
Of summer air intoxicates my blood.
The incense of a passionate temptation
Drifts like a sacrilege o’er nave and shrine
Through the religious calm. And coldly dreading
Some sign of wrath, I raise my, eyes to thine.
And lo ! A blush —a human blush is spreading
Across thy saintlv-fair ascetic face.
A minute yet—that awe and wonder bridle —
And in one palpitating, hot embrace
I find the Woman, where I saw the Idol !
VON KOTZE.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
* Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, S000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-

which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is note

6s. 6d. added for postage.

Sie
SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 4, 1899.
The Saxon Heptarchy: Australian
Edition.
The Bulletin offers eleven Federal amend-
ments for the consideration of the Premiers’
Conference :
(1) That all the public debts of the federating provinces
be taken over by the Commonwealth and consolidated
into a uniform Federal stock.
(2) That after the provincial debts have been thus
taken over no province shall borrow money on its own
separate account under any circumstances whatever.
(3) All State railways shall be handed over to the Com-
monwealth, and all railway construction and railway
management shall he vested in the Commonwealth
Government. It shall be an instruction to the Common-
wealth Government that in all future railway building it
shall utterly ignore provincial boundaries and separate
provincial interests, and its object in every case shall be
to give the cheapest possible access to the nearest avail-
able port or city.
(4) All rivers flowing through more than one State shall,
with all their abluents, be under Federal control.
(5) The Federal capital shall be built within a Federal
territory of (say) 5000 square miles, of which the Common-
wealth shall he the sole ground landlord for ever.
(6) The site of the capital shall be in the most central
situation conveniently available. It shall be in the
interior, remote from possibilities of invasion ; and as far
as possible it shall be placed where the natural increase of
population will develope new country, and thus make
land which is now practically worthless into a Federal
asset of great value.
(7) In the case of disputes between the two Federal
Houses the matter at issue shall be settled by a joint
sitting at which the vote of a bare majority shall decide.
Said joint sitting to be held within a month from the
arising of the deadlock.
(8) In all cases where there is any reasonable doubt as
to whether certain powers belong to the Federal or the
State Parliaments, the Federal Parliament shall have the
benefit of the doubt.
(9) If any district amounting to not less than, say,
30,000 square miles of territory demands separation from
the province or provinces to which it is now attached, and
a poll taken by the Federal authorities shows that two-
thirds of the adult population voting is in favor of such
separation, it shall be in the power of the Federal Parlia-
ment to grant separation, and either create the territory
in question into a separate State or attach it to another
existing- State, as the inhabitants may desire.
(10) The Federal Government shall have power to estab-
lish a uniform Civil and criminal code throughout the
Commonwealth.
(11) The control of Education shall be in the hands of
the Federal Government.
* * * *
The Bulletin submits its little list of
amendments to the Conference of Premiers
with no definite hope that any good will re-
suit. It puts them on record mainly for its
own satisfaction, so that in after years its
grand-children may be able to turn up the
time-yellowed leaves of this issue, and draw
attention to the fact that the old man
preached much solid wisdom to a deaf
and idiotic generation in the days of
long ago. At the present moment, in fact,
The Bulletin seems to be in a smaller
minority than it was a year ago, and, in a
certain sense, it likes the situation. Every
prophet who had a true story to tell was in
a minority in his own day. The advent of
dreary Dickson to the Premiers’ conference
appears to have strengthened the provincial
and correspondingly weakened the Austra-
lian element in the show. And most of the
amendments submitted by the New South
Wales Parliament the consideration of
which amendments is the real business of
the Conference—go to further weaken the
Australian spirit of the Constitution, which
was a much diluted spirit at the best. The
demand that one province shall have a lien
on the Federal capital—that the rivers, in-
stead of being Australian property, shall be
measured off'into lengths and that each pro-
vince shall lurk separately on the banks of
its own bit of stream—that the present
awkward and unwieldy provincial boundaries
shall be fixed for ever, or as near to it as
may be—that the debts shall be kept separ-
ate—and that each province shall manage its
own railways so as to keep up a perpetual
railway war with the next province, and
build them in accordance with what it re-
gards as its separate interests, not the
interests of Australia, are all signs of a
further falling away from the national idea.
So The Bulletin starts business once more
as a voice crying in the wilderness. The
wilderness is as good a place as any other to
cry in this hot weather.
* • •* «
And it asks the New South Wales politi-
cians, who seem to be running the largest
part of the proceedings just now, if it isn’t
possible for them to look j ust for once at the
Australian aspect of the business, even
from purely selfish motives, and let the
narrow local aspect slide. New South
Wales is the most populous province ; it
represents over one-third of the people of
Australia now, and there is no reason why it
shouldn’t represent one-third or more for
centuries to come. If the railways are
federated New South Wales will own over
one-third of 11,000 miles of line—equal to
3666 miles—instead of being the separate
owner of 2600 miles. If the liabili-
ties are pooled it will owe about £5O
per head of federal debt instead of
about £5O per head of provincial debt.
It will be one-third boss of a whole conti-
nent instead of whole boss of one-tenth of
a continent. It will have one-third interest
in the six capital cities of Australia and Tas-
mania instead of being complete owner of only
one, and that not the largest. It will have the
principal voice in the education system of
all Australia, and the largest share of
the ownership of the Federal capital. If the
provinces throw in their lot together unre-
servedly-*- the' people of New South Wales
will have the leading interest in and the
largest control of all Australia ; they will be
principal boss in Rome instead of being sole
boss in their own village. New South Wales,
under an enlightened policy, ought to be the
leading advocate of complete union instead of
one of the notable defenders of parochialism,
because it has more to gain by union and
more to lose by provincialism than any of its
neighbors.
* * * *
This is the purely selfish view of the case,
but it is put forward as an appeal to
those who refuse to consider any other
aspect. But it would be better—a thousand
times better—if the selfish view could be
wholly forgotten. Australia is now where
England was in the days of the Seven Saxon
States. It was a woeful shock to these petty,
one-horse monarchies when they were obliter-
ated and a united England was made out of
their ruins, and the kingdoms of Kent and
East Anglia and Wessex probably viewed the
horrible circumstance just as New South
Wales views its very similar circumstance
to-day. Nobody wants now to go back to the
days of the Seven Kingdoms, or remembers
. that he belongs to the monarchy of Kent,
and ought to look with loathing on the
monarchy of East Anglia, across the estuary.
Nobody remembers now that, when the
union was made, he should have arranged
for the absolute ownership of “ his ” rivers,
or that the commerce of Essex should have
been compulsorily dragged a long way round,
at great expense, to Yarmouth, because the
more adjacent London is located in the beastly
foreign State over the border—London being
to the old East Angle something like what
Melbourne is to the people of Riverina to-
day. The G. Reid of to-day probably looks
back with some scorn on the primitive Saxon
who would have prevented the forming of a
solid English nation, and thrown the pros-
pective British empire overboard, in order
that the North Folk, of what is now Norfolk,
might avoid losing their separate identity by
being merged into the same race as
their brethren, the South Folk of Suffolk.
Yet G. Reid is a primitive East Angle him-
self. His yearning to preserve the separate
identity of the provinces, and to make quite
sure that the distinctions between the New
South Welshman and Victorian and South
Australian shall not be wholly merged into a
common Australian identity, will look just as
ridiculous 1000 years hence as the similar
howl of his parochial progenitor does .when
it is looked at 1000 years back.
The Bulletin’s idea of Federation is to
make an Australian nation—to obliterate
the Queenslander and New South Welshman
and Victorian and all the rest so completely
that their separate existence will be for-
gotten, and make Australians of them all.
It would like to sub-divide Australia afresh
into 40 or 50 convenient-sized States or local
government areas according to geographical
conditions, so that the old lines where the
Cornstalk’s prejudice ended and the Gum-
sucker’s bigotry or the Crow-eater’s paro-
chialism began would be forgotten, and
people would drop the old provincial idea
through their inability to remember which
provincial idea they inherited. It would like
to make Freetrade so absolute throughout
Australia that every district and town could
trade freely with its most convenient
port, instead of being hampered by a
provincial railway-system whose object is
to protect * ‘ our ” big city at a grievous
sacrifice of national interests. And, instead
of the dictum that it is the first duty of
every province to preserve its own territory,
this paper would like to substitute the axiom
that it is the first duty of every Australian to
preserve the territory and interests of Aus-
tralia, but that the interior divisions of the
continent are merely for convenience in the
matter of local government, ( and the exact
boundaries of the part called Victoria or New
South Wales or Mudgee or Prahran or Wes-
tralia or Kiama (where the ghost used to be)
or Croajingolong or Queensland or One Cow
Point have no patriotic or national signifi-
cance at all. No Englishman nowadays
wants to quarrel or fight with his fellow-
Englishman because the boundaries of Nor-
thumbria or Mercia or Wessex have been
interfered with. The old Seven Kingdoms
have served their turn and been dropped,
and it is difficult to find out now the exact
point where the Wessex man or the Mercian
used to have dark suspicions (just like
George Reid and George Turner glaring at
each other across their little border) that the
other fellow was trying to steal his trade or
wanted to embezzle water out of “ his ”
river. Considering the intense loyalty
which most of the New South Wales provin-
cialists have for England, they would do well
to follow England’s good example in this
matter. The constant policy of the much-
reverenced “mother-country’ has been to
make of its island one nation and one people.
Australia is, for all practical purposes,
smaller and more compact than England was
when it effected its memorable union ; there
are fewer days’ travel between Brisbane and
Perth now than there were between Carlisle
and Winchester in the days when the Seven
Kingdoms shut up, or between Middlesex
and Caithness in the days of Queen Anne.
Just for once The Bulletin has a burst of
loyalty, and it invites the disloyal L. F.
Heydon, and other bad revolutionary charac-
ters, to join it in following England’s
example.
* • • •
Therefore, it drops this small seed of
national Australian sentiment by the way-
side, to be burnt up in the glare of George
Reid’s eye-glass, and scorched by the hot
reflection of Turner's brown suit, and
trodden on by the large, flat feet of Dickson,
the auctioneer from Brisbane. Still, it
hopes that its descendants may come along
and find some sort of a tree growing where
the seed was dropped. In any case, it will
have a certain satisfaction in knowing that
it dropped the seed, and didn’t hoard it up
in its weskit-pocket, where it would have
done no good in any case.
"Our” Rivers.
One of the N.S.W. Government’s proposed
“ amendments ” of the Federal Constitution has
just received practical illustration in a rather
sudden fashion. The illustration arrived from
Goodooga, some kind of a place away in the dry
north of Cornstalk Land, in the shape of this
telegram, with large anguished headlines :
THE INTERCOLONIAL CONTROL OF RIVERS.
COMPLAINTS OF SQUATTERS.
Goodooga, Tuesday.
The river below Dirranbandi has again been stopped,
and the water turned down the Narran by a dam at Cur-
rawildi, erected by the Australian Pastoral Company, so
as to supply their Yerranbah station with water. All the
settlers along the Biree, Bokhara, and Ballandool, num-
bering some hundreds, have to forego their right to the
water, and thereby lose large numbers of stock, till one
station is served. The sufferers on the N.S.W. side of the
border have to put up with this sort of thing from
Queensland squatters, and appear to have no redress
for their grievances. Even if the dam was let go at once,
the Biree cannot now run through, and there is no hope
of the water reaching here. The Bokhara for miles is
perfectly dry, and the only water obtainable in Goodooga
is from the bore. Federation would, indeed, be a boon to
this district if it would stop Queenslanders doing as they
like with the main watercourses.
* « * *
When the river question was before the Fede-
ral Conference, it practically resolved itself into
a duel between N.S. Wales (representing the
purely provincial aspect of the question) and
South Australia (representing the Federal
aspect). The other provinces were neutral,
or nearly so, having but little personal
interest in the matter. S.A., of course, took the
Federal view of the case through necessity, for
its one river of any account is the lower
Murray, and if the provincial doctrine prevails,
and it is held that each province can absolutely
annex all the waters of any river passing
through its territory, then Victoria and N.S.
Wales—but principally the latter—can drain
the Murray for irrigation purposes, and leave
S.A. only a dry ditch. The case for the Crow-
eaters goes into these few propositions : The
rivers were provided by Nature to water Aus-
tralia—or as much of it as they can be made to
water ; not merely a political 'section of Austra-
lia. They were there thousands of years before
man came and drew his ridiculous little boun-
dary-streak on the map, and their usefulness
shouldn’t be limited by any such streak. The
rains which create them are produced by the
evaporation of all Australia and all its sur-
rounding seas. If there is to be any union, or
any brotherhood, or any Australian nation,
then the interests of all Australia must be con-
sidered equally. It is a thirsty land, and its
people are somewhat akin to an isolated little
community living on a scanty dole of water on
axaft, and according to all fairness the precious
fluid should be shared out equally—not the
whole lot grabbed by the largest individual in
the crowd. Therefore South Australia pleaded
that all rivers flowing through more than one
province should be joint property under Federal
control, and that the Federal authority should,
when the demands for irrigation purposes get
in advance of the supply, dole out the water to
the communities along the river bank, so that
everyone would receive the same treatment
irrespective of political boundaries.
• * • •
Certain of the New South Wales delegates,
on the other hand, savagely resented the idea
of joint ownership or Federal control. They
called the Darling and Murray “our” rivers,
and declared that every drop of water therein
was New South Wales property. The claim is
wildly ludicrous even as against South Aus-
tralia, for N.S.W. is wholly dependent on that
province as an escape for the occasional flood-
waters which would otherwise drown out its
western country ; therefore if the Murray is
solely a N.S.W. river, that province can be
called upon to shift “ its ” river out of S.A.
territory, and is, in all fairness, liable for any
damage “ its ” river does in flood-times before
it is shifted. It is still more ludicrous as
against Queensland and Victoria, inasmuch as
a great part of the Darling waters come down
from Queensland, and the bulk of the Upper
Murray from Victoria—a fact of which the
blatant Rkid and Want crowd was, and appa-
rently still is, wholly ignorant. The Federal
Convention made a compromise on the ques-
tion, and one reason why the Reid Government
and its press organs urged the country to reject
the Bill was the absence of any absolute pro-
vision that each province could do as it darned
well pleased with “its” own rivers. And
among the amendments to be insisted upon at
the new Conference is one put forward by
N.S. W. that each province can use up all the
water in its own rivers at its pleasure, and let
the province further down the river get on as
best it may.
* • • •
Curiously enough, the first province to suffer
by this doctrine is N.S. W. itself. All the vapor-
ing of Premier Reid to the effect that “ every
drop of water in the Darling belongs to N.S. W.”
didn’t alter in the least the geographical fact that
Queensland has a mortgage on some hundreds
of miles of the upper waters of that great but
unreliable stream. And as the N.S. W. Govern-
ment holds that each province can do exactly
what it pleases with all the rivers in its terri-
tory — well, Queensland simply has done what
it pleased. It has annexed for irrigation pur-
poses all the water of three little rivers flowing
into the Darling, and shut off the principal
water-supply of Goodooga, and left the stock of
a few hundreds of N.S. W. settlers to perish.
And the simple-minded folks of Goodooga want
Federation to come along quickly, so as to stop
this injustice, being blankly ignorant of the
fact that their own Government is fighting
tooth and nail for a system which will make
the injustice permanent. At present the
trouble is only beginning, for irrigation and
water-conservation are hardly started yet in
South Queensland. When they are fairly
developed, Queensland, in dry seasons, will
probably wholly divert the Culgoa, Biree, Bok-
hara, Ballandool, and a number of other small
streams, and five N.S. W. counties may practi-
cally retire from business—that is, provided
the Reid Government’s river policy is adopted.
• • * •
To all present appearances, it will be adopted
—unless South Australia refuses to federate on
such terms and stands out. Tasmania and
Westralia have no interest in the matter, as
none of their rivers (barring a few miles of the
Ord where it diverges from W.A. into the
Territory and back again) flow through any
territory except their own. Victoria, on the
whole, is willing. For about three-fourths of
the distance over which the Murray forms
the boundary line of Victoria and New South
Wales it is fed almost wholly by Victorian
streams, and an arrangement by which Victoria
will be made sole owner of these, to use and
divert them as it pleases, suits Victoria well
enough. Besides, that province has always
claimed to use the Murray itself for irrigation
purposes as much as it desires, and, if that
river isn’t put under Federal control, there is
no one to stop it doing so—unless N.S. W. likes
to start a war over the question. On the
whole, the provincial idea of river ownership is
likely to suit Victoria well enough : it is
almost certain to get as much that way as the
other. And it certainly suits Queensland,
which wants to annex, for its own sole
use, the upper waters of the Darling
in dry seasons, and turn a considerable
part of norihern N.S.W. into a desert in the
process. And N.S.YV., which originated the
idea, reckons that it is suited also. N.S.W.
wants authority to rob South Australia of its
share of the Murray waters in drought seasons,
though using that province for drainage pur-
poses in wet cnes ; and Victoria and Queensland
are quite willing to rob N.S.W. in their turn,
and nobody is likely to seriously complain
except S.A., which hasn’t a chance to rob any-
body. Whether N.S.W. will get more out of
the "deal than it loses is an open question.
Queensland's share of the business will be clear
profit in any case.
* * ♦ *
Anyhow, the beginning of the whole miser-
able, sordid row was the claim put forth by
Premier Reid and his little clan of provincial-
ists that every drop of water in the Darling
river system belonged to New South Wales,
and that New South Wales would allow no
interference with it whatsoever. And the fact
that Queensland has asserted its ownership of
a great part of the Upper Darling system
by turning an important section of it off at
the meter, and that it may turn a lot more
of it off when its water conservation works
are further advanced, is a very interesting


comment on these bumptious statements and
on the bumptious gang which made them. The
sayings and doings of that gang over Federal
matters have brought them into contempt
and, unluckily, have brought their province
into contempt also. Now the Reid Govern-
ment has either to compel Queensland to cease
blocking or diverting its northern rivers, or
else tacitly acknowledge that its alleged sole
ownership of the Darling river system was the
usual lie—one of the many malicious lies which
it put forward to delay or prevent Federation.
The melancholy little sun-bleached town of
Goodooga, which fries sadly like a lost soul up
on the gridiron of the N.JS.W. border-land, is
waiting for information.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Foreign-trade Crank.
The following letter, signed “ Percy Wake-
field,” and dated from Bondi, Sydney Daily
Telegraph hadn’t sense enough to suppress,
but gravely published in full:
ENGLAND’S FAITH IN FREETRADE.
TO THK EDITOR.
Sir.—ln confirmation of the unanswerable statements
made in your leading article to-day, under the abovo
heading, I can give you a remarkable instance of the
evils resulting from the Continental protectionist and
bounty-fed systems. During a recent visit to England, I
was staying with some friends at Bristol, and one even-
ing after tea noticed a peculiar white sediment at the
bottom of the teacup. Thinking this somewhat remark-
able, I asked the lady of the house if it was due to the
Bristol water-supply, or to the kettle. She replied :
“No ; I never noticed it before.” We then all began to
feel the bottoms of our teacups, and to rub the sediment
between the forefinger and thumb. It wns distinctly
gritty ! Pater familias, on being appealed to, said, “ Oh,
that’s nothing. Thousands or tons of Bath stone are
being shipped away continually to the continent to be
ground up and mixed with the bounty-fed beet sugar, the
surplus of which is being sent over to England, and sold
at ruinously low prices compared to the West Indian
sugar. If you want good sugar, you must pay for it.”
That is the only consolation we got, and the look on his
wife’s face was anything but sweet!
But perhaps our Protectionist friends prefer a little
pumpkin in their jams or swede turnips by way of a
change—as they do in Victoria—instead of the genuine
article, If sugar and all the products of Nature were free !
One of the moat remarkable features about
these little Freetrade yarns is that the name
and address of the authority is hardly ever
given—the unanswerable argument generally is
that somebody writes to allege that some per-
fectly anonymous person told him something
and didn’t adduce any evidence in support of
his statement. In this case quite a lot of
questions crop up. How was it that family,
which knew all about the adulteration of
foreign beet-sugar, had never noticed the strange
sediment before ? How many thousands of tons
of Bath-stone are shipped away from England
annually to adulterate the 3,500,000 tons of
sugar produced on the Continent of Europe?
What kind of lunatic is it who imports stone
all the way from England to adulterate sugar
with, instead of using the local article —think
of carrying rock by sea to Trieste or Ham-
burg, and then humping it by rail into the
interior of Austria or Germany to mix with the
sugar, when these countries produce every kind
of stone that any human being could want to
adulterate with ! Bath-stone is very ordinary
lime stone, and the same sort of stuff can be
found all over the Continent. Further, accord-
ing to official statistics, Britain exports very
little stone, and what it sends out is mostly mill-
stones and grindstones ; while it imports vast
quantities—perhaps to adulterate its sugar
with. Moreover, considering the penal rigidity
of the anti-adulteration laws so far as food is
concerned in France and Germany—the great
sugar-producing countries—how does the im-
ported limestone escape notice ? And, if it is
only Protection that leads to adulteration, what
is the cause of adulterated milk, tea, coffee, and
butter ; and why is Birmingham, in Freetrade
England, a name that has become synonymous
with bogus goods ? Is there any evidence that
there is more turnip in Victorian protected jam
than in the protected jam of N.S.W. ? Also,
how does Percy Wakefield know that the
sugar he was using on that memorable occasion
was the imported adulterated Protectionist
article, seeing that about half the sugar used in
Britain is refined in Britain, and (if it has Bath-
stone it it) is adulterated under the great, pure
Freetrade system in the country where Bath-
stone is handy ? The event happened at Bristol,
too, a great sugar-refining town, which probably
uses its own product to save freight. Of course,
the Freetrader may be satisfied to jump to the
conclusion that grit in the sugar must have got
there through Protection, but it isn’t evidence.
And why does the Freetrade man always yell
at Protection for making things dear, and yet
curse it for making sugar cheap? Also, by the
way, lime is necessary for refining sugar, and
even the Freetrade refiner uses it—only, when
he is seen buying it, he does it from the purest
motives, and, when the Protectionist refiner
buys it, that fact conclusively proves that Pro-
tection is a most disastrous policy. Anyhow,
will “Percy Wakefield” supply the name
and address of his informant, so that he can be
written to for further particulars ; and has he
a sample of the grit about him ?

The Misfit Man.
Up to now, Richard Burgess, the lonesome
tramp, arrested on general principles, is all the
Q. police have to show for their researches into
the hideous Gatton mystery, and evidently no
stone will be left unturned to hang that super-
fluous individual. In such a case as this, the
police have a whole flock of motives for hanging
somebody. For one thing, they want a rest,
and the only way to get that is to hang some-
body who looks, at least, reasonably probable.
Also, what is still more important, they want
to preserve their professional reputations, and
the only way to do that is to hang the right
man—or else to hang the wrong man, after
persuading themselves and the public that he
is the right man, which will do just us well.
The police started by trying to follow a track
from the crime to the man, and failed miserably
—partly because the Gatton bobby hadn’t
sense enough to prevent the crowd trampling
out every trace of the murderer or murderers.
Then they went out and found a man
who looked as if he hadn’t any friends, and
are trying to trace him back to the crime.
And their methods, so far as they can be in-
spected at a few hundred miles distance, seem
most rascally. Bekgkss was arrested and re-
manded on a charge of stealing a saddle. No
saddle was found in his possession; nothing
definite has been heard about anybody losing a
saddle ; and the police presently dropped that
accusation altogether, so apparently it was a
mere invention all along. Still, it served to keep
Burgess in gaol while the police were trying to
make him fit the big crime. Then they got
him two months for vagrancy —having just been
discharged from gaol without any money, he
was more or less a swagman from sheer neces-
sity ; and this gives them more time to fit him
into the crime. They wanted him identified as
the man who was at the slip-rails on the night
of the murder, so, instead of having him in-
spected immediately after arrest, they spread
his photo., full face and profile, all over the
newspapers, till everybody in South Queens-
land (to say nothing of the rest of Australasia)
could recognise him at first glance. Then
they got the witnesses to look at him, and
they all at once remembered him. They had
caught a moment’s glimpse of a strange man in
the dark, and didn’t know him from Adam—
but for weeks they had had Burgess’s photo,
before them, and had been unconsciously fitting
his face and head on to the shadow that flitted
across the road that night, so the recognition
was inevitable. Also, one man who saw
Burgess by daylight, and talked to him at
some length, recognised him also—but he swears
that he met Burgess on the morning before the
crime fifty miles away from the place where it
was committed. This witness, apparently the
most reliable one of the crowd, seems to be
kept very much in the background. In
reality, Burgess doesn’t seem to fit the
crime at all. He was a stranger, and had,
apparently, no time to get an accomplice, or
to get to know the Murphys, or to accumu-
late an animosity against them. The Mur-
phys, apparently, didn’t know him, and they
weren’t likely to let themselves be beguiled
into the bush by a perfect stranger. It isn’t
proved so far that ho had any revolver to
shoot them with, or any money to buy a revol-
ver. He was stone-broke, yet the three de-
ceased weren’t robbed. He isn’t covered with
bruises and scratches, though the murderer
seemingly had a desperate struggle with two
strong, healthy girls. It was hardly possible
for such a crime to be done by a single
person—yet Burgess appears to have been
a one-man tramp all through his wan-
derings. And even if he was the man
at the slip-rails that man hasn’t been con-
nected with the murder up to date—and the
ridiculous method of “identification ” makes it
impossible to place any reliance on the asser-
tion that he was the man. Of course when a
man with no money or friends is in gaol, so that
he can’t hunt up evidence to prove that he
wasn’t there, and there is no Crown Defence
Department to do it for him, and half the
police-force of Queensland has either cot to
prove that he was there or admit itself a failure,
and nobody really objects to his being hanged,
he has a very fair chance of being made to fit
the crime somehow. But Burgess certaiidy
looks a terribly bad fit. Perhaps the police
know something more about Burgess than
appears on the surface of things. If they don’t,
then Burgess is a cruelly-persecuted man and
entitled, should there be no further evidence
against him, to handsome compensation at the
hands of the Btate. If the police have deter-
mined to keep him in custody on mere suspicion
they should be honest enough to say so. His
committal for two months for vagrancy is a
shocking perversion of justice.
The “ Natural~Ally.”
Aeter all the eloquent assertion that Queens-
land is the “natural ally” of N.S. YV. in
federal matters, and that N.S. VV. should on no
account federate without its “ally,” the re-
marks of Premier Dickson at the Federal
Council were damp and cold. Oily James
didn’t hold out the right hand of friendship in
any enthusiastic spirit. He was a very cautious
auctioneer, and his language wasn’t so much
that of a cordial friend as of a severe man who
had called for the rent. Queensland, he says,
will demand that a formal Convention be held
to consider N.S.W.’s proposed amendments if
N.S.YV. insists on submitting them—not a mere
conference of Premiers. He objects to the
proposed constitution of the Federal Senate,
and considers that the Federal referendum
should be left out, and he remarks that a lot of
the Draft Constitution wants revision. Also,
Dickson holds that the Federal Government
should take over the State debts, which is
quite outside the N.S.W. programme; and he
wants the financial scheme revised with a
special view to the requirements of Queensland
—the province that needs a heavier revenue
tariff to keep it going than either Victoria,
S.A., or Tasmania. And in all Dickson’s
oration there wasn’t one comforting word about
fixing the capital on N.S.YV. territory, though
that idea was supported by sundry Tas. and
Vic. politicians. Queensland apparently doesn’t
feel at all like N.S. YV.’s natural ally, though
the latter province insists on regarding it in
that light, and intends playing a lone hand in
the new Federal movement. But the Northern
province has never been wildly affable to its
neighbor—in fact, the “ natural ally ” theory
has been a mystery all along. Queensland
maintains its own isolated mail service, unlike
N.S.YV., Victoria, and S.A., which run a
friendly partnership. It has hit N.S.YV. badly
with its coal duty, Tasmania being the
only other province which levies a duty
on N.S.YY T . coal. It is the only province
which charges an export duty on wool to
prevent it reaching the N.S.YV. railways or the
N.S. YY T . ports, and, having the heaviest tariff
in Australia, it has no special sympathy with
the one allegedly Freetrade province. Also, its
Tory Government and its nigger policy keep it
out of sympathy with its more democratic
neighbors ; and while N.S. YY T . regards it as a
humble friend and small brother, it regards
itself as a province of such vast possibilities
that it w'on’t be a small brother to anybody
under the sun. For such a mild, bald old man,
Premier Dickson appears to have remarkable
possibilities as a disturbing element. There
are even dark suspicions that he will insist on
having the Federal capital himself.
The Teacher’s Soul.
“ T.Y.K.” makes severe gashes in a very
tough question :
Ed. Bulletin.— The little wife of a very Iff" .State-
school teacher friend of mine attended a certain snUirlian
R.C. Church last Sunday, as usual. A vis mu lu iest.
pitched into the public schools of N..5.W., called them
jrodless places, sinks of iniquity, hotbeds of in natality,
and declared that absolution could not and would not be
given to those degraded R.C. parents who sent their
children to the hated State-schools. This was the last
straw. The pale little lady rose from her pew near the
altar ; “ Come along, children ! ” she said to her son and
her daughter, “ this is certainly no fit place for us.” Then
she walked calmly out, heedless of gapers and starers.
The priest got a bit of a jerk, but soon resumed his
vituperative career.
Now, I want, to have a word over this vital matter.
Priests have repeatedly denied to me that absolution is
withheld from R.C. parents who patronise public schools
—have laughed the matter off as absurd. Nevertheless,
I know fathers who cannot get absolution because
they will persist in sending their children to public
schools. Does the R.C. Church expect the numerous
R.C. teachers under the Public Instruction Department
to send their own children to R.C. schools on pain of
being denied absolution and Holy Communion, the
truest solace a devout Catholic can have? 1 consider
that R.C. public school teachers who send their own
children to R.C. schools are disloyal to the Department
they serve. Apart from this, however, the R.C. Church’s
position in this matter is most illogical. Surely, if it is
an evil for an R.C. child to be taught in a public school,
it must be an immeasurably greater evil for an R.C.
parent to teach in one.
If the Church were honest enough and game enough to
be logical instead of being wedded to expediency it
would henceforth forbid, under pain of excommunication,
any R.C. person to enter such an allegedly immoral ser-
vice. Of course such a step would be er inexpedient,
because in many country townships tho R.C. public
school teacher is the only decently-educated male member
of the congregation, lie is invaluable for running concerts,
getting up subscription-lists and organising bazaars,
besides being always good for a guinea when the Christ-
mas offerings and the Easter dues come along.
Only one priest, out of the many whom I tackled, ever
attempted to argue the matter out. He said that tho
Church did quite right in forbidding R.C. boys and girls
to attend public schools, while permitting—nay, even
encouraging—them to run the risk of losing their immor-
tal souls by entering the service of the P.I. Department
as pupil-teachers—because the presence of so many good,
devout Catholics was the leaven that prevented the whole
system from becoming totally depraved. But why not
push the argument further and encourage pious but
brainy Catholics to leaven the wicked professions of the
bookie, the bilker, the bludger, the burglar, the spieler,
etc. ?—especially etc. ?
I am a Roman Catholic, but fortunately I am not. a
public school teacher. If I were, I don’t know that I’d
nave grit enough to act like the little woman who dis-
dained to sit meekly in church while a priest was doing
his best to make her children ashamed of their fat her and
his profession by publicly stating that that father was a
frequenter of an immoral place.
If the immorality of the public schools of this province
is so pronounced, all conscientious R.. 0. public school
teachers should resign immediately - and damn the inex-
pediency !
The Fly-Speck’s Bobby.
Centralisation of police—badly wanted for
many years—has at last arrived in the Land of
Sleep-a-Lot. Hitherto, in many parts of Tas-
mania, the municipalities controlled the police-
force ; and, the average municipality not being
much larger than a fair-sized paddock, the
spectacle of a constable endeavoring to ride-
down a suspect was like a fat man chasing a
rabbit on a cart-horse. The alleged criminal
simply got through the boundary-fence and
walked into the next district, leaving the dis-
tressed peeler sitting on his horse ; and as it
took some time to pass the warrant on to the
officer in charge of the adjoining paddock, the
aforesaid a.c. often walked through several dis-
tricts undetected, and sometimes clean out of
the island. Mostly, however, he stayed just
where he was, which was by far the safest
game considering the general tactics of the
municipal police-force. Some of the most sen-
sational crimes perpetrated in Tasmania of late
years happened within municipal control, and
out of half-a-dozen one only was sheeted home.
One has hub to mention to Tasmanians the
triple Fullerton murder to bring the glaring
stupidity of municipal constables to t heir recol-
lection. The slow arrival of centralisation was
chiefly caused by the love of a little brief
authority shown by the municipal wardens and
councillors, and their dislike lo relinquish it.
To be saluted by the township hobby was as
good as a dinner to their washups of the coun-
try bteneh, and to be suddenly dropped by the
Guv’ment peeler is a nasty powder that will
stick to their tongues for some time to come.

Wanted—A Tasmanian Labor-party.
The blockaded Austrians—allowed to land in
Maoriland on the shipping co. giving security for
their maintenance—were invited to proceed to
Tas., where work was offered them as navvies
at 7s. per day. The VV. Coast miners and Hobart
workers protested, with the result that Premier
Braddon cabled the Austrian consul at Auck-
land, warning him that there were already
hundreds of unemployed in Tasmania, and that
shipping co.’s landing aliens would have to
enter into a bond of .£lOO per head that none
of their passengers were likely to become a
burden on the State. Tas. toilers are not
satisfied with the Premier’s action, charac-
terising it as inefficient. The weary Premier
says there is no law or regulation that will allow
him to move “ any forrarder ” in the matter,
and he really doesn’t know what to do next.
Now, if the Austrians go to Tasmania they will
go under contract, and their importers are not
likely to allow them to become a burden on the
State charities ; therefore the shipping co’s are
not alarmed at the stiffness of the proposed
.£lOO bond. Though the Austrians are certain
to become a burden on the labor of the State
by reducing wages to the sauer-kraut and garlic
level, that is a matter which, in the absence of
a Labor-party in Tas. Parliament, the Premier
is not likely to take into consideration. And,
after all, what better can be expected of a pro-
vince that, in the matter of Chinese, persists in
remaining the dirty back-door of Australia?

The Universal Clontarf Bill.
There is a growing tendency to regard the
“ push ”as a purely Australian endemic. Yet,
whether the cause of the evil be the spread of
education, the lessening of the hours of labor,
the absence of municipal recreation rooms, the
vacuousness of the average family circle when
sentenced to “solitary ” with itself, the filtra-
tion “ downwards ” of che habits of University
students, the decrease of reverence, the accessi-
bility of beer, the “comitatus” of the football-
club, or the amateur J.P., no one who reads
the British and American papers of last year
could have failed to notice that the evil was
practically universal. The N.Y. papers
have been full of correspondence on the
subject. The good old “ flogging ” argu-
ment with the usual anachronism about
the suppression of garrotting in England was
trotted out, and in some parts of America there
was even talk of “ Vigilantes ” clubs to cope
with the trouble. In London the same: lead-
ing article after leading article appeared on
Hooliganism, as (another injustice to Ireland !)
pushism has, during the last year or two, been
called there, and ail sorts of remedies, mostly
brutal, some of a well meaning, socialistic
sta'mp, were proposed. Manchester suffered
from an acute form, which had its special
name. Belfast suffered severely, especially
during the football season, but there, no doubt,
the fact of its having been the year ’9B had
something to do with the traces of color notice-
able in most of the local pushes. The facts
available would seem to indicate that as no
merely local causes engender pushes, more
radical remedies than any yet proposed are
necessary for their extinction.

Dear Bvi.listin. — Having recently given police-court
evidence about a serious Sydney street row I happened to
witness, I was approached by one of the arresting con-

address I’ll sec that parts up with a quid should
tile lines he paid.” Now, considering the policeman’s
matter-of-fact assumption that 1 had, upon oath, given a
bogus name and address, isn’t it time to ask how often
are convictions obtained by means of such “ tips?" The
only reason 1 had for giving evidence at all was that half-
a-dozen persons who 1 know witnessed the affair posit ively
disclaimed all knowledge thereof, and 1 saw that, if I
didn’t speak, the cowardly rulliuns would escupo punish-
ment.

Ed. Bulletin. —You voice the disgust you feel
that sucli a charge us oonspiracy-to-murdor
should bo publicly made against a member of
N.S. W. Parliament, and that there should
follow the accusation no demand by an out-
raged public for rigid investigation. “If
N.S. Wales,” you remark, “ does not wake up
to the gravity of I his sort of thing she will find
her name a byword among even the South
American States,” &c. But had you, dear
Bulletin, looked deeper into the matter and
considered facts which are within the know-
ledge of everyone, you would have seen that
the public lethargy in this connection is not
duo so much to lack of abhorrence of the crime
attributed to the politician referred to, hut to a
lack of confidence in the motives of those who
have permitted their names to be associated
with the accusation. From which political
clique have all the sensational, baseless and
unproved accusations that have disfigured N.S.
Wales history for the last ten years emanated?
Please answer and then tell me if you can
honestly blame the public for refraining from
going into spasms of denunciatory eloquence?
The public, I fancy, mutters to itself “ A
plague on both your houses ! ” and then sensibly
holds its collective jaw. I have no regard for the
accused—absolutely none—but candour com-
pels me to say that the aspect of the affair that
stings me most as a citizen of N.S. W. is that
there should be amongst our Parliamentary
representatives persons whose deliberately-
made public accusations of an atrocious crime
should be received with an indifference so
marked as to form an indictment against the
accusers. The accused’s position is not an envi-
able one, but what is t he posit ionof his accusers
in face of the ominous silence of the people of
N.S. W. ?—T.P.

According to the scanty information supplied,
the Federal Conference of Premiers started with
Reid’s 1700th repetition of that old, thread-
bare, time-yellowed wheeze with which every-
body is so familiar:
The amendments must, lie of a substantial nature, or he
felt certain that the N.S. W. people would not be ready to
accept the bill. The sacrifices which the mother colony
was willing to make in the cause of Federal union were far
greater than those of the sister colonies. That being so,
he held that some consideration should be given to the
claims which N.S.VV. made for a revision of the Common-
wealth Bill, on the lines suggested in the resolutions.
That daily moan about the alleged sacrifices of
N.S.W. has been repeated very, often now, and
yet not a soul has ever attempted to seriously
define these “sacrifices.” It is simply stated
that the “ sacrifices ” are so self-evident that
anyone can see them, and the subject is left
there. Here is the situation :
(1.) N.S.W. proposes to keep its lands and railways—
like the other provinces.
(2.) It takes no responsibility for the debt of any other
province.
(3.) It will pay exactly the same taxes as the rest—not
a copper more or less. Having more people than the
others, it will probably yield more Federal revenue, and
it will also get more Federal expenditure and more of the
Federal surplus, to make things square.
(4.) It proposes to keep its own rivers under its own
exclusive control, and wants authority to cut off S.A.’s
water supply also.
(5.) N.S.W., Westralia, and perhaps Queensland, may
be said to represent the revenue-tariff element, Victoria
and the other provinces the Protectionist element in Aus-
tralia. They all put their fiscal systems in the melting-
pot together, and take the same chances.
(6) N.S.W. can do with a lower revenue tariff than any
of its neighbors, and the Reid Govt, demands that the
Federal tariff shall be fixed to suit its requirements, and
that the other provinces shall take their chance of bank-
ruptcy. The financial scheme of the Draft Constitution
left the tariff question absolutely open. The provinces
(N.S.W., Victoria and S.A.), whose customs revenue is
below the Australian average took the risk of having to
raise more revenue than they actually wanted ; Tas-
mania, AVestralia, and Queensland (if it came in) had to
face the hazard of not having enough revenue. The
Reid Govt, declares it won’t stand an arrangement
whereby it shares the risks with the other states.
(7) N.S.W. demands possession of the Federal capital
in return for the concessions it doesn’t make.
. (8) The one concession that is made the equal repre-
sentation in the Senate—is given almost equally by
N.S.W. and Victoria to the small t provinces, it isn’t »
special concession made by N.S.W.
Where are these wearisome “sacrifices”’
Despite official re-assurances c,ic .poly of
water at Pera (N.S. W.) artesian ■ v .tlement,
near Bourke, doesn't improve, ; nd toe settlers
have been unable to get enough to iCcep their

feature of the business is that, thougu they have
to get a living from their blocks ana the Govt
hasn’t, the State experimental farm anti orchard
