“ Forty Thieves ” ends its career at Her
Majesty’s next Wednesday. It hasn’t been a
strikingly successful pantomime, despite its glit-
tersome costumes, and the armoured Amazons,
and the pomp and splendour of Bagdad, and the
gaudy fooling of Gulliver and the Ass. The audi-
ences have thinned off latterly in a way to which
the Firm’s pantomimes are not accustomed. The
book is largely responsible for this ; that frail,
little streak of literature was never strong enough
to stand the burden laid upon it, and a great mis-
take was made in trying to centre one of the
biggest jokes round Hooley when that blighted
bubble was so dead and gone and extinct
that he might as well have tried to
pass a bad cheque on the megatherium.
In these last days Hooley drifts more and more
into the background, and the Ass—which has
taken to drink, by the way, in addition to its
other iniquities—carries an increased share of the
burden to make things level. Also Albert Whelan
lets himself go in a series of imitations of popular
actors, which are even better than those he did of
yore. Whelan, as a mimic, has no rival in Aus-
tralia, and the pity is Australia isn’t big enough to
give him a fair show.
• • • •
The “Geisha,” after doing a highly suc-
cessful season in Melbourne, is due at Her
Majesty’s on Thursday, February 9.
** * *
Old “ Formosa ” has done something like
consistently crowded business at Sydney Lyceum
for two weeks—an unusual thing at the Lyceum,
where a thin sprinkling of people so often smatter
over the big house till it looks like a large wilder-
ness flecked over with an Israelite here and there.
But “Formosa” has really gone with a rush. It
is well-mounted —barring a remarkable bridge in
the last act, which stops short in the middle of the
river, as though the materials had given out—and,
so far as the leading parts are concerned, the co.
does its business well. The Jenny Bolter of Miss
Helene Burdett has undoubtedly a great deal to
do with the success. That very new young actress
has improved greatly in the last few months, and
her Potteresque head, surmounted by the Potter-
esque hair, shows signs of having a promising
brain inside. Also, she dresses Jenny Boker as
that gorgeous Queen of the Flossies should
be dressed, which counts for a great deal. It is
absolutely no use for the enthralled Johnny to
kneel before his siren and tell her that her
loveliness has driven him off his globular little
head if the lady is attired in an ill-fitting grey
bag which looks as if it had been blown on to her
with dynamite. Thus it happens that the dowdi-
ness of one at least of the two good girls, who
seems to have rushed at her clothes in the dark,
spoils her little illusion completely. And in the
same respect Diver and Bert Bailey stand out
in conspicuous contrast with two of their
male accomplices—especially the small accom-
Elice whose coat looks too short and his
elltopper too big. As for Fred Patey,
he is attired chiefly in a hairy cap and a gruff
voice and a dog, and looks his part well enough ;
and Harcourt needs little in his character as old
Boker save a pair of large boots and an honest
heart; but among the presumably well-dressed
part of the show the small gentleman with the big
sad hat is a kind of a shock. Fortunately, he is a
person of little consequence. Jenny Boker will
be a vision of loveliness in her gilded halls of sin
for only a few nights more, and, all things con-
sidered, the writer will be rather sorry when she
makes her exit. “ Flying Scud ” will follow.
** * *
The Sleeping of London at Sydney
Royal comes to an end next week. On Friday
night that city will be wakened up and informed
by its landlady that the bed is wanted for the next
boarder, and the “English Rose ” will take posses-
sion on Saturday (February 11). The old piece
has fairly established Charles Holloway’s reputa-
tion in Sydney as a city manager, and as it has
boomed consistently all through it should have
also added much wealth to nis bank account.
Sydney never took C. Holloway very seriously
before. He was regarded as a small migratory
individual, not so much himself as Plain Bill’s
brother, and city people didn’t really expect to
see him —only to learn occasionally, in an obscure
corner of the paper, that he was bogged some-
where in a wet season while trying to take a small
company through toßoggabri or Boppy Mountain.
And when C. Holloway suddenly rose and demon-
strated that he was a front-rank manager, with a
big, capable company, and put through a large
drama in excellent style, and filled the Royal for
five weeks without a change of bill, he created a
surprise. If the performance isn’t a mere Hash in
the pan, and can be kept up for a few months
longer, the new lessee of Sydney Royal will never
be Billolloway’s brother again. The difference
will be that Bill will be C. Holloway’s brother.
Anybody who has ever been anybody else’s brother
will understand the difference.
** * *
Lieutenant Frank Travis, a ventriloquist
of groat weight, figuratively speaking, will appear
at Sydney Tivoli on Saturday. Report has it that
Travis is the most wonderful ventriloquist in the
world, and can obliterate a receipt-stamp with his
voice at forty yards range; and The Bulletin
Bees no reason why these statements should not be
strictly true. The Tivoli, for some weeks past,
hasn’t had anybody on the programme who was
the most wonderful something or anything in the
world, so the new man will be all the more wel-
come. A very pleasant item on the present pro-
gramme is the ‘ ‘ Terrible Storm, ” furnished by
the Sisters Phillips and-Brothers Lorenzi—strange
how the variety stage is crowded with youthful
“sisters” and “brothers,” yet no couple ever
think of announcing themselves as the Aged
Parents Smith, by way of a change; and Miss
Peggy Pryde keeps finding new songs in the
depths of her inexhaustible basket.

Two Sydney theatres closed up last week»
and thereby considerably relieved the pressure of
competition. Rufus Dawes left off at the Cri-
terion after being flogged to moderately good busi-
ness for some weeks, and the Ori. is deserted.
Also the J übilee Singers have started on a sub-
urban tour prior to their departure for Maoriland,
and the Palace is again peacefully eating its own,
or Mr. “Tattersall” Adams’s, head off in Pitt-
street. Probably, however, it will again be occu-
pied before long. The luck of Dante and the
Jubilees has removed the impression that the
Palace was synonymous with misfortune.
f B it B
“One of the Best,” a drumful military
drama in seven tableaux and ever so many kilts,
made an imposing reappearance at Melb. Royal
on Saturday. Three years ago this was an
original work of fiction, but since then Blandolt
has founded it on the Dreyfus case, to the glory of
Zola and the confusion of Major Esterhazy. In
fact, The Bulletin begins to believe that Bland
Holt wrote “ One of the Best ” under instructions
from Victor Napoleon. Anyway, he has brought
it up to date with a flourish of trumpets, and Mel-
bourne evidently intends to take a serious view of
the proceedings for twelve nights, or even longer.
“ One of the Best ” is a well-built melodrama for
the most part. The hero is a reasonable young
soldier, who earned the Victoria Cross for valor
before the curtain rose, but who afterwards
does nothing in particular beyond getting accused
of another man’s treachery to Hinglanu. Also,
the actual purloiner of the papers from General
Charley Brown’s safe is a well-balanced skunk,
and the general’s daughter, who plans the burglary
for the skunk aforesaid, and ultimately gives him
away to papa because he refuses to marry her,
strikes one as being a life-like exponent of hysteri-
cal sin. Even the cheerful cockney humor of
Bland Holt’s comic relief has a reasonable side to
it, whilst Mrs. Bland H. gets better chances than
have fallen to her lot on several previous occasions.
“ One of the Best ” is the drama where Mrs. H.,
cutting a jolly little figure in a knickerbocker suit,
produces a letter for Walter Baker from her
ta-ra-ra pocket. Perhaps it isn’t usual for a
lady’s-maid to carry billets-doux on a bike; never-
theless, the natty knicker suit is full of human
interest, and the ta-ra-ra pocket scores an over-
whelming success.
• • * •
Some excellent scene-painting, a big brass
band, and a Scottish regiment which carries many
legs manfully, make this an interesting spectacle.
As a serious [>lay it is well acted by Miss Watson,
Walter Baker, Arthur Styan and Miss Ross. In
a less degree Albert Norman and Charles Brown
are satisfactory representatives of Hingland’s
glory, and sundry six-line parts are neatly tilled.
The only conspicuous fault of the first night should
be easily remedied. Walter Baker’s degradation
was fully three minutes too long in coming to a
climax, but when the scene is curtailed in accord-
ance with the military fitness of things it will be
quite as distressing as on Saturday, and much more
enjoyable. A modern audience likes them to
hurry up with the grief and woe.
* * * #
The Broughs closed their Bijou season
this week with four performances of “Dandy
Dick,” the revived Georgiana Tidman and red-
headed Noah Topping proving to the satisfaction
of all concerned that they are somewhat more
effective than ever in their good old parts. By
the way, Mrs. Brough is lucky in having three
good characters —Madame Sans-Gene, Niobe, and
the Weary Fragment Tidman—which will be
better suited to her age ten years hence. Reg.
Dartrey, as the Dean who dabbles in horse-
doctoring and gets arrested for his secret error,
gave a fairly successful sketch of a commonplace,
smug parson. Mr. H. Rotherham was a service-
able butler, and Miss Bessie Thompson made a
capital wife for the awful Noah. Other bright
points in the show were Mario Majeroni,
MTntyre, and Percy Brough’s make-up as a
prematurely bald majah with a wrecked liver and
a languid disposition to pack up a few charcoal
biscuits and toddle when duty calls.

The new Brough season at the Princess’s
is to open on Saturday, and Anthony Hope’s much
eulogised comedy, “ The Adventure of Lady
Ursula” is the bill fixed for the occasion. Un-
doubtedly the Brough co. has been gaining
popularity of late at the Bijou, and the interesting
question as to whether it will flourish at the
Firm’s house is likely to be answered in the
affirmative.

Rickards closes the Opera House on Fri-
day, leaving that rather decrepit structure in the
hands of the rebuilder and architect. On Saturday
he will start “doing his utmost to deserve a con-
timiance of the public’s kind patronage,” &c., &c.,
at the Bijou aforesaid. A pantomimic quartette
party—Sisters Phillips and Brothers Lorenzi —will
figure in the centre of the inaugural programme,
and considering that manager Rickards nas well
and truly striven to build up a lucrative past The
Bulletin herewith commends his Bijou future to
the care of his grateful patrons, and Providence in
general. Especially Providence. The Bulletin
doesn’t put much faith in public constancy.

To-night (Thurday) is the last but one of
“The Geisha,-” at the Princess’s, and the manage-
ment, out of its great love for womankind, has
arranged to present everv lady visitor with a pic-
torial souvenir. Also, the yearning for souvenirs
will be so great that the distribution will almost
inevitably be repeated on Friday. Meanwhile,
anyone anxious to get a souvenir without going to
the theatre can obtain the coveted rapture on pay-
ment of two-and-sixpence. This is a most thought-
ful offer.
** * *
London Referee publishes a letter bear-
ing on the matter of first-night demonstrations
and counter-demonstrations. The writer says:
“A good deal of what has been wrongly called
organised opposition would be done away with if
managers of certain West End theatres would
consent to keep their ‘ paper ’ out of the unreserved
parts of their houses on a first night. Thero is
nothing more annoying to your average first-
nighter than to find a number of people frantically
applauding everything, whether it possesses merit
or not.” Which reminds The Bulletin that faint
mutterings of “ counter - demonstration ” have
lately been heard at one or two Australian theatres
where the management inclines to the “bluff”
system of first-night applause. It was all right as
long as the public in general belioved the indis-
crimmating enthusiasts to be genuine fools, but,
now that it has detected a method in the madness
of these noisy nuisances, the public begins to
growl ominously.

Wilson Barrett returns to Australia in the
middle of the year to further enlighten our
dramatic darkness. Since his return ’Oino, Wilson
B. has posed throughout the country as a mission-
ary fresh from the heathen. According to news,
pars., he found Australia in a condition of barbar-
ism so far as Art (Barrett’s Art) was concerned,
and left it educated, elevated, and worshipping
the one true god, Mr. Barrett.
PROLOGUE TO UNWRITTEN DRAMA: “THE
CRUSHED TRAGEDIAN."
[This prologue to be spolcen (or ranted) by Wilson Barrack,
Esq., on his return to Sydney after his controversy
with No-gent Itobertson.]
Wilson Barrack, Esq., advances with stilted strides to
centre of stage, halts and strikes an attitude like a
footrunner about to start for a race, and then pro-
ceeds :
Once more I stand on Sydney’s fatal shore—
Alas ! how changed from what I was before t
I came, I saw, I ranted ! pawed the air !
Tore passions into tatters everywhere.
Talked as no human being ever talked,
Walked as no mortal creature ever walked.
For this 1 earned my due reward, God wot—
Some feeble maunderings of Lewis Scott
In columns of D.T. Quick did I note
A chance to advertise ; and so I wrote
A line or two in praise of that dull elf,
With half a column praising up myself!
Then Scott wrote back and praised himself (and me),
And all things were as merry as could be
Until there came, with unexpected jar,
Some cursfcd outcast, signing “ A.N.R.”
Great God 1 It makes me green when I recite
The frightful things the ruffian dared to write.
My Wilfred Denver weak and ineffective !
My Hamlet played like Hawkshaw the Detective 1
M.v finest speeches stigmatised as platitude 1
Great Heaven, I cried, I’ll strike—and struck an attitude '.
All hell shall stir for this 1 Let folk be warned,
Hell hath no fury like a mummer scorned !
I called my minions, Hodges and his crew,
And Carter (mind the hyphen) Edwards too.
And Lewis Scott came forward with the rest,
A volunteer— Thank God we did our best.
We dubbed him drunkard 1 Called him, in our rage,
Conceited as a monkey in a cage !
Though never monkey, caged or free, could beat
Our boundless, baseless, infinite conceit.
With personal abuse and feeble witticism
We tried to meet his nasty, carping criticism.
We cursed him soundly for his interference,
We cursed his manners, morals, and appearanoe;
But that the editor had had his fill
And stopped us we’d be cursing of him still 1
But now the play awaits me and I go
Once more to murder Shakespeare 1 Soft and slow
That music there ! The Limelight man, What Ho !
If any critic dare to show his head
May Jove send thunderbolts to strike him dead.
Or, if Jove fail me in that consummation
ril stun him with my English Reputation.
So let us to the play ! Kind friends adieu !
Luck, and the Limelight man, will pull me through !
At Poverty Point.
Ada Reeve, who is supporting Arthur Roberts in a new
musical farce, gets a burst of enthusiasm from critic
Carados, of the London Rekeree “ Miss Ada Reeve,
who plays the part of a French singer, is a d-licious
morsel. ' When she comes on she brightens up the stage
just as much as if they had turned up all the lights.
Her acting and her singing and her dancing have a
piquancy, and a little of that sort of thing goes further
than a bushel of ‘ chaff.’ Her acting has the quality of
genuine sensibility; you can see that when she sings a
coon song, by the way in which her movements correspond
alvvavs with the expression of her face. What an expres-
sive face it is, too, with her pretty little teeth, and her
laughing eyes, and her saucy nose perched there like a
little bird!”
Max Beerbohm, dramatic critic of Saturday Review
(actor Beerbohm Tree's brother), says that Ada Reeye,
with a good part, “ would make the fortune of a legiti-
mate farce.”
Charles Kenningham, undoubtedly the most successful
of latter-day musical importations, has been “ resting ”
on full salary since the start of “The Geisha,” in which
there is no tpnor part worth mentioning. Looks as
though the Firm had great hopes of “La Poupee when
Kenningham was engaged, and didn’t think of producing
the other opera yet awhile. As matters stanil, the un-
employed tenor represents a discount of £25 a week off
“ The Geisha ” profits.
Tenor Kenningham possesses the rare faculty of being
able to distinguish musical pitch. Mozart, was the first
known instance of this. The possess' r, on » chord being
struck in an adjoining room, instantly names the key,
and this can be done also with a single note, which can
be named. Kenningham is also a fine pianist and organist.
The reformatory ship Sobraon is proving very valuable
to N.S.W. bands in search of recruits. Some four or five
members of the Permanent Artillery Band are ex-Sohraon
boys, and Bandmaster Hutchinson now asks for more
reinforcements from the same source.
The waxworks people are, no doubt, already faking
effigies of the Gatlon Murphies and the Melbourne ’ead.
The catalogue of one Australian show is practically all
“ Chamber of Horrors.” Butler, Deeming, Dean,
and the Makins have the boldest type in the book,
and much space is also devoted to ungrammatical
accounts of the bushrangers and their exploits. Glad-
stone, Bismarck, Parnell V— well, they are essential evils
who have to he sandwiched, somehow, between the
baby-farmers and Captain Moonlight.
Melb. Opera House, now about to be demolished, holds
the Australian record for a successful show. The first
production of “Tambour Major” ran 101 nights at the
Opera House.
Thk Bulletin's London correspondent recently took a
rather pathetic view of Lottie Collins' attempted suicide.
Another correspondent—the cynical and gruesome one-
writes the story thus “ Lottie Collins, the original
* Ta-ra-ra ’ girl, made sundry half-hearted attempts to cut
her throat, the other week. She was taken to a London
hospital by ‘ two policemen and a man who described
himself as a relation,’ from which it may be supposed
that Lottie is now a ‘back-number’ who didn’t lay up
treasures on earth in the boom-de-heyday of her success.
It is getting to he the rule for faded celebrities to seek the
bubble advertisement even at the blade of a pocket-knife.
A very short time ago half London would have helped to
carry Lottie to the hospital or the police-station or some-
where, if her shapely throat was even scratched."
They have struck the bedrock of cheapness at Victoria
Hall, in Melbourne, where free are now
advertised—“ dancing, singing, and boxing.” “Free"
means that tickets for admission are given away with a
pint of beer.
Rip Vun Winkle Jefferson, who visited Australia in the
early sixties, and will be 70 on Feb. 20, has retired from
the stage. His father, grandfather, and great grand-
father held distinguished positions behind the footlights.
"T.D.” : I presume that, the authors of the Napoleonio
yarn now running through Sydney D.T. intend to drama-
tise their effort. It would, of course, need muoh working
out in the explanatory part, at present adroitly
“ slummed ” by Paolo's inordinately lengthy letter to his
dear ma—which would cost something like two guineas
if sent through the N.S.W. post at ordinary rates. If two
men are discoverable who are able to make up alike, and
behave with a fair degree of similarity to Emperor Nap.
and to each other, the “double ” idea should do well on
the stage—always provided that playgoers have not grown
too tired of the First Empire.
“Viator”: I never heard of a city where theatrical
managers are all so friendly as in Melb. Nor am I sure it
is the best thing for the public.
Wybert Reeve, the veteran lessee of Adelaide Royal, has
a fine collection of Thespian autographs and letters and
old-time playbills. One much-prized epistle is from
Walter Montgomery, offering to take him into partner-
ship, in London. Amongst others are original letters
from Charles Dickens, Chas. Mathews, Edmund Kean,
dramatist Sheridan, Wilkie Collins, Edward Sothern, and
Tom Taylor. Wybert Reeve, who is now far over 70
years old, claims to have given Henry Irving one of his
first engagements.
An educated baboon is appearing in a serio-comedy at
the London music-halls. After going through a per-
formance the hairy beast sits down to dinner, and is eat-
ing voraciously when his wicked friend (the trainer) fires
a revolver at him, whereupon he throws up his arms,
takes one last despairing look at the vittles, and falls
dead. Curtain. Then the baboon comes before the cur-
tain hand-in-hand with his assassin, and gravely bows
to the audience.

A Sydney cinematograph show exhibits, amongst other
attractions, a spirometer, or lung-tester. Visitors are
considerately informed, per programme, how many
inches a healthy person of certain height ought to raise
dial-hand. And the list is rounded off with the pleasing
p.s., “In the first stai-e of consumption less 30 p.c. ;
second stage, 63 p.c.”! One way to spend a pleasant
afternoon—go to a biograph show and find out whether
you’re in galloping consumption 1

The Ballarat Woollen Mills lately called for
competitive designs and tenders from builders of steam
engines, that of the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, being accepted. These engines are of
the compound surface condensing type, with cylinders
17" and 38" by 60" stroke, and will indicate 700 h.p. on a
consumption of 151 b. steam per hour. The design is a
handsome and massive one, and they are probably the
finest example of engine-building yet produced in the
colony.*

Cablegraphic report of the Mutual Life Insur-
ance Co. of New York at 31st December, 1898 : Insurance
in force, £199,530,000, being an increase of £7,203,000 on
the amount in force at 31st December, 1897 ; assets,
£56,985,900, being an increase during the year of
£4,873,000 ; contingent guarantee fund (being the surplus
of assets over liabilities), £8,673,090, which is £1,382,000
more than the surplus at the end of 1897. In each case
the Mutual of New York shows the largest increase
recorded among insurance companies for the year.
The New Zealand Insurance Co. ended a very
bad year on 30th November, 1898, with a loss of £15,541.
In addition to this it paid a dividend of 10 per cent.
(£20,000) which made the year’s loss £35,541. As the
profit balance was only £2734 the co. deliberately ab-
stracted £35,000 from the reserve-fund to pay the divi-
dend with—a proceeding whose utter folly requires to be
expressed in language that Tiib Bulletin' doesn’t chance
to have about it at the present moment. A few recent
records of the New Zealand Insurance Co. are as follows :
Net Profit Dividend
1891 .. £29,228 12J per cent. = £25,000
1892 .. 40,142 15 ~ 30,000
1893 .. 28,805 13} „ 27,600
1894 .. 26,905 13} „ 27,500
1895 .. 21,453 13} „ 27,500
1896 .. 29,097 13} „ 27,590
1897 .. 11,074 ll| „ 22,500
Net Loss
1898 .. 15,641 10 „ 20,000
Profit £171,163 Dividends £207,600
During these eight yonrs the New Zealand In-
surance Co. has once paid a large dividend against an
actual loss, anil three times it has paid away a consider-
ably larger dividend than it earned. On the whole, it
has paid away £36,337 more than its net earnings, and in
addition the amount set aside to cover depreciation of
assets has not kept pace with the losses, so the investment-
fluctuation fund has dropped from 119,910 in 1891 to
£3266 in 1898—another falling-away of £7644. Also, two
notable items in the investments are Transvaal Govern-
ment Bonds, £5726, and Argentine State securities,
£24,600 ; latter, at all events, looks none too reliable.
The New Zealand Insurance Co. has a paid-up capital of
£200,000, and its total reserves are ' 205,459, so it is still
an exceedingly strong concern. But even a very strong
concern can't afford to take such liberties with its consti-
tution as this one has done in the past two years.

The Ballarat Banking Co. (Ballarat, Vic.)
realised a profit of £2906 during the half-year ended 31st
Dec., 1898—about £4 3s. 7d. per cent, per annum on the
shareholders’ funds (capital £85,000, reserve £54,000).
The past 4years’ profits of the Ballarat Banking Co.
have been :
December, 1894 ..£3710 June, 1897 .. ..£2BBl
June, 1895 .. .. 3449 December, 1897 .. 2637
December, 1895 .. 3463 June, 1898 .. .. 2785
June, 1896 .. .. 3250 December, 1898 .. 2906
December, 1896 .. 2936
After a period of decline, the profits of the Ballarat Bank
have now showed an increase for two half-years in suc-
cession. The usual 6J4 per cent. div. absorbs i 2337 ;
£5OO is added to reserve, making it £54,500 ; and the re-
maining £69, added to previous balance, leaves £2279 at
•credit of profit and loss. The reset ve of cash and Govt,
securities (£67,773) is equal to 32 per cent, of all liabili-
ties to the public, so that both profit and cash
reserves are above the average. The Ballarat Banking Co.
■occupies a unique position in its province. It pays the
biggest dividend among the purely Victorian banks, and
the only ones in Australasia which give better returns to
the shareholders are the West Australian, N.S.W., Com-
mercial of Sydney, Australasia, and Bank of Adelaide. It
is the only Vic. bank which paid dividends steadily all
through the smash ; the only one which lost none of its
capital or reserves in that disastrous period ; and also the
only one which has added to its reserve every half-year
all through the depression era. Which makes a pretty
good record for the Garden City’s bank, even though the
gorgeous 11 per cent. divs. of the days prior to ’92 are no
longer available.

The twelfth half-year of the Australian Joint
Stock Bank, dating from the 1893 reconstruction, and the
third dating from reconstruction No. 2, closed on 31st
Dec. with a net profit of £266. The career of the A.J.S.
during the six years in question has been a good deal
varied :
Profit. Dividend.
June, 1893 .. £12,221 .. Nil.
December, 1893 .. 51,040 .. 5 per cent, = £22,951
June, 1894 .. 32,083 ..6 ~ = 24,869
December, 1894 .. 21,915 ..4 ~ = 21,530
June, 1895 .. 24,932 .. 4 „ = 22,676
December, 1895 .. 25,825 ..4 ~ = 23,354
June, 1896 .. 20,629 .. Nil.
Loss.
December, 1896 .. 48,200 .. Nil.
June, 1897 .. 63,507 .. Nil.
Profit.
December, 1897 .. 1,725 .. Nil.
June, 1898 .. 3,746 .. Nil.
December, 1898 .. 266 .. Nil.
December, 1897, was the date when interest was reduced
from 4J per cent, all round to 3 per cent, on the A and B
■deposits (about £4,000,000) and 3}/, per cent, on the in-
scribed stock deposits (£787,664). At this cheap rate a
bank that is in reasonably good condition should he able
to earn a decent profit, but the £266 realised last half-
year is only equal to tenpence per cent, per annum on the
-shareholders’ alleged funds (capital £1,167,887, and re-
serve £52,000). If it is a fair thing to assume that a sound
bank, with all its assets written down to actual present
value, and getting its deposits at a very reasonable price,
should earn 2 per cent, per annum even in these hard
times (and 2 per cent, certainly looks a very moderate
■estimate), then the A.J.S. Bank, on last half-year’s
figures, needs to write-off £1,193,000 as lost, out of the
£1,219,887 shareholders’ funds in the balance-sheet, and
start fresh with a paid-up capital of £26,600.
There is a great lack of information in the re-
cent balance-sheets of the A.J.S. Bank, by which to
judge its position. It doesn’t show how much the new
deposits amount to (they are lumped in with the recon-
structed A and B liabilities), so it is difficult to say
whether the new business is improving or not. It is im-
possible to say accurately what the reconstructed A and
B deposits are, or how much was paid off during the half-
vear, or what price they were bought at (though it is
known that a considerable amount were repurchased at a
large discount), or how much profit was made out of the
transaction. It isn’t even mentioned that the profit thus
made went to reduce old losses, though apparently it
must have done so. No estimate has ever been supplied
as to the amount of losses to be provided for. The old
bank’s reserves (nearly £890,000) were set aside in 1893 to
cover losses as they came to light, and when last heard of,
some considerable time ago, this fund was reduced to
£96,000. Probably it is all gone by now. The A.J.S.
wrote off hardly any of its capital at the 1893 crisis, while
some other institutions which certainly look more promis-
ing made enormous provision for losses from this source.
A while ago it was alleged in London that the losses
w mid swallow up almost the whole paid capital. But the
pa lful subject dropped there, and the A.J.S. makes no
sig i of cleaning things up and explaining its real posi-
tion.
The note and bill circulation shows a good in-
crease for the half-year, which looks as if the current
business was keeping up. The cash and liquid securities
(£1,406,007) are fully £200,000 more than they were six
months ago, and equal nearly 20 per cent, of liabilities to
thp public, which also looks well enough, and deposits
have increased a little. But it is impossible to do other-
wise than assume, from the profits shown, that there are
large losses not shown in the balance - sheet, which
losses, unless values improve greatly, must be provided
for somehow. If the profit on buying up the old deposits
at a discount can be made to cover them, well and good,
but though the A.J.S. is supposed to have made a
considerable saving in that way lately, the situa-
tion seems to have improved backwards instead of
getting better. If losses can’t be covered in that way
they must come out of the capital and reserves, and the
whole of these are not equal to the amount which the
Commercial of Australia lately wrote off atone fell swoop.
And the A.J.S. has only £391,505 of uncalled capital to
fall back upon, if necessary—a smaller amount than the
reserve capital of any other bank of its size, or near its
size, in Australasia. Altogether, there are very few
promising features about the A.J.S. just at present.

The City Bank of Sydney announces that its
earnings for the half-year July-December, 1898, were
£Bos3—the smallest half-year’s return save one for a very
long time past. The profits shown since ’93 have been
these:
December, 1893 .. £10,730 December, 1890 .. £BO7l
June, 1894.. .. 11,408 June, 1897 .. .. 8048
December, 1894 .. 10,004 December, 1897 .. 8395
June, 1895.. .. 10,930 June, 1898 .. .. 8273
December, 1895 .. 19,020 December, 1898 .. 8053
June, 1890 .. .. 8,108
Dividend at 4 per cent, per annum (£8000) is declarisl,
and the remaining £63 makes the accumulated profit-
balance £9029. The City Bank has declared 4 per cent,
dividends since June, 1890, and it has earned just the re-
quired amount so accurately that the balance only varied
from £4B to £395. In its previous 5 per cent, days the
balance varied from £4 to £3O. But it is generally under-
stood that the City Bank reserves a portion of its profits,
amount not known, to write down the book-value of
its assets, the amount of losses and depreciatio n
to be provided for not being known either, and that when
the unspecified amount thus reserved accumulates in an in-
definite number of years to the indefinite amount re-
quired to provide for the depreciation, whatever it may
be, the visible profits will expand—always provided no
further depreciation turns up in the interval—by an un-
known quantity, and the dividend may possibly increase
by something per cent., and even then leave £r, or even
more, to be added to the reserve. These remarks are
based on an explanation made some time ago by the boss
director at the annual meeting, and, of course, the
inference is that the balance-sheet does not show
the present value of the assets or the present
position of the bank. But a little thing like that
is too common in Australia now to be worth men-
tioning. Only it would be a vast amount better if every
bank which has losses to provide for put the estimated
depreciation plainly among its assets, and showed in each
balance-sheet how much of the profits were set aside in
reduction of the deficit. Then it would appear exactly
whether the gross profits were increasing or diminishing,
and how far the dividend was justified.
The half-year’s profits as set down in the
balance-sheet, are equal to £3 4s. 4d. per cent, per annum
on shareholders’ funds. During the half-year note circu-
lation has increased by £3OOO ; deposits by £43,000 ; cash
by £91,877. The latter item now stands at £377.843 —
equal to the solid provision of nearly 32 per cent, on
all liabilities. (The City Bank has no reserve of
State securities whatsoever.) Also the long-stationary
item of £138,843—“ Premises and Furniture” has
been reduced by £6 18s., which looks as if the bank
had at last started to make a little allowance for deprecia-
tion of its furniture and props. But the share-market,
which sometimes knows what it is talking about, still
estimates City Bank stock lower than any other bank
stock in Australia on which dividends are paid regularly ;
the new issue, £8 paid, is offering, on date of writing, at
£3 10s., at which price it yields £9 2s. 6d. per cent, per
annum to investors.

r * ♦ * *
Broken Hill Proprietary Co.’s shares drift
steadily Europewards ; 609,597 are now on the London
register, and 350,403 scattered over Australia. Adelaide
has 186,398; Melbourne, 118,914 ; and Sydney, 35,105.
The Australian shareholder has apparently come to the
conclusion that the co. is within speaking distance of
another reduction of divs., and so retires in J. Bull’s
favor. Last half-year, for which the accounts are out,
the usual £144,000 was paid ; but, after allowing for de-
preciation, a net profit of £122,151 only was made. The
directors and officers attempt to excuse the shortage by
saying that the removal of smelters and other changes
have been detrimental; but it is certain that sulphide-
treatment cannot be carried on, according to present
knowledge, on a scale big enough to pay £144,000. And,
though there are many tens of thousands of tons of ot her
ore still in the mine, the gradual increase in the propor-
tion of sulphides worked must go on; that seems
absolutely inevitable. Though, therefore, the causes
mentioned may have operated to reduce the past half-
year’s returns, other still more powerful influences will
be at work in the future.
* • • •
Local people at Broken Hill, observing the
diminishing profits of the B.H. Prop, and the economies
everywhere introduced, begin to fear a reduction of
wages and the trouble that that means ; and there is no
difficulty in gaining credence for any such rumor as that
a thousand or two Austrians or Spaniards (both the
manager and the assistant-manager, as well as the
manager of the next biggest mine, have for years past
worked chiefly with Spaniards) are on the water. Special
pains have therefore been taken to show shareholders
what great improvements have already been made in their
interests. Thus the last half-year and the corresponding
half of ’9O are compared, and it is shown that 94 per cent,
more ore (much of it intractable sulphides) was handled
for 7 per cent, less money, notwithstanding that the
operations of extraction are carried further now than they
used to be.
The big co. used to publish with its accounts a
“ working summary,” which showed the net cost and net
profit per ton of ore treated for each half-year ; but this
interesting and valuable table was dropped in ’96—pre-
sumably the fall in profit was too catastrophic. But, any-
how, the statement of the reduction in average cost per
ton would have been an assurance to shareholders that
the workmen have suffered as well as themselves—not a
convincing assurance, it is true ; but the assumption
would have been that way. Taking 1890 (the year when
the railway line was in use first) it is found that the cost
per ton was in one half of the year, £4 10s. 9d., and in
the other, £5 Is. lOd. In those days it was nearly all
good smelting ore. By the end of ’95 (still on smelting
ores) the cost had been brought down by General
Manager Howell to about £2 ss. This last half-
year, costs amounted to £419,220; the quantity of ore
handled was 200,584 tons (though some more was mined
and stored) ;so that the cost per ton was £2 Is. 9d. But,
then, in this last half-year over 70,000 tons of sulphides
were treated, involving, as regards by far the greater part
of it, the successive processes of concentration, roasting,
smelting and refining, instead of the comparatively in-
expensive smelting and refining which served in the old
days.
The increasing proportion of sulphides means
slower smelting; hence the demand for still further
smelting power. The co. will, it is understood, transform
three present 80-ton furnaces at Pt. Pirie into 120-tonners,
and add two 120-tonners to the plant. The struggle to
maintain the divs. will apparently not bo abandoned
lightly. Fortunately the B.H. Prop., unlike almost every
other mine on the Hill, has not anything like reached its
hauling capacity.
B.H. North’s balance-sheet and accounts for
the half-year to Bee. 31 are made to show almost a dead
level—income (additional to a mysterious “interest.
£330,” item), £20,960 ; expenditure, £20,961. Writer saiu
“ made to show," because the co. hasn’t charged itself
with depreciation. This sort of thing is becoming pretty
common with Barrier co’s, whose directors seem to play
with this item. There is promise of greater things in the
now-current half-year, when the development work lately
proceeding will allow’of several more ore-faces being tapped
and greater economy practised. Possibly the mill will
run three shifts daily instead of two ; and a little cheaper
work may thus be done. But per contra the ore, always
hard, is getting harder, and that will take off much of
the very thin coating of gilt that the North gingerbread
was likely to have. One thing in shareholders’ favor is
that there are only 100,090 shares in the co.

* • * *
Clarke and Co. (Adelaide) give an excellent re-
view of Westralian mining for ’9B in their January circu-
lar—a long way ahead of any other issued in Australia.
Admitted that the market has been disappointing in the
face of increased gold-yields and divs. All but four of
the big Kalgoorlie mines have fallen in value, while
numerous co.’s in London and Australia have either ceased
to exist or have been wiped off the market slate. Over-
capitalisation and a glutted market have sickened things
generally. A want of confidence exists in the directors
controlling some of the biggest mines, and the exposures
made in several cases have not helped to restore it. In
nearly all cases directors in London are too heavily paid,
while in several they participate in profits—a robbery
hitherto unheard of in Australian mining. The mines,
too, have not realised the anticipations of the beginning
of last year. Lake View did not increase its pro-
duction as expected. Associated milling returns fell
off and Lake View South and L.V. Extended
proved unpayable. Brookman’s has gradually be-
come poorer. The Perseverance and N. Boulder
returns from oxidized ores fell away, and the Kalgoorlie
Mint ceased to crush. Mines that have come right up to
the mark, as well as those which have dropped far behind
it, are enumerated. At Kanowna, Menzies, Coolgardie,
Bulong, Cue, and the Norseman leases have been thrown
up right and left—in too much' haste in many cases. The
results of the depression have been too drastic, just as
those of the boom were carried too far in the other direc-
tion. In several instances good gold has been won by
miners from claims abandoned by English co’s as reck-
lessly as they were floated. Only one flotation of anv
moment is recorded for the year—what a falling-off is
here !—Sons of Gwalia, at Mt. Leonora. Of course there
have been many reconstructions, the chief of which are
given while “ cats ” innumerable have been allowed to
pass away unobserved in their sleep.
• • • •
Whispered in London a few months back, and
the rumor came to Australia, that Whittaker Wright was
going the way of Hooley and most other boomsters—that
he was giving 10 per cent, for money, etc. Stocks in
Westralian Mines which he largely controls came tumbling
in the face of big yields and developments. It would now
seem that this rumor, as well as the war-scare, was well-
engineered by some person kindly disposed to him. It
has worked that wav, anyhow. It gave him a show of
getting out at the top and in again at the bottom, un-
mindful of yields, divs., and developments ; whether he
took it or not no man here knoweth. First a bull and
then a bear. How often the remark—“ the bear has the
best of it in mining stocks ” ? Better still, writer thinks,
the position of the man who can be a bull one month and
a hear the next. All manner of nasty things were said of
Wright while away from London on holiday. Soon after
his return he brought out the Le Rio mine, British
Columbia, capital a million, £950,000 payable in cash, the
balance (£50,000) for working capital—and he got it.
The shares were largely over-applied, and were at a sub-
stantial premium when the mail left.

This note, which reaches Thk Bulletin from
Buldamia, 21 miles N.E. from Norseman, is given for
what it may be worth :—“Prospectors at this place since
its discovery 2} years ago have had much graft and
struggle. Some crushings have yielded 3oz. per ton. The
battery is four miles from the field at the Lake ; it leaves
a lot in the tailings, some of which have assayed as high
as 2Joz. The want of proper gold-saving appliances is
severely felt. The richest sulphides on the Norseman
goldfields are here ; some have assayed up to 70oz. Some
stone recently treated by the Gold Recovery Co., Kal-
goorlie, gave from one 2ft. reef here lOoz. 18dwt. 12gr.
gold per ton, and sample from another 4ft. reef 15oz.
6dwt. 2gr. gold per ton ; neither showed visible gold. All
the reefs and lories are vertical, and the stone is like the
Kalgoorlie ore in all particulars. One peculiar feature is
that the sulphides live very close to the surface. The
country is hard, and no particular depth has yet been
explored. The country is a vast dyke with an east and
west trend, and extends about two miles in length. A co.
that would put up suitable plant would make things
hum.’’

Kalgurli mine, Hannan’s, decided to adopt the
Lake View methods of treatment, and this after getting
every possible information from Mt. Morgan,'Cripple Creek,
and elsewhere. Plant designed to treat 0000 tons a
month. Upwards of £30,000 in hand and 10,000 reserved
shares.
It was baldly cabled some weeks ago that
the resolutions of the Great Boulder committee to rescind
the milling contract, etc., had been lost—with the infer-
ence that the directors had won right along the line. The
resolutions, Tint Bulletin finds, were carried by 1)01(1
votes—a substantial majority, but short of the proportion
requisite to alter the articles. The first resolution was a
vote of censure on the directors, and the articles do not
provide for a three-fourths majority in this case. The
directors’ hides, however, will probably have been found
thick enough to stand it without resignation. Manager
Hamilton's wire anticipating success for the Konemann
process was, of course, referred to, and several persons
barracked all they knew for the directors, including Louis
Aarons, formerly of Sydney Stock Exchange. He read a
cable predicting big success for the process, and after de-
clining to reveal the name of the sender, admitted, under
great pressure, that it came from “William Smith,” at
which announcement the meeting fell over itself with
laughter. W. T. .lones, up to the time of the meeting,
was regarded as a director dissatisfied with the contract,
but, at iast moment, he turned tail and even declared that
if Konemann offered to cancel the bargain he would be no
party to it. The process may turn out all right. Writer
hopes it will, and that the Boulder co. will pull out on the
deal. But will that excuse the directors in making a con-
tract under which theco. finds all the money for the plant,
pays 12s. per ton—double the cost of the most effective
system known so far—sacrificing 40 per cent, of the
residues of the gold recovered, and takes in return a
half-share in the profits made? Estimated that the
working of the process will eat up 20 per cent, of the
mine's profits, and it will need to be a great process to
stand that. They now declare that it is going to revolu-
tionise the gold history of Westralia. The same has been
said of every gold-saving machine or method. The pro-
cess has never been tried elsewhere. Should the process
fail, or do ho better work than cyanide, it is to be hoped
that the directors who launched into this extraordinary
milling contract, and wrapped the whole thing in mys-
tery till it was dragged from them, will modestly con-
tinue to hold on to their seats, so that they can be
ignominiously fired at next meeting.
Shares in the Mt. Reid Co. (a British concern of
150,000 shares at £1) have had a good rise recently.
Owing to sensational rumors that the co. had “ struck it
rich," and not seeing any official report in local or other
papers respecting same, shareholders naturally dropped
in at the legal manager’s office, situated, of course, in
Hobart, to make enquiries as to the truth of aforesaid
rumors. The management cheerfully admitted that
phenomenal assays had lately been made (one going as
high as llOOoz. silver and 30oz. gold per ton), but as the
co. had not been in the habit of publishing assays, good
or bad, in the past it was not going to do so now, or
in the future. How consoling, especially for interpro-
vincial holders of scrip, who are absolutely in the dark as
regards reason of improvement in prices, and who may
have sold some considerable time after the extraordinary
discoveries at the mine without being aware of any
improvement having taken place.
Rocky River (Tas.): “Fair amount of yellow
copper distributed through the whole formation.” But
what is a fair amount— 3 per cent, or 30 per cent. ? What,
too, has become of the £30,000 worth of ore at grass men-
tioned when the thing was floated, as well as taken account
of as a sort of asset in the co.’s balance-sheet? What has
become of the report made on it by the acute Holroyd,
of telluride celebrity ? Why not published ? Is it because
He declared the ore at grass valueless, and that the mine
was not much better? His report might not be interest-
ing reading to some people, hut it is doubtless bona-fide,
and the comparison with other reports would be found
remarkable.

The alleged appointment of somebody
described as Earl Beauchamp to be Governor of
N.S.W. is probably a cablegraphic eri’or ; the new
Earl Poulett is more likely the individual meant.
There are several reasons for this idea. In the
first place, hardly anybody out here ever heard of
Beauchamp, and almost everybody has heard of
Poulett. It is certain there is a Poulett and not
at all certain there is any Beauchamp. The re-
port states that the new Governor is musical, and
as Earl Poulett, when Viscount Hinton, lived for a
number of years by grinding a barrel-organ in the
streets, he obviously fills the bill. N.S.W. is very
earnest just now over the teaching of cookery, and
AN ILL WIND.
HE : “ This wind is awful —it spoilt a silk hat of mine this morning."
SHE : “ Yes; and it necessitates wearing such expensive petticoats!"
as the present Earl Poulett is the son of his dis-
reputable old father and his father’s cook, his
appointment would be touchingly appropriate. If
Poulett doesn’t know something about cookery be
certainly ought to. Moreover, the kind of cookery
Poulett knows about is doubtless the cheap kind
just suited to Australia’s present circumstances ;
he was fired out at a very early age, along with his
mother, by the old earl, and has run after his
vittles in a precarious sort of way ever since, and
he wasn’t brought up on the rich sirloin of beef or
other extravagances.
Earl Poulett, N.S.W.’s suspected new Governor,
has lived mostly in the odour of tripe and cheap
pigs’ feet and twopennorth of gin, and by reason
of his inexpensive upbringing he would probably
be glad to take the billet for half salary, and the
other half could go towards paying off Raid’s de-
ficit. N.S.W., too, is essentially a sporting com-
munity, and the new Earl Poulett, as a person
who has been in gaol for getting a gun under false
pretences, has a sporting air about him which
seems to fit the case right down to the ground. If
there is no mistake, and it really is Beauchamp
and not Poulett who is coming here, The Bulle-
tin reckons that an immediate effort should be
made to swap peers. Beauchamp is said to be a
piously-inclined young man who doats on Gre-
gorian chants and will be like unto a long, dull
hymn in a dry land. A rakish costermonger-
earl with a barrel-organ and a bashed hat, who
would preside at meetings of the Executive
Council with a black eye, and a pipe, and a bull-
pup (or a monkey) under his arm, would be much
less oppressive. Australia has had Brassey and
Buxton already, and is quite tired of serious-
minded Governors.
* * * *

If true, as stated, that Premier Reid was
consulted prior to the appointment of Gov. Hamp-
den’s successor, then the selection of an unmarried
figure-head may he due to a cunning suggestion.
Heigh-ho ! for the tightening of those “ silken
bonds ” if the Earl finds his “ Countess ” amongst
the Sydney “Naieest ” !
Curious to note how ready the Sydney
morning papers were to drop the Gatton and
other similar affairs as soon as something else—
the Samoan embroglio—came along. For three
weeks they had boiled and re-boiled the bones of
the various local corpses — and whereas the
Dubberke confession was a relief in its way, the
Samoan coup was a positive godsend.
• * • *
The theory that the Gatton slaughterer
afterwards killed his accomplice is in accord with
the practice of the logical criminal, who never
loses sight of the fact that what is known to two
is no secret. Mister Tod, please note.
• * * *
The very newest police-theory about the
Gatton murder is that Burgess, the alleged
assassin, whom so far, apparently, no one can
connect with the crime, afterwards butchered his
accomplice—that mysterious person whom nobody
ever saw or heard of—and buried him in some
place unknown. This makes the matter even
more complicated than before. Burgess, so far as
he can be traced on both sides of Gatton, was a
solitary, seedy battler, with no mate. He is not
known to have brought a mate to the district, and
as no mate has gone amissing there, lie seemingly
didn’t take one away from it. But, as no one
man could well do that stupendous butchery
single-handed, the other man has to be assumed,
and, as he isn’t to be found now, his grave has to
be assumed also. So it is concluded that the man
who doesn’t seem to have been there got rid of the
fellow who apparently wasn’t anywhere by knock-
ing him on the head with something, and putting
him nowhere in particular. Of course, the police
may “know something,” but that is how the
matter strikes outsiders up to now. So —
* * * *
The wholesale and general identification of
Burgess as the man at the slip-rails on the night
of the Gatton murders and as the dubious
stranger who was seen all round the district for
many miles in every direction just before and
after that date, is a fine sample of something or
other. Every paper of plant and respectability
in Bananaland printed a picture of a man with
all the more prominent features of the accused
and labelled it “BURGESS.” Consequently
every person who has looked at him since has
been startled to find him strangely familiar—seen
him somewhere. And the mind, struggling to
locate the recollection, fits it on to the figure of
the first person who comes to memory of whose
features no impression was retained. To those
good people who saw in the moonlight the
rough outline of the man at the slip-rails the
features of the picture fit naturally into the blank
below the slouched hat. In all probability if
Burgess could be secretly carried about the country
he would be unflinchingly identified everywhere
as the man Miss Hysteria and the resident church-
warden saw behaving in a suspicious manner on
the night of the local crime.
* * • •
Some more aibout the boot-box tragedy :—
The veteran Dr. Neild, in maintaining that the boot-box
victim, Mabel Ambrose, died of suffocation, was probably
right, as the girl Dubberke, telling the awful story of the
unhappy Ambrose’s death, deposed that Madame Rada-
lyski said she “ had placed her hands over the girl’s
mouth to stop her from screaming, and her eyes were
starting from the sockets.” Dubberke, who avers that
she confessed because her conscience pricked her, is in-
deed a cool hand, and a queer study of a 20-year-old girl.
Her conscience evidently didn’t prick her much until she
found the police had struck her trail. To many who live
in the neighborhood of South Yarra, Dubberke was
pretty well-known as an average, smartish style of girl,
such as may be seen in scores any Saturday night in
Chapel-street, Prahran ; her only point of real beauty
being a pair of exceptionally deep, dark eyes.
This sweet charmer, according to her own story at the
inquest, suggested that one of the dead girl’s teeth should
THE PRIDE OF SEX.
SHE : “ Why don’t you go out and look for work?”
HE : “ ’Ow can I with these disgraceful clothes on?"
SHE: “ Well, I had—and mine arc worse."
HE: “ You ’aren't no position to keep up — and,
besides, a woman ’asn't got the same pride a
man ’as.”
bo knocked down her throat to prevent identification.
After the squeamish Tod declined to help remove the
body from one room to another and cram the dead girl
into the trunk, Dubberke kindly volunteered her able
assistance, stripped the body, cut oil most of the hair,
bent the legs so as to lit the box, helped to burn the
clothes, and then made herself a cup of tea while further
arrangements were discussed. Then she went with Tod
at dead of night and helped to draff the trunk over a
fence across a lonesome paddock by a lonesome riverside,
hiding it in some bushes till a belated pair of lovers had
departed and left the way clear. Also she helped
to fix the wire and Btone to the trunk, and grisly
deed !—to slide it into the Yarra at one o'clock
in the morning. Then the girl with the protruding jaw
returned to the house and no doubt took more lea, her
Conscience having evidently been mislaid in the hurry of
circumstances. For sheer deliberateness, it would be
hard to recall many characters in the history of crime fit
to straighten the jaunty hat of this Dubberke girl. At
the detective office: “ Her first request was for a cup of
tea, and on its being supplied she threw herself back upon
a sofa and leisurely sipped it.”
The beautiful Sunday-school side of young man Tod's
character is revealed in Dubberke’s confession: "Tod
was at the house the night before Mabel died and slept in
her room. . . . About half-an-hour after breakfast
Madame told Mabel to go upstairs and go to bed. Tod
was then with witness in the kitchen. They heard Mabel
crying out, and Tod asked her what Madame did that
caused her to scream. She replied that she did not know,
but that Mabel always screamed when Madame was treat-
ing her. lie seemed much concerned. When Mabel
stopped crying Tod called out and asked whether Madame
had finished, and Mabel answered ‘ Yes.’ Tod went up-
stairs, and, on returning, said to witness, ‘ Poor girl, 1
can't help having some regard for her.’ ” It is at such times
that the plain, unvarnished sinner appears to better advan-
tage than bis church-going brother, whose saintly family
connections forbid him to be associated with an illicit baby.
If the author of Mabel’s unborn child bad been a blas-
phemous, wrong-thinking youth he would probably have
shut down on the abortion scheme at the sound of bis
paramour s agonised cries. Whereas Tod—reared in an
odour of sanctity and necessarily a coward when exposed
to the risk of scandal—waited till the screams had ceased
ere going to console his girl, and then took credit for his
kindness in not being' able to “ help having some regard
for her.” The good young man, whose donah died.
Verily, Dubberke has marvellous nerve. Before being
called at the inquest, she yarned away with the “d.”
sitting beside her, laughing lightly at intervals, picking
out “ nice hoys'' in the court with tier big blue eyes, and
now and again glancing down at the little patent-leather
shoes which she twiddled in the sunlight. When called,
she crossed the court, and to those who saw her for the
first time, seemed almost younger than her 20 years. Her
hair was hanging down, her dress was a trifle short and
girlishly cut, and altogether she would have easily passed
for a schoolgirl of 17. But the evidence ! What a con-
trast to her appearance ! The police witnesses—some of 30
years’ experience—were arrant chumps compared with this
girl on a witness-stand for the first time in her life. As
soon as the Crown Prosecutor said “Get into the box,”
and made everybody think of the dead Mabel Ambrose
getting into her box, people saw another Thekla. Men
present, who had seen every “big” case for years,
reckoned her absolutely the best witness they had ever
met. Apparently possessed of an education far in ad-
vance of the average farmer’s daughter, Thekla described
in a soft, ladylike manner how she had suggested ham-
mering out one of the dead girl’s teeth. From start to
finish there was not a mispronounced word nor an
ungrammatical sentence, and Thekla stepped down as
fresh as paint after standing up for nearly three hours.
She concluded by a triumphal admission to solicitor Levy
that she had been visited by “ dozens of sweethearts.”
The trend of the cross-examination had obviously been
to show up the fair witness, but the latter beat Levy
badly by calmly owning up to all his accusations with
the air of a Madonna. “ Isn’t Mr. Levy a horrid man?”
she commented at the rising of the inquest. “He asked
me such nasty questions. But I didn’t care; I was de-
termined to speak the truth, and he didn’t expect that.”

boot-trunk inquest was provided by a tall, fair,
handsome young man—married, of course, who
came forward and calmly swore that he couldn’t
remember anything. The Crown tried to jog his
memory by placing a portrait of Mabel Ambrose
in his hand, and when he said that wasn’t the girl
at all, the Crown was rude enough to ask “ what
girl?” Eventually witness admitted that he had
met three girls in the street one night. To the
best of his belief it was one night, but he wasn’t
exactly sure. He and two friends went with the
girls— to the best of his belief—in a cab
somewhere. He didn’t know whether it
was South Yarra they went to or not, but
they called at a house and he stayed there
a few moments and then left. Apparently
he didn’t discover where he was when he left,
either, but he admitted that to the best of his
recollection he was sober. His reason for embark-
ing on the enterprise, he explained, was because
he thought he might as well go home that way—
which is much the same as taking a short cut from
Redfern toDarlinghurst through Manly. The only
thing he was certain about was that, although he
couldn’t say who any of the women were, he had
not “ mashed ” them. They had been introduced
—of that he was convinced, though every other
fact was to the best of his belief. And, with a
sense of having bravely done his duty by being
introduced, he proudly “stood down.”

The Caledonian strain in the youth Tod,
charged with complicity in the Melb. boot-trunk
murder, came out strongly at the inquest. When
Tod’s girl left her home for the purpose of abor-
tion he went to a boarding-house keeper of the
locality in his capacity of commission-agent, and
said he oould got her a nice, rospeotable young lady-
boarder, and would ask only half-a-week’s money
as commission. Mabel Ambrose duly turned up
at the house to explain that she intended to stay
for a week only, so her Scotchbyterian “boy”
drove the particularly keen bargain of getting a
week’s board at half-price. Tod is quite lost in
gaol. He should have remained at the Sunday-
school and^ good-fellowship union.
The childlike innocence of somo Molb.
constables is really touching. The other night a
local officer observed a man ooming away from a
big house on St. Kilda-road in the early a.m. His

slippers and pyjamas railing about some man he
had seen from his window leaving the yard with
a bag over his shoulder.
* * * *
A clergyman at Golden Gully (Vic.),
is a militant Christian with “ a quick left. ” The
other Sunday a “ push ” entered his church and
began to raise Cain. The parson expostulated,
but, as the roughs refused to “get,” he climbed
down from his pulpit and threw one or two of
them into the street after “a very willing go.”
None of the congregation offered to give the shep-
herd a hand, having no inclination to spoil sport.
The members simply perched round on the pews,
and barracked for the good cause. The local
paper decla-ros that the collection that morning
was exceptionally good. The Bulletin doesn’t
doubt it, and begs to call the attention of parsons
generally to the fact that the public will always
pay handsomely to see the minister fight it out
with a representative of Satan in a 16ft. ring,
where they wouldn’t give a red cent for mere
windy disputation.

translation of the “card” of the Bank of New
South Wales is: “ New South Wales money-
house—chief man’s salute.”

“ Antalien ” : At North
Sydney recently saw two vege-
table hawkers with carts: one, a
Chinaman with a half-caste child,
t’other a Caucasian with two small boys.
Chinaman did all the door-to-door biz him-
self, while his youngster brought along the cart;
Caucasian drove his cart, while directing, with
much superfluous blasphemy, the house-to-house
canvas of his kids. Now, which of these twain
were the better citizen?
• • * *
S.A. Chief-Justice Way’s illness is causing
his friends much anxiety. What was originally
an attack of influenza has developed into pleurisy
and, at time of writing, a very serious case. The
little man’s health has been indifferent of late
through overwork consequent upon Judge Bun-
dey’s illness and Gov. Buxton’s absence ip Eng-
land. Meanwhile S.A. lawyers complain that big
cases which have been hanging over for months
will be further delayed, unless at least two acting-
judges are appointed to assist Boucaut, the only
one well enough to do his work.

A Sydney suburban bootmaker has his
Bign—“Boots souled and healed.”
It is touching to watch Sydney street-
cleaners in the early mornings. When the man
with the hose comes round he has been preceded
by the lean and hungry dosser, and the bony and
famished mongrel, and the all-devouring goat,
with the result that all the casual rubbish-boxes
on the sidewalk have been investigated and most
of their contents spread about the gutter. Some
of the refuse is gathered up and
replaced in the proper receptacles,
and when the hose is turned on
the remainder floats down street
and accumulates in masses at
convenient places. Half-an-hour
later all the boxes have been over-
turned again, and the scavenger
who comes round by-and-bye
spends most of his time in
gathering up the cabbage-
stalks and empty jam-
tins again, and the
remainder in curs-
ing the dosser
and the mongrel
and the goat, and
the Mayor and
city aldermen, and
his predecessor with
the hose, and the uni-
verse.
“ Porthos ” : Re cri-
minals wearing moustaches
as a general rule. Is it the
hair on lip that causes them
to fall from grace, or is wicked-
ness a good hair-stimulator for
the upper lip? Possibly a law
prohibiting the growth of these
sin-producers might be the short-
est way to the millenium. Give
wickedness the cut direct.
Sleath, M.L.A., tho loudest and probably
also tin* dullest and most ignorant lioro in N.S. W.
Assembly, appeared in Sydney Central Polios
Court last wooi< in a squalid matter concerning an
old woman and two young ones and a wine shop.
The old woman alleged that Sleath haunted her
wine-shop, and pawed her two daughters while
they sat on his knee, and that he called
her blastiferous names when she attempted to
interfere, and rushed at her with threats of
“ screwing her neck." Therefore she asked that
Sleath, M.L.A., who is a married man with a
family, should be bound over not to screw her
neck. Tho old lady alleged that Sleath tried to
haul her on to his knee on one occasion, but that
lion, member repudiated the charge with indigna-
tion and declared that none of the family ever sat
on his knee. Me admitted, however, having
threatened to “tip" the old lady's head in the
sink once when he was in having an ice-cream.
The aged mother fled fron. the wine-shop after that,
her alleged reason being that she was scared off
by Sleath, M.L.A., or afraid lest her daughters
might get off that politician’s knee and rush at
her, or something of the sort. The daughters in
their evidence rather backed up Sleath (one of
them is reported as having stated that he had
“ slept at the house frequently since the night of
the dispute ”) and accused the old lady of can-
tankerousness ; also, Sleath offered to take his
“dying oath ” that he wouldn’t address her again.
In the end the hon. member was ordered not to
molest old Mrs. Brown for three months, but he
gave notice of appeal, so the alleged pawing of
the Brown family in a cheap wine-shop will pro-
bably be discussed in a higher court. But it is a
miserable, dirty, squalid little row.
The people who run the Cinematograph missed
a good thing by not being on hand to reproduce
the police-court antics of Sleath aforesaid. It
was, without exception, the most awful exhibition
of inane conceit and crass stupidity this writer
ever saw after watching N.S.W. Parliamentary
debates for years. When “Wileannia” took
charge of the case he put one foot on the chair and
preceded every word with a series of guttural
grunts, such as “er-haw, hah, er-ah, erhaugh,
and heehaw.” He had evidently come to court
with the idea that the affair was a huge joke, and
whatever elements of danger were in the case
would be quickly scorched into nothingness by his
burning eloquence. His remarks were absurd,
his questions utterly irrelevant, and a cynical
crowd laughed derisively. Every time they
laughed Sleath stopped, folded his hands, and
leered complacently—he actually thought they
were laughing at his wit. And when the scornful
hilarity became thunderous, Sleath’s crazy bellow
was loudest of all. The case lasted from 11.30
a.m. till 2.45 p.m. without interval for lunch.
Towards the finish the statesman staggered
the Court by asking it—“ Even if it was
true that there was anything wrong between
him and the females he was pleased to de-
scribe as young ladies, was it not the duty of
this wicked old woman (the young: ladies’ mother)
to have screened it, and hid it?” When the
P.M. suddenly burst in on the flood of his elo-
quence with the terse announcement that Richard
Sleath, M.L. A., was bound over to keep the peace
for three months, in default one month’s beastly
common jug, the legislator gaped at him in aston-
ishment. If the sun had burst and the earth got
off its axis, he couldn’t have been more dumb-
founded. For 30 seconds he went “off his
kadoova ’’; then he jammed his hat on and strode
towards the door in awful fury. Two policemen
made at him, and signalled for more ; but they
were not needed—terrible Dick quietly submitted
to be bound over.

Two Sydney doctors are arguing. Each
A GOOD TIME COMING.
CUSTOMER (who has called for a, shave, and over-
hears terrible noise overhead): “Great heavens I
what's that row? ”
THE BARBER'S WIFE: “O noth ink; it's on'y my
'usband 'avin a fit. He'll be right in a minnit,
an'll soon be down to attend to yer. ”
has his Sunday organ. One maintains that a
vegetable diet means health ; the other yells for
flesh and swears that fruit and cabbage spell
diabetes : “ eight out of ten persons in Australia
exhibit the most pronounced diagnostic feature of
that dreadful disease ” —Rats ! —owing he says, to
“ tho craze for eating fruit. Meat did not produce
it—sugar, fruit and farinaceous foods in excess
certainly did.” But doesn’t much meat help on
albuminaria—and which is the more deadly in
Australia, albuminaria or diabetes? The fact is
simply this —what is food to one is poison to
another. Look at those two doctors. Some men
are built to calmly eat fruit, others to ferociously
gnaw chops. Go and inspect the pair.
“When a man is forty, he is either a fool or a
physician.” Both those doctors are (at a guess)
just over forty. Each has probably found out what
suits himself. But let him not measure other
people’s corn by his own bushel.
• * * *
The Bulletin regrets to hear that the
Sydney “ Pen and Brush 'Club,” as it was pro-
posed to call the ink and paint amalgam, has gone
bung. This paper sorrowfully predicted its down-
fall some time ago, as it could not see how a
Bohemian dub could succeed in Sydney without
the help of the Philistine. The idea of a club—
where the musician would regale his brother artist
of evenings with inspired music, while the news-
paper-man would read aloud passages from his
paragraphs to assembled multitudes, and where
the painter would hover round, making life-studies
of the distinguished men alluded to —had no
vitality in it. The musician, after eight or ten
hours’ teaching, wants to get miles away from any
music, while the newspaper man would rather bo
fired from the mouth of cannon than read any-
thing of his once it has appeared in print. Be-
sides, there are no Bohemians now. Everyone’s
married, and who ever heard of a married
Bohemian ?

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 30, 1899.
My Dear Myee —
Judging by his portrait, the new N.S.W. Gover-
nor is going to take his place in the same class as
the tryingly-intellectual “M.E.J.,” who used to
cause frightful tremors among just-ordinarv people
by starting casual allusions to the orations of
Demosthenes at her Govt. House Garden Parties.
Earl Beauchamp is 27 years old, has the head of a
well-fed Shelley—or other “shirt-collar” poet —
and is said by those who have gazed upon him in
the flesh to be too Beautiful for anything. He
doesn’t wear a moustache, has every single taste
Ouida might have showered upon her heroes, loves
hunting as well as Homer, divides his soul between
gardening, blowing the saxhorn and Thucydides,
and—-but I might go on like this for ever. His
Ex. isn’t married, and has a big sister who
adores amateur theatricals. Govt. House will
probably receive its ownest own—for there’s
no doubt about it, this appointment is
simply the rarest good thing Sydney sassiety has
ever dreamed of in its wildest flights—early next
pat,ip shop tmm and Mi* rent, sky-high.
The hereditary interest which new Gov. Beau-
champ takes m Gregorian music will commend him
to that section ot oydney Anglicanism which has
taken to plain chant. Gregonamsm is seemingly
gaining converts m Sydney, for Arthur Smyth,
the instigator of the soulful craze at St. James’s,
is now inoculating the people of Christ Church
against the florid banalities of modern music.
Our only two remaining male gubernatorials,
His Ex. and Captain Fielden, came down from
Sutton Forest for the Anniversary Races, taking
the Regatta on their way. Lady Hampden and
the Brand girls have not been seen in public for
quite a long time, and Rand wick had to depend
on itself, as it were, on Thursday for good dressing,
Certainly there was an excited rumor that a live
Duke was wandering on the lawns, but the flutter
subsided when the only Alba turned out to possess
thin little legs and a big nose, and a generally-
decayed appearance. His ducal charms were
further discounted by a grey shrunk suit and a
straw hat with a dark-brown past. Despite all
the slurs cast on local snobocracy, Sydney does
not condescend yet to dukes who dress anyhow.
> th® Duke, who left for London by
the Himalaya on Saturday, must carry away an
awful opinion ot what Australian girls will say
under no pressure whatever. At the Anniversary
races, chatting away to a party, he remarked in
the clippy fashion now in vogue that a certain
race was N.1.8.D. Don t you know what that
means . said he to the prettiest girl in the group.
She answered without turning a hair, “ Not too ”

blushed up to the tint of lus strawberries, and

that things were Not Too Blooming Dusty.
In frocking at the races the pretty wife of a well-
known bookie cut the most conspicuous figure.
She wore a bright-rose polonaise looped up
over a sea of pallid flounces, and was slender
enough to carry off the construction satisfactorily,
This kind of frock generally finds its way on to a
big fat lady with a red face, where it looms in the
air like a red brick coming you from an
enemy s sung'- Miss Inez Bensusan s dark color-
ing was brightened up effectually by a pretty
piped blouse of cerise glace, worn with a white
alpaca skirt braided in black velvet, and a large
black picture-hat. Mrs. Tom Watson had a cool,
collarless blue-and-white muslin, with a black
velvet neck band. Mrs. Ma,carthur Onslow’s
black foulard was sprinkled with white pepper,
and topped with a black-feathered hat. One art-
less little white frock had a transparent muslin
bodice, through which you divined the pink
ribbons run in-and-out of the camisole beneath.
Another sweeping white dress was serried from
toe to throat with tucks and invaluable lace, and
was served up on a pink silk slip. But white
over pink never sends thrills through aesthetic-
minded people. Those white-muslins-over-pink-
glazed-toilet-tables of the past have somehow
taken the keen edge off the otherwise delicious
combination. A great many simple white gowns
were in evidence. After white came black-and-
white checks, most of which were touched up
happily with cerise or turquoise.
The two days were “off,” as far as local swelldom
goes, so there was a big increase in feminine punt-
ing. The stand never hugs the ring so hotly when
society is looking on, you know. I noted curiously
how callously the male kind repel any timid little
enquiries as to “ What do you fancy for this race,
Mr. Browne ? ” The common parry is a blank
“I’m not betting this afternoon,” which sounds
well from the lips of hardened turf-eaters like
Blank and Dash and Asterisk, who couldn’t keep
off if they were paid. But really the persistent
way in which some women pursue men with half-
crowns, and demand, on the ground of the very
slenderest acquaintanceship, that the pursued one
shall go and lay that half-a-crown on a dead cer-
tainty that couldn’t fail if it tried, must be a sore
trial to encounter at a race meeting. Particularly
when the plunger says, confidingly, “111 leave it
all to you, Mr. Browne; just put it on whatever
horse you think is going to win,” and adds, as an
epilogue, later on, “ why didn’t you put my
° n Skinbone they all said he was bound
? And \ SU P.P° S ® you backed him for your-
se}f' and Sllmlar insults. Which isn t the sort
of amusem ent a real live man goes to seek at
races J or so I have been told.
Saturday’s meeting was chiefly remarkable for
the emptiest members’ stand for years. A few
well-dressed women were brave enough to show
that they were not spending their off season in

Mrs. Monty Severn’s black and white checked

Starched white frocks again characterised the
dressing here, and amateur hat-builders brought
out a big display of curious erections on which
undiscovered flowers hob-nobbed with fowls of the
air, and tinselled nets were stitched over all to
keep them still. Some of these efforts are the
most genuinely hard-worked tributes to the
cricketers’ charms that ever will be eventuated.
As yet Bananaland lias supplied the loveliest
example of what a devoted girl will do in the
throes of cricket measles. Two badly-strioken
girls watched young Clem. Hill eating peaches,
one day, and when he had finished and moved off,
one of them made a rush at his plate, and carried
off a peach stone. By the light in her eye as she
showed it to a friend she might have captured a
whack of diamonds. Somebody told the pretty
story to Clem, afterwards. Whereupon the
youthful hero broke into language that showed a
horrid lack of sympathy with the poet’s sentiment
about “A clod, a piece of orange peel, the end of
a cigar, once trod upon by a princely heel, how
beautiful they are ! ”
Local art-circles have been grieved to hear of
the death of Miss Catherine Devine. She was at
one time a member of our Art Society, and, while
she was in Sydney, exhibited some flower-painting
of no mean order. Nine or ten years ago she re-
turned to England, and was commonly supposed
to be doing well there. Her talented sister, Mrs.
Stoddard, whom she resembled personally, was
shortly going home to join her. And now comes
the news of her suicide in a fit of temporary
insanity.
The second meeting of the Press Bazaar didn’t
bring things much nearer the goal, somehow. The
newspaper proprietors again failed to roll up; an
additional Herald representative, in the shape of
Mr. Samuel Cook, being the only new male comer,
Miss Gordon, of the Sun, offered her services as
secretary pro tern., and Mr. Crawley, of the
Sunday Times, is to share her labors. Mr. Cook
was appointed chairman of the committee, and
that is about the extent of the business done to
date.
The scare about Dolly Vane’s retreat to a con-
vent hit Sydney hard one day this week. A
certain journal’s notice-board put up in big black
letters “Miss Vane Enters a Convent!” and
everyone rushed for a penn’orth. Beyond the
sell there was nothing in the news to feed excite-
ment. A clipped interview mildly stated that
the popular little Dolly had determined on taking
the veil in two years’time—having already found,
possibly, that all is vaneity. YVhicli announce-
ment has started everybody asking “ Who is he?”
