A band of resourceful Tasmanian girls, kept out
of the running for eligible navals by too-severely-
straight-laced parents, lately took the game into
their own hands, and schemed to give themselves
a night of unadulterated joy. They rented a
furnished private house, invited a crowd of naval
officers, and made quiet preparations for a ball,
The local big ballroom was engaged that night by
a Personage’s At Horne, and as soon as the heads
of the family had set out for this function the
“girls” made ready and slipped off to their
rendezvous. The ball was naturally a wild
success.
Which recalls a somewhat similar society
function that happened in Sydney years ago. A
number of school-girls in pig-tails and short dresses
—none of them were over fifteen—conceived the
idea of giving a ball in the Town Hall. They
boldly interviewed the Mayor, who was Young
then, and coaxed him into lending the building,
sent out invitations, and ran, “all on their own,”
a highly successful hop. Its only failing was a
scarcity of supper, but the unusuai number of
brilliantly pretty girls in their own natural bloom
made up for all short comings. Among the
perpetrators of this daring festivity were Lady
Bertie and her sisters, Mrs. Livingstone-Lear-
month, and Mrs. Allison.
The Jubilee Singers farewelled Sydney last
Saturday, and are now taking their rich voices
round the suburbs. Madame Orpheus McAdoo
certainly has a rare insight into frocking. On
Saturday she ivas wearing a dream of a frock in
black embroidered net over white silk, with one of
those skirts that cling at the top and flounce out
at the bottom, and have the look of fitting and yet
hanging away from the wearer. The whole dress
was exquisitely jetted, and a ruched white bebe
ribbon outlined the pattern of the jet uniquely.
Long, transparent sleeves were crossed with this
fine white ruching, and three bold dabs of gera-
nium red brought the frock into a becoming light
on the wearer’s dusky face. Also, Mrs. McAdoo
wears her frocks with more daring and chic than
any other platform lady known to Sydney.
History repeats itself, but often reverses itself
as well. Prof. Wood sailed from us with a bride.
Mr. Henderson comes to us with one. Mr. Hen-
derson, who took a brilliant historical degree here,
went to England subsequently for the Oxford
course, and while there won the same prize for
history that is the brightest jewel in Prof. Wood’s
crown. It was, therefore, quite fitting that he
should be recalled to explain to the youtli of Syd-
ney how noble a thing is history, while his senior
is sporting in the shade with the tangles of
Neaera’s hair. But it was not so meet that he
should have played such a joke on Prof. Scott, the
beloved bachelor professor of the University.
Prof. Scott, most hospitable of men, wrote to the
new lecturer, asking him to share his abode in
Sydney. “I shall certainly come,” wrote Mr.
Henderson in reply, “and bring my wife with
me.” In the least possible time after the receipt
of this letter in Sydney Mr. Henderson received a
cable bearing the pregnant message, “ Don’t.”
I am sorry to have misled you regarding tho
wedding of tho trader who has won all Grenfell’s
fair hearts by supplying delicious confections for
Grenfell’s fair heads. The wedding has not yet
eventuated, and the luxury attributed to tho cere-
monial which didn’t take place was apparently the
work of the local imagination endeavoring to
launch a popular personage into matrimony with
suitable splendor.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Melb. Softgoods Association had an annual
pionio on Jan. 20th, when a mighty gathering of
yard-measurers, job-liners, and gentlemen who
‘walk the floor’ took a noble pride in their con-
nection with the Trade. None but a draper can
properly appreciate a draper's greatness, and oven
then it is necessary for him to have ‘ served in the
retail ’ if he would realise how high he stands
above the little world outside of The Trade. J. M.
Bruce, speaking for the Softgoods Association,
said he ‘looked upon it as being a power in the
land,’ and he advised all young men in the various
departments, especially the ribbons and under-
clothing, to ‘ put their bands to the plough ’ and
help to accomplish Federation. Whereat tho
assembled association washed its hands with in-
visible soap and looked towards Heaven, saying
‘ What’s the next article ? ’
“The formal opening of the Federal Council
was a wretched show theatrically. It was done
strictly on the cheap. The two rows of Naval
Brigade-men who line up on Parliament House
steps on the occasion of the opening and closing of
the Legislature, were not brought along on this
occasion. A few loafers hung round tne top of
Bourke-street, and about a score of ladies went
into the galleries, including Lady Brassey in a
grey silk dress and her regulation smile.
“From the ‘State’ dinner-party given by Gov.
Brassey to the Federal Council, and to which
ladies were invited, Mrs. Alfred Deakin was
absent, though her eloquent husband was there.
Mrs. Deakin never goes to any public or semi-
publio function. She is one of the handsomest
women in Melbourne, and quite the tallest of any
importance, but hides her inches under the family
roof-tree. Being studious and having other in-
terests, one of the most interesting of Melbourne
ladies is thus totally unknown to social life.
“ Only a few Federal councillors have brought
their women folk with them. Smartest among
the visitors is Mrs. Neil Lewis, wife of the
Hobart politician, a very chic woman, always
well-dressed and stylish. Ancient Adye Douglas,
game old dog, brought along his young wife, who
appeared like a daughter looking after her pa,
though certainly Mr. Douglas, despite his 80 years,
can look after himself.
“At Mayor M‘Each aril’s theatre-party at the
Princess on Thursday night appeared the Hon. de
Moleyns, back from sugar-caning in Bananaland
and much fatter than of yore. He still wears his
spec-pincers and wild, innocent, round - eyed,
ready-to-be-shocked expression. The old sassiety
K’rls cackled round him in the interval, and sent
ving messages to his haughty sister, the Coun-
tess, in Edinburgh.
“Lady Turner’s breast-work of jewels blazed
beside tne star-decked vest of Premier Braddon—
that st&oped, wrinkled - faced, aristocratic old
statesman —at the theatre-party. Sentimental
society girls say there is a romance and a blood-
curdling story in every one of the five deep, dark
wrinkles that form a musical scale on his forehead.
“ The Brassevs came ever so late to that theatri-
cal matinee. The lights were so low when the
vice-regal party tip toed in that they had enough
to do finding their way. Lady B’s costume was
heliotrope and black. Her small daughter wore
one of the quaint, tight frocks prevalent just now,
with close-fitting tucked sleeves, while her hat had
a blue satin crown and brim of pleated white
chiffon. Little Miss Brassey, rather pale and
button-nosed at the present moment, evidently
inherits her mother’s elegance of deportment
She sits bolt-upright in her chair, disdaining to
lean back, and carries her bits of arms exactly
according to maternal tactics. Other juveniles at
the matinee were two small Wesley-Halls, clothed
completely in tiny tucks and real lace, each con-
nected with a point-lace kerchief gathered in to
fit the wearer’s head and crested with Prince of
Wales plumes. With them came their new aunt-
stepmother, her three sisters, and a brother-in-
law. The bride—a tall, pale, plump damsel with
bright grey eyes—sported an ostrich boa, with
appliqued grey cloth cape and silver straw hat,
trimmed with grey and white chiffon.
“Mrs. Brough, with silver threads showing
among the pale-gold of her hair, worked hard
among the tea cups, in a simple white frock. Lily
Titheradge, a blue-eyed, breezy, unaffected damsel
off the boards, nibbled sweets with school girl
zest, and discussed dad’s approaching London
apjiearance in a congenial Pinero piece, though
only a curtain-raiser. Elizabeth Watson, in grey
tweed and green velvet belt, looked twice as
attractive off the stage as she did in the Bland
Holt contribution to tne programme—a scenefroin

“ Have society functions anything to do with
the inefficiency of the Melb. detective in tracking
crime? The Melb. detective is a person of one
idea—his whole intelligence is concentrated upon
safeguarding society against itself. At a recent
big private At Home, at which vice-royalty was
present, the detoctivos stationed at tho entrance
doors averred that of tho whole 800 guests there
was only one—a man—whom they didn’t know.
All tho same, they never saw the active thief,
who, getting mixed up with the going-away throng,
was merrily helping himself to ladies’ purses and
so on. They left him to the eare of a stout coach-
man, and although there was quite a scuffle
between the two, owing to the thief being made
to give up his prey, they never noticed it—they
weren’t looking for pickpockets in his direction.
“Elaborate invitations for the Osborne-Madden
wedding wore flying round the whole of last week;
and 1 Cloyne,’ the C. J’s town house, has been done
up from cellar to attic for the big festivity on Ist
March. Gov. Brassey is taking quite a fussy
interest in this particular nuptial demonstration.
Unlike the Yallange importation, who exhibits an
ever-increasing lankiness, the forthcoming bride-
groom falls in nicely with the Madden standard of
plumpness ; as the leading lady herself avers they
are ‘both fair, bbth fat, and both contented,’and
both cousinly good friends—all the elements of an
equal match.
“The procession of the Vice-royalties, aug-
mented by the whole Madden family, less ‘ papa ’
who is still away concluding matters with his
future son-in-law, was voted by unsooietv folk as
the best show-piece at the Empire Bazaar on
Saturday at Melb. Exhibition Building. A bride-
elect, in pink muslin and blue chiffon hat, being
led round by a live Governor, is of more interest
to the feminine mind than second-rate cosies and
photo.-frames. No one bought, but everyone
looked at the group as much as they wanted to.
Lady Brassey, still olinging to her iovely yellow
and white, looked regal at the head of the march.
“Whether all the Christians in Victoria were
rejoicing together during the Xmas holidays at
Mornington it is impossible to say for certain, but it
is a fact that the most fashionable hotel in Sorrento
was this summer entirely occupied by Jewish
families, with but one exception—and that family
was named Ham !

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Thus far, there has been no charge of
piety against Burgess.
“ Jacob’s Well.” Very well provided for
by his former chief, Reid.
Premier Braddon will be 70 in June. He
has been 20 years a resident of Tasmania.
Beauchamp will be the third earl appointed
Governor of N.S.W. Jersey and Belmore pre-
ceded him.
It is confidently stated that N.S.W.
Speaker Abbott will resign his office at the close
of the Parliamentary recess.
Beauchamp, at 27, is the youngest N.S. W.
Governor. Loftus, appointed at 62, was the
oldest at date of appointment.
The Bulletin has at last discovered some-
thing really serious concerning N.S.W. Minister
Oarruthers. He was born at Jamberoo.
Remarkable transformations in the life of
a N.S.W. small-celebrity—parson, M.P., manager
of a private detective office. To wit — pray, pay,
pry.
Ex-Premier Hugh Nelson 40 years ago
headed a movement to separate what is now
Queensland from N.S.W. Then he was a graziers’
mouthpiece.
Lord Beauchamp, the new N.S. Wales
Governor, must talk well; he was president of the
Oxford Union, an honor held by Gladstone in his
University days. Also, by our own B. R. Wise.
“Sammy Ballarat,” Anglican bishop of
the Garden City, has been fined for “furious
driving.” Samuel pleaded absence of mind or
something—probably imagined he had the A.N. A.
under the whip.
Dicky Speight Is back in Melbourne from
Westralia on his way to China. The jovial victim
of David Syme’s “reforms” has secured a good
appointment in connection with the Anglo-Chinese
Railway TYeaty, or something to that effect.
Dr. Higgins, R.C. Coadjutor Bishop of
Sydney is one of three whose names have been
submitted to the Pope for the vacant see of Meath,
Ireland. His name has been similarly submitted
for over half-a-dozen sees since his arrival in
Sydney.

Tom Doyle, publican and Mayor of
Kanowna, was once reported to have found a big
nugget which, however, like the since-famous
Uolden Sickle, was never seen by anybody else.
When a new-chum arrives on the “ Feather ” he
is generally “ kidded ” to go and ask Tom a few
questions about his “slug.” New-chum, as a
rule, has afterwards to patch up his innocent
countenance. Tom’s “ slug ” is absolutely mur-
derous.
Julian Salomons, who now takes the boots
of Saul Samuel as N.S.W. Agent-General, was a
colleague of Samuel’s in the Cowper Ministry of
29 years ago. Samuel was Treasurer and Salomons
Solicitor-General in that Ministry. They are the
only survivors of that Govt. ; Cowper, Forster.
Robertson, Sutherland, Manning, Egan, and Owen
having all passed away. Samuel and Salomons
to ff ether In the second Robertson Cabinet
Oi 1000.
The true story of Premier Reid’s Q.C.-
ship is said to be this : Shortly before last N.S.W.
general election, a very high member of the judi-
cial bench sent for the Same Old George, and “put
it straight ” to him whether he thought it would
be right for the Premier of N.S.W.-in case of
defeat, mind you—to go back to the junior Bar.
1 his appeal was not in vain, and the P.C. became
a Q.G., knowing that he had burned his legal
bridges behind him.
Most “ Crimean veterans ” who pass out
nowadays are toothless old battlers with worn
crutches. South Australia’s latest contribution to
the roll, though, was a young fellow of 63
George Amb who was in the Crimea with the
Sutherland Highlanders, under Colin Campbell
and later at the relief of Cawnpore. And the
thing that he seemed to remember with most
affection was that, within three years of ’listing
he stocid fourth man in the rear rank on the
right-hand side of the colors.”

Lady Florence Dixie, whoso approaching
visit to Australia is cabled about, was the indirect
cause of John Brown’s demise. That faithful
gillie caught his death o’ cold in going over to
make “kind inquiries” re the dreadful outrage
which proved such an awful hallucination.
The election in England of retiring Gov.
Buxton (S.A.) to the presidency of the Anti-
Slavery Society is a proxy compliment to grand-
father Buxton, who worked zealously and spent
copiously in the anti-slavery cause, and who
thereby won a niche in Westminster Abbey.
A prospector who found in Westralia an
alleged mine much puffed by the Forrests, but
which, so far, hasn’t come to anything, now
carries cards (gilt-edged, but not gilded out of
that mine), on which he is described as Mr. So-
and-so, “explorer.” Shades of Sturt and Giles!
Also he gives his address as “ Australia.”
The lately-deceased nonagenarian Rothery
built 60 years ago the house he died in, 10 miles
from Mandurama (N.S.W.). This house was
often threatened by bushrangers and was actually
stuck up by the Frank Gardiner gang, but the
tough old man in building it had judiciously
loopholed the structure, and he quickly routed his
foes.
The Argus, in a recent leader on names,
gushed much over the beautiful suggestiveness of
the name “ Thekla,” and then proceeded to point
out with what horrible and creepy cerements the
boot-trunk business had invested it. But why
not follow out the moral and similarly hold up the
young lady’s second name, Regina, to universal
detestation ?
Burgess is in Australasia a sinister name
it was that of the leader of the Maungatapu
assassins—Burgess, Levy, Kelly and Sullivan.
Burke, an alleged alias of the Gatton suspect,
recalls Burke and Hare, the English murderers,
the former of whom gave a verb to the language. ’
J. Butler, S.A. Minister of Agriculture,
who has been visiting Melb. and Sydney, disputes
with Treasurer Holder the honor of being the
thinnest man in S.A. A wag once placed Holder
and Butler together, and offered to wager that
even when combined they couldn’t throw a
shadow.
GOOD HEALTH WITHOUT DRUGS.
4.—Mothers and Children.
Mothers who would keep their children in good
health should give them, morning and evening,
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa made with hot milk. Deli-
cate men and women, who have weak lungs, to be
hale, robust, and healthy, should use Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa morning and evening, and all men who
have to be exposed to the bleak uncertainty of our
trying climate should fortify themselves before
they face their daily toil with Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-
Cocoa, and they can then brave the fury of the
elements with equanimity. The writer speaks
from personal experience and from observation of
beneficial effects on others. Tea opens the pores
and temporarily excites, coffee stimulates the
action of the heart, whilst Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa
gives strength, stamina, and builds up and
strengthens the lung-tissues. It is indeed a
wonderful food beverage. Nothing has ever been
discovered that can approach it in giving lightness
of heart, joy of life, fleetness of foot, and that
general feeling of comfort which only comes from
a full capacity to enjoy every pleasure, moral,
intellectual and physical.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.
Merit, and merit alone, is what we claim for
Dr. Tibbie’s Vi-Cocoa, and we are prepared to
send to any reader (a postcard will do) who names
The Bulletin a dainty sample tin of Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa.
' Writer was one day stand-
ing under the shop-verandah at
corner of Bourke and Elizabeth
streets, Melbourne. It was raini-
ng hard, and the water in the
gutters overflowed the iron foot-
bridges. A priest and a Presby-
terian minister were among those
who took shelter at this spot. A
woman with a baby sought to
cross the street. She hesitated to
plunge through the rushing
waters. The burly priest darted
across, caught the woman in his
arms, and landed her and the
kiddy, safe and dry-shod, on the
pavement. The Scotchman re-
garded this somewhat unusual
performance with grim approval.
“That was verra weeldone, sir,”
said he kindly to the priest.
“Ah well, d’ye see,” smilingly
answered the latter, “ the thrue
Church is ever foremost in good
works.” “ Hum ! ” said the
minister, “ maybe so. But ye’ll
obsairve, sir, that the Kirk o’
Scotland kens hoo to keep oot o’
the wet.”
Lord Augustus Loftus is
not only the oldest-retired Aus-
tralian Governor—he is far and
away the most important political
character, now that Grey is dead
—who has figured in the vice-
royalty of Australasia. Most
people have forgotten Loftus as
British ambassador at European
courts in thesixtiesand seventies.
Doubtless he was a good diplo-
matist of the plump, somnolent
kind. Lord Cromer—wasn’t it?
—said that the best ambassador
is one of whom the newspapers
have little to say. Loftus got
through without making a re-
markable object of himself.
Colonel Spalding, though
ousted from the Chief Magis-
tracy, does not propose to leave
Norfolk Island. He owns a plantation there, and is
building himself a house, where he hopes to live
down “ calumny ” and put money in his pooket.
Note that the family motto of young-man
Beauchamp (pronounced Beecham, please, and
see that the name is on every box) is, “ The lot is
fallen unto me in a fair ground.” Which blessed
motto is bound to be claimed as particularly ap-
plicable to N.S.W.
E. J. H. Knapp, about the windiest teeto-
tal and general bore on earth, is spoken of as
strongly in the running for the Governorship of
Norfolk Island. What the unhappy islanders
have done to deserve this The Bulletin doesn’t
know. However, “the greutest good for the
greatest number ”: better that they should be
tortured than that the blood of a big community
like Sydney should be turned to gall at the break-
fast-table by Knapp’s hideous prosings in the
daily papers on every subject under Heaven.
Keely, the most persistent impostor of the
century, is dead, and there are some men and
newspapers in America who think that he died
with the seoret of his alleged new motive force un-
revealed. His “motor” gave him a great lift —
the idea was strong enough to take down many
weak people for their money. Keely would never
submit his inventions to the public eye, and as
many people love darkness rather than light, and
only respect the unknown, he became notorious
and rich. It must have been a pupil of Keely’s—
he came from America—who took Lansell down
with “a perpetual-motion machine,” the details of
which fakement and taking-down would make
good reading.

Ine completely satisfactory ldentifica-
tion of Burgess was one of the biggest farces ever
P‘ a yed in the name of law—even in Lagland.
With the photo, of the suspect in full face and
profile published everywhere he would have been
as easily identified among a dozen prisoners as the
late Parkes—or Rassmussen the pill-man. The
identification may not have lacked certainty, but
as for its being “ satisfactory ’’—Rats ! Also,
there was a Toowoomba man named Weaver who
says he saw Burgess seven miles from Toowoomba
on Dec. 26—the day of the outrage—and talked to
him in broad daylight; and told the police. But
he wasn’t asked to identify; his identification was
at the wrong spot for the police theory.
Somebody is exhorting Melb. National
Gallery trustees to buy one of Tudor St. George
Tucker’s pictures ere that somewhat uneven artist
goes Ome. Tucker, indeed, has done some excel-
lent figure-painting but his high-flown front
names somehow or other make it hard to take him
seriously. There is at least one Melb. man who
cherishes a bitter scorn for those front names.
The snorter in question is a solicitor. He com-
plains that he lost a case through having to call
Tudor St. George T. as chief witness for plaintiff.
Tuckers manner of proclaiming his historical
titles gave a splendid opening to the counsel on
Vavasour, or Perkin Warbeck Fitzwilliam, until
the jury took the case for the side which called
him to be all a joke, and found for defendant.
Some Australian birthdays for February :
Editor Finlayson, of S.A. Register, 56 on 3rd;
Arthur Garner, 48, and ex-editor Haddon, of the
Argus, 60 on Bth ; General Downes, ex-Military
Commandant, S.A., 65, and B. R. Wise on 10th;
Judge Molesworth (Y.) 57 on 23rd ; Judge A.
Inglis Clark (Tas.) 51, and Julius Vogel (M.L.)
64 on 24th, and Premier Reid 54 on 25th. His
Yes-No attitude is symbolised by She fact that the
year of his age, 54, just reverses the year of his
birth, ’45.
“G.S.”: Lieut. Gaunt, who figures so
prominently in connection with the Samoan
trouble, is a native of Ballarat and a son of Vic.
County Court Judge Gaunt. When a boy of
between 13 and 14 he with his younger brother
had his imagination much inflamed by stories of
bushranging. His uncle, a well-known western
squatter, while on a visit gave the boys a sub-
stantial cheque for pocket-money. Whereupon
the lads suddenly disappeared. Days passed
without a trace of them, but at last the parents
got a wire to say they had shipped for Launces-
ton. When the boat arrived they were detained.
Both were armed with revolvers and knives.
“What’s your game, boys?” inquired the In-
spector of Police. _ “ Bushranging," was the
answer. Now, one is a Lieut, in the Navy and
the other a Lieut, in a British dragoon regiment
in which he first enlisted as a private.

Some Australian papers reprint from
London Daily Mail the weary chestnut about
ow l*te millionaire Tyson once went on a
steamer trip, and “ not knowing that the s.s. 00.
* iad made an alteration whereby passengers were
to be fed by the company for the same amount as
ac * hitherto been paid for the passage only, took
a h»m and a loaf of bread on board, and ate of his
humble fare during the trip. When they told
him on his return that he had spent money in
vain, Jimmy’s wrath at the loss was terrible to
behold.” The story—which is told of Grimaldi
in Dickens’ life of the famous clown, and was pro-
bably ancient then—goes on to say that Tyson
was very mean in the matter of clothes. Now, aa
a fact, the old man when in town always lived at
first-class places (such as Petty’s Hotel and the
Warrigal Club), and in Sydney always got his
clothes from a fashionable George-street tailor,
paying full price and never quarrelling about the
bill. Present writer once spoke to him about the
ham-and-steamer story. “Yes, that is an old
tale,” said the man with the blue-grey beard and
the steel-blue eye—“they said I took a ham-bone
into the berth and gnawed it like a dog. To tell
you the truth, mister, I never greatly liked boiled
ham. They should have made it corned beef.”

“B.B.B.” : In an Australian country-town
dwells a professor of “ magnetism,” who bas ob-
tained such influence over another resident as to
induce him to give up his business and take his
family to reside on a farm, where, according to
the prescribed regimen, the eldest son, who was a
smart, handsome fellow, now drinks olive-oil by
the quart, and has let his beard and hair grow till
he looks like a Domain-dosser. The female mem-
bers of the family haven’t taken kindly to an order
of things which ordains that their hair shall be as
short as the men’s is long; and an attempt by
paterfamilias to “shear” mater while asleep caused
the lady to make a bolt of it with her daughter.
One of the exercises prescribed for the father and
son is to ride the plough-horses round the paddock
every morning, at daylight, the horses minus sad-
dles and the riders clad only in shirts, after which
the pair adjourn to a sawpit for a “ sawdust-bath.”
Looks as if the biggest lunatics are outside the
asylums !

“ Scotty ” : The struggle for a crust. Ad.
appeared in Melb. dailies seeking manager for a
small sheep-station. Apply box 002, G.P.O. One
young man, testimonials in his pocket, shepherded
that box for two whole days. At last an old
gentleman came for the letters, and the candidate
buttonholed him on the spot. Got the billet, too,
and has been over a year in it. Saw him yester-
day. Leggings ; cummerbund ; helmet; “ squato-
ter’s-gap ’ ’ shave, and all complete.

“Terrick”: When I was on Oondamine
station the darkies assured me that when a dingo-
slut produces a litter of pups she leaves them in
charge of a mate and lights out to where she
knows there is a lot of pups nearly half-grown,
arranges a swap, and carries back one of the other
slut’s progeny, the mother following behind to
select one of the blind squeakers and return with
it. Mother of younger pups is afraid that while
she is away foraging they may crawl out and be
snapped up by eaglehawks or other enemies. The
elder pup has been taught to stay in, and conse-
quently when mamma tots ’em up at night she
finds that they are all there. I thought this was a
“ fairy,” but have verified the fact for myself.
* * » «
“Y.”: He ants. Whilst admitting all
“ Jeh ” (8., 24/12/’9B) has written to be correct, I
would go further. Once saw an ant wandering
far away from the nest, and wondered what he
was after. Watched for a while and saw four
others come up to him from opposite directions
and lead him home, lie had got “ bushed ”
possibly because he was minus antennae. Saw a
great fuss once among a community of these little
blackfellows, and presently a single ant was led
forth by three custodians, who “lost” him and
returned. Followed expellee to another colony,
and saw him also get the “ kick-out ” there. Evi-
dently he had been marked so that all respectable
ants might know him for a bad lot, and he had to
wander off cheerlessly on his own. Interesting to
watch a colony shifting camp when disturbed by
bush-fires. The young and aged are removed first
—then the eggs and “ tucker.”
* 41 * *
Harry Stockdale, re assertion of a Sydney
D.T. correspondent that “in our eucalyptus
forests no one should die of thirst, as all the
young trees contain, even in the driest seasons, a
supply of very drinkable water, which can be ob-
tained by cutting them into convenient lengths
and standing their small ends down, a vessel being
placed underneath to catch the water ” :
Evidently the first thing for a lost and nearly thirst-
dead man to do is to make sure that he is in a “ eucalyp-
tus forest”—then feel in his trousers pocket for the
station-axe ; and if he wears a waistcoat the vessel for
catching the water is sure to be in his watch-pocket. If
not, the only thing to do is to lie on his back and let the
blessed fluid drain in. Unfortunately for the value of
this suggestion eucalyptus forests are not often to the fore
when men (ret lost, and few men, lost in a dry country,
have had the forethought to take along an axe and a tin-
dish ; and even if they had, when thirst got properly
hold of them the articles would soon be dropped. Many
even have been so thoughtless as to get lost when there
are no forests, eucalyptus or otherwise. There certainly
is a mallee tree the roots of which yield water
when cut and stood on end, but it is not every
kind even of mallee that is so endowed. Also,
no man can possibly tell what another or even he
himself would do if “ lost.” When a lost man’s drinking-
water is done, the excitement and dread immediately
create a frightful thirst, and if the weather is hot and the
man keeps on trying to “ find ” himself he will not last
long, unless he finds shade, when he may linger two or
three days. On Christmas Day, 1882, on Cooper’s Creek,
Central Australia, two men called at Trappamerry
station (where I was) and got dinner. They filled their
billies and started again, but turned up a big blind creek
in mistake for the Cooper, and, having finished their
water, they came to where the creek forked. One went
up one bed, the other up the other, agreeing to make
back to the fork on finding water. One man got water
and, returning, waited till evening and then went in
search of his mate, whom he found within a few miles
dead—so rapid are sometimes the combined effects of
heat-thirst and anxiety.

“Jinda”: A sly-grog shanty on one of
the Western (N.S.W.) rivers has caused the death
of a constable, a senior ditto, and a sergeant of
police. Horrors, all of ’em ! Also, ia teamster —
who drew third horse in one of “ Tatt’s ” minor
sweeps—“did in” .£l9O, jumped a new waggon-
ette and pair of horses “over the bar,” and finished
up by dying in the fowlhouse, where he had been
thrown “ to make room for someone who could pay
his way ! ” It took three years to kill the police-
men—in instalments—but five days sufficed to lay
the bullock-puncher out.

“L.A.E.” : At a Morpeth (N.S.W.) pub.
was a “ pet ” ram so vicious that he had to be
locked up in the stables ; whenever he got out he
would charge anything that came in his way.
Once he got a fowl in his stall, and, as it could not
fly over the partition, he made pulp of it. Was
very partial to beer, and visitors used to “ shout ”
for him for the fun of it ; but, after once getting
too drunk to keep his legs, he could never be got
to look at “ the mocker ” again.
Strange to watch the
evolution of error in native
names ! Carratbool, near
Hay, was originally Carra-
dook native - companion.
Then a draftsman in com-
piling a new parish map
mis printed it Carradool ;
the railway came, and it
was altered into the present
Carrathool. Gillenbah and
Narmdera, were respectively
Gillelbah and Narandoora.
Can anyone say why G’naroo
should have the silent G’ ?
This is a native name, and,
when first written, must
have been spelt phonetically;
why was the G stuck in, un-
less it formed as near an ap-
proach to the native pro-
nunciation as the recorder
could compass ?

At a South Coast Agricultural Show Brown
(say) had two pens of cows, and other competitors,
having three pens each, chaffed him about not
being able to raise another. Then Brown knocked
up another pen of “any sort” of milkers, and
this took Ist prize.

* * • *
About 30 years ago an enterprising young
Cornstalk joined the police, and promotion began
to come so rapidly that he would
by now probably have been In-
spector-General had not someone
wanted to know how he was doing
it. A squatter had been losing
sheep, and Robert was set to
watcn the hut of a suspect. When
the man had gone out, the trooper
killed and skinned a sheep, hung
it in the humpy; and, when the
poor devil returned, followed him
in, and arrested him for sheep-
stealing. Result—a stripe. His
next essay was to fire a bullet
through his saddle, and assert that
it was done by a desperado with
whom he had had an encounter,
in which he displayed great valor.
The hole was right through the
saddle; but, when saddle was
afterwards put upon horse, it
struck the inspector as singular
that the animal escaped uninjured.
So singular was it that the police-
man was suspected, and fired out
of the force. Afterwards it tran-
spired about the sheep-killing,
and the imprisoned man, after
doing two years, was liberated —
but, of course, without compensa-
tion.

“ Wal.” : The paper-bark
tree (“ H.M.T.” 7/l/’99) is not
peculiar to W.A., being found
also in Victoria. Have seen it
growing along a tributary of
the Thompson River, Gippsland.
Bark easily removed from trunk, and
divides into thousands of fine sheets. Pro-
bably the tree exists in other parts of Australia,
most likely on river-flats where there is sufficient
moisture.

“Gobung”: While fishing in the Fitzroy
(Q.) recently I was using a defunct sea-mullet for
bait. Went down the river bank to find a better
spot to fish at, leaving the mullet to lead an un-
obtrusive existence on the grass till I returned.
When I got back, saw mullet disappearing down
an old grey mare’s throat, the tail being just dis-
cernible, sticking out of her mouth.
* * * ♦
Recently an army of cockroaches invaded
the office of the C.P.S. at Lodgetown, in the Big
Scrub, and devoured several pounds’ worth of
shilling legal stamps, leaving the _ “ tuppences ”
severely alone. The C.P.S. industriously applied
himself to the task of capturing the offenders;
and cockroaches are now found to be attached as
stamps to plaints and summonses. Apropos : to
clear out cockroaches —paint the walls of the in-
fested room with Paris green (poison, by the way).
Paint also the legs and bottom sides of kitchen
tables, &e.

“ J.W.H.H.” asks
Is the term “ goanna ” distinct from “ iguana,” or is it
merely a corruption thereof ? Is the “ iguana " proper
found in Australia?
“ Goanna '’ is a corruption of “ iguana,” but the
lizard to which the former term is applied is not
even of the same family as the true iguana, which
is not found in Australia.
* » * *
From an English paper : “ The Australian
savages eat the little green ant raw. They stamp
on an ant-hill till the ants come out and run up
their legs. These they then scrape off and put
them in their mouths.”

“Yar.”: Have never seen a red-bellied
black snake climb a tree, but once found one in
the spouting of a house 12ft. from the ground ; m
that it must have climbed up the wall Saw one
the other day having a great feed of brown beetles
that were falling helpless from a pepper-tree. He
had, apparently, the “oomehither ” on ’em.
Tom Fitzgerald believes firmly in the
genuineness of his “ Bunyip,” but he has ceased
to advertise it as sich. The bunyip of Australian
tradition is a terribly noisy myth, combining the
bulk of a hippopotamus with the soul of a croco-
dile, whereas Fitz.’s zoological exhibit is a gentle,
little, fur-coated conundrum terminating in a
naked periwinkular tail that seems to have been
pioked from a shell with a pin. It lives on flowers
and fruit, and when the expectant multitude was
asked to reoognise the long-lost Bunyip in the act
of holding a rosebud between its four little fingers
and a thumb, the multitude said “ Gam ! ” So
Tom Fitzgerald bowed to popular superstition
and took the Bunyip out of the advt. But his
faith remains unshaken. It seems to be built on
the fact that his unique treasure utters small,
plaintive cries in the night time only, and was
captured under bunyipian circumstances.

the feelings of a solitary, timorous new-clmrn
when several score of hi trued, bellowing brutes
rush at him with erect tails and lowered heads.
Of course he flees, and of course the jocular kmc
give chase with hilarious upheaval of heels and

Albert. Marsh, now “on the road ’’ (writes The Bulle-
tin s London correspondent), has married Harriet Vernon,
one of the massive, shapely pilasters of the nmsicalls.

Mnguie Moore is not without rival in her triumph
over the frailty of too much flesh. London papers
have been gushing over the success of Mrs. Kendal
in “The Elder Miss Blossom,’' at the St. James’s.
Eat, fair, and as much over 40 as the imagination
Of the boldest spectator cares to suggest, she takes
the house by storm in a piece of which the interest
is wholly sentimental, and in a character that is
dangerously near the ridiculous all the time. A
middl-ageil woman receives a proposal (by letter)
meant for her niece, gets ready for her marriage,
and discovers her mistake on the arrival of the bride-
groom. That is all it is, and such as it is, it is
played by a leading lady whose tendency to fleshi-
ness is tremendous, yet the audience weeps oyer
the picture of the woman’s desolation.
Dear Beu.BTiN. — Some time ago I wrote to con-
tradict the statement that John Coleman had copied
his “ Scarecrow Dance ’’ (as we in Australia call it)
from Billy Ashcroft. Will you allow me further to
put you right on the subject of James Milburn
and the suggestion that he was the originator of
Coleman's dance? First it was Ashcroft, and
then Milburn 1! You publish a letter from one-
legged comedian Jimmie Shields, as follows: “Rt
par 24/12/'9B. 1 saw Milburn at the Theatre Royal,
Yarrow-on-Tyne, 20 years ago, doing the ‘Scarecrow
Dance.’ Milburn is still alive. I saw him before I left
England on the 11 11 ’98.” I am struck by the fact that
Shiflds had no sooner landed in Australia than somebody
(who must have got Coleman “set”) induced him to
write the note you innocently printed. Shields hadn’t
seen Coleman dance. He simply told you that he saw
Milburn do the “ Scarecrow "20 years ago. Which scare-
crow, what scarecrow, and how scarecrow? I knew
Jimmy Milburn’s business 20 years ago, also 30 years ago,
and 16 years ago, and I can assure you he was not the in-
ventor of the dance which is known as the Scarecrow
Dance in Australia, and which is altogether foreign to
Milburn's style. Let me add that Milburn's style was
formed before one-legged comedian Shields was born, and
that 20 years ago Milburn had a daughter married to G.
H. Macdermott, the “ Jingo ” man.— Fred B. Norton.

A theatrical co. has been stranded in an Australian
capital for some time, and there have been ructions.
Hardly an evening has gone by without a strike either
among scene-shifters, performers, or orchestra. The
manager departed at the very start, and his address is
unknown. On one particular evening the orchestra took
Cossession of the stage, and declined to allow the show to
egin unless arrears were paid. As a compromise, they
took the “ gate ” for that and succeeding two nights—it
totalled £3 10s. all told. The latest strike was amongst
the men who attend to the front tor a few shillings a
night.
“Taney” : The female elephant, in circusability, is far
beyond the male. Fitzgerald's Bessie is a reminder of
Cooper and Bailey’s Titania. She died in Auckland,
through eating her keeper's monkey-jacket, in which was
a box of phosphorus matches. The show left next day
for South America, by the ship Golden Sea. It towed
poor Titania far out, and left her. Fishermen afterwards
reported on her as the sea-serpent.
A pretty actress once well-known in Australia has
a considerable temper. One night her best scene was
partly spoiled by the mistake of a young actor, and she
bit him very enthusiastically when the act was over.
Comedian and pantomimist Joe Tolano, of the bygone
Rignold days at Sydney Majesty’s, now- runs a little pub.
in a Sydney suburb. Still acting—for Boniface has to
seem a “ good sort" to everyone for 16 hours a day, and
a man must be an artist to do that successfully.
Dear Bulletin : You have been making several refer-
ences to lightning shorthand reporters of late, and I
would like to ask any of these champions if they under-
take to “take” Peggy Pryde for a quarter of an hour’s
turn, and transcribe* the notes correctly afterwards.
Peggy is willing to wager that she will break up nine-
tenths of the claimants to 200 and 220 words a minute in
about five sentences, and all the experts I know would
sooner take 10 Bartons and 15 Deakins than the sprightly-
little coster girl. So far I know of only one man in the
world who has ever taken Pegg.y Pryde, and that is her
husband. —McG.
Most visitors to “Robbery Under Arms ” expect to find
A. Dampier, as Starlight, charging wildly through the
piece on the fiery Rainbow ; and, when they' discover that
he doesn't mount a prad all through, they mostly go
away thinking that the veteran actor has never been
aboard a horse in his life. As a matter of fact, Dampier's
first start in the show business was as a circus-rider. A
quarter of a century ago he was a star bare-back per-
former in England, and it was not till a big smash per-
manently incapacitated him from riding that he ever
thought of “going on the stage.” One of Dampier’s
fellow circus-performers was the present English come-
dian, Terry, who was clown.

Temple, the and rubicund author of the late
lamented “American Girl” libretto, claims to be a
nephew of the Bishop of London of that ilk. He was for-
merly a curate under his uncle’s charge. From curate
Temple arose to the heights of commercial travelling for
hides, and thence to theatrioal managership. Another
link between Church and Stage.

A picnic for theatrical antiquities, many of them pen
sioners of the Distressed Actors’ Fund, was brought off
In the Dr.imatio Home (Melb.) grounds t'other day.
The venerable roysterers indulged themselves with an
entertainment after their little feed, as a matter of

ootogenarian went so far as to pipe a song expressive of

while.

THAT FASHIONABLE DRAWL.
“ I want to see some laice, please.”
“ Wrong shop, miss. Try the poulterer's, next door.”
College Morrison. The comedian began under the name
of Harry Morrison. At Ballarat, Manager Hoskings
advised him to call himself Power, because he excelled in
Irish comedy—though he was Scotch. His powers
seemed to me slim, but he made two hits. One was as
the Girl of the Period, in Sydney, the other as Jim Swain,
in “ Black Sheep,” with Chas. Mathews. He was acting
the Limerick Boy, in Calcutta, and sharply whispered for
Reuben to come on. Reuben was reclining against the
wing, and fell down dead.
Dear B. —Saw a nervous wreck of an actor playing a
parting-scene. Had my own girl by my side in the stalls
and my feelings towards her were a measure of the actor's
art. Result: Art bad. It wasn’t affection in him—l
knew that by my own girl—but mere disordered excite-
ment, proved physically besides by his voice getting
harder as the scene progressed. Now, Love does not
harden the voice, as I could prove—l have tried it on my
own girl. This is the reason why so many middle-aged
people despise the theatre. They have been there and
their lives furnish them with criteria of love-making that
actors cannot satisfy. Why should I pay to see a man
make love when I and my own girl do it better every
night?— The Stroller.

“ScotW.”: Amongst timber, n footman
can dodge any horseman. It is also known that a
good runner can beat a horseman, both starting
from a stand-still, 50yds. out and back ; but with
a handy propping beast of tin- old stock-horse
type the quad, can win. 1 once won (on foot) by
getting my friends to yell. The horse, an old
racer, thought it was a finish, and bolted. Re-
cently, at Narandera, a trooper ran down a young
man who failed to reach the trees. A few yards
more and we should have had a game of hide and-
seek, or rounders, or catch me if you can.

“Baract”: He causes of cattle-rushing
and ghosts (E.S.G., B. 24/12/’9B). I remember

stampeded by ducks flopping suddenly down into

The Bank of Victoria Limited.
TWELFTH HALF-YEARLY REPORT.
To be submitted to the Shareholders at the General Meeting to be held at Noon on TUESDAY, the 7th FEBRUARY,
1899.
The Board of Management submit their twelfth report to the shareholders of the bank, together with the balance-
sheet for the half-year ending 31st December, 1898, duly certified by the auditors in the forms prescribed by the Cony
panics Act :
Balance of undivided profit on 80th June, 1898 ... ... ... ... ... £22,649 10 IQ
Net profit for the past half-year ... ... ... ... ... ... ... 27,800 8 8
Together ... ... ... ... ... ... ... ... £60,449 19 1
Which the directors propose to apportion as follows
To dividend on preference shares, at 5 per cent, per annum ... ... £10,419 0 0
To reserve fund, making it £70,000 ... ... ... ... ... 20,(XX) 0 0
To balance carried forward to next half-year... ... ... ... 20,030 19 1
£60,449 19 1
An instalment of one-third of all remaining deferred deposits will be paid in advanoe on the 13th March, and the
board has in view to discharge the balance of these receipts as quickly as possible.
The dividend on preference shares will be payable in Melbourne on and after the Bth February, and at the
branches and in London on receipt of advices.
The transfer-books of the company will be closed from the 26th inst. to the 7th February, both days inclusive.
EDWARD MILLER, Chairman.
J. D. LAW, General Manager.

A Man Can’t Work
WHEN HE IS
SUFFERING WITH LIVER DISEASE.
HE CAN’T EAT, SLEEP; CAN’T GET
COMFORT ANY WAY BUT ONE—WAR-
NER’S SAFE CURE WILL CURE HIM ;
GIVE RELIEF AT ONCE.
Just a little pain may so distract a man’s mind
that it will cost him ten pounds. Life is a battle.
To succeed one needs all his energies: and a clear
brain. Liver disease, torpidity or weakness,
makes a man irritable, prevents sleep, causes head-
ache, flatulency, insomnia, and a tired, run-down-
out-of-sorts condition.
The treatment is simple, readily obtainable, safe
and sure. Mr. Robt. Hoystead, Bellair’s Hotel,
Wagga Wagga, says : “For years I was a terrible
sufferer with my liver, and tried several medical
men at considerable expense without any good
resulting. Therefore I determined to try War-
ner’s Safe Cure. Before long I found a change for
the better, and by persevering with it' was com-
pletely cured. Now lam never done recommend-
ing it to my friends.”

POINTICAL
POINTS
Tasmanian Federalist’s name of the
Federal capital—Unitia. It would surely be nick-
named Sneezeville.
It is quite a mistake to imagine that the
president of Sydney Water and Sewerage Board
should be a civil engineer. To succeed, it is
necessary to be a political engineer.
The freak of Q. elections is the appearance
of mummer Walter Bentley Begg as a Labor
candidate. Begg’s sudden affection for the horny-
handed and enthusiasm for political and social
reform smells—Bentleyish.
Premier Reid doesn’t want Federation.
Now, he has only to work his monkey-tricks to
remain boss in his own country. In a Federal
Parliament he would have to address, as it were,
not a vulgar jury, but a bench of Judges.
Edward Braddon (Tas.) is now Premier,
Treasurer, Postmaster-General, Minister of Educa-
tion, and Minister of Agriculture ; and if he would
consent to be Chief Justice, Bishop, Governor, and
a few other little things, it might be managed,
and would be a great saving.
HARMONY. —On a Collins-street tram.
The preponderance of Church of England
adherents over the adherents of other churches in
N.S.W. is, to some extent, accounted for by the
fact that Gov. Macquarie’s order that all orphans
were to be brought up C.E. was observed to the
letter for over a generation.
Several S.A. M’sP. are quaking in their
shoes as to which Assembly constituency ex-Agent-
General Playford will contest next April.
“ Honest Tom ” is a certainty for any of the pro-
ducing districts, several of which are now repre-
sented by violent anti-Kingstonians.
Both Sleath and Willis voted against the
Suffield Royal Commission motion, notwith-
standing that Sleath got one to clear his character
in the Bennett case, and Willis wanted one to
clear himself of the dummying charges. Let
their constituents make a note of it !
The long-looked-for Q. democratic weekly
is about to arrive. Early in Feb. will appear the
first issue of Progress —“a journal devoted to the
advancement and prosperity of Queensland as a
colony, and as a state of the Australian Common-
wealth.” The venture will be owned and edited
by Mr. Drake, an experienced and able journalist.
A new political party is on the warpath in
Victoria in view of vague rumors of an early
dissolution. It is called the Workers’ Political
Party, has the usual 1001 planks of every ephem-
eral organisation of political Labor, and its
manifesto is endorsed by Hancock, Maloney,
Prendergast, Stephen Barker, Toomey (from
N.S.W.) and Temple, of the Shearers’ Union.
Things political are lively up North.
Labor Central Executive—president, Glassey—has
been busy endorsing candidates. For Clermont
editor Lesina was chosen, although sitting Labor-
member Cross submitted his name for approval.
Cross is now' running “on his own,” backed by a
flattering letter from Glassey. What is the
leader’s position in this case ? As president of the
Executive he endorses Lesina’s nomination ; as
Tom Glassey, he barracks for Cross. The dual
positions are untenable, and must lead to party
upheaval. If Glassey intends to ignore the deci-
sions of the L.G.E. he should cut himself adrift.
If the Jap is the curse of the pearling
business, the Chow is the curse of the other Q.
fishing industries. Hoo-ki-lah fishes seven days a
week ; uses a small net —often of illegal mesh ;
and, last but not least, never spares a small fish.
Let him once tackle a stream and the European
fisherman can make tracks as soon as he likes, for
John will exhaust the spot, sell all the fish he can,
and live on shark and miscellaneous refuse.
Premier Kingston will come into sharp
conflict with S. A. Labor-members when Parliament
meets again, on Feb. 28, if he persists in confining
its discussions solely to Federation. They want
to talk at large on the unemployed question, etc.,
while, as they nearly all represent city districts,
they will not be so anxious to get away electioneer-
ing as their provincial colleagues will be. The
elections are likely to be postponed from April to
June.
S. A. Labor-party has decided to run a labor
candidate against Works Minister Jenkins at the
approaching Assembly elections. A similar
attempt was made to oust him at the ’96 election,
and Democrats were so disgusted at the labor move
that they put Jenkins, who has been a consistent
Liberal since he first entered Parliament, 12 years
ago, at the head of the poll. Incomprehensible as
it may seem, there is every probability of a labor
man opposing Premier Kingston in West Ade-
laide !
Whispered—only behind thick walls and
in desolate places—that Q. Minister Chataway
may soon secede from the Dickson Cabinet.
Alleged that Chataway is the only man of fine
feeling and gentlemanly instinct (!) in the Q. Govt.,
and that his conscience revolts at the dirty
trickery of the Ministry to secure a continuance
of perks. The rumor even attributes to Chataway
a desire to fight Dickson in the latter’s electorate
if Labor and Opposition will leave the course
clear.
Cairns, the nondescript city of N.Q., is
howling for a fresh M.L.A. Hoolan, the nonde-
script politician, erstwhile Laborist, is apparently
touting for a fresh seat—with the result that
Cairns has asked the gyrator to come forward and
represent her in her full, smellful, alien dignity.
John will probably consent. Little Lissner
allowed Hoolan to get the credit of pushing for-
ward the Cairns-Etheridge railway claims, with
the result that Lissner has practically got his walk-
ing ticket.
Premier Dickson is cautiously waiting to
see how the Federation cat jumps in Bananaland.
Is a Federationist, of course (they all are) •
declines, however, to express himself definitely on
the Commonwealth Bill. Is just as hazy and un-
satisfactory on the question as were Byrnes and
Nelson and just as slippery. The Q. elections
are just at hand but the Federation question is one
you hardly ever hear mentioned. Roads, bridges
railways to Dead Dog, are the order of the day and
everything else may go hang.
Pastor Bevan says he thinks Premier Reid
is influenced by the true Federal spirit. All the
same, he considers it has been impossible for Yes-
No to avoid being tempted to look upon Federa-
tion from the party standpoint. But he hopes
and trusts the Chameleon will at last stick to one
color—and that the right one. But, altogether, the
curly soul-saver fears that popular interest in
I ederation has thinned out, and - er—somehow—-
ah—hum—just so. Which is just about as satis-
factory as an average sermon.
Apropos of the fact
that N.S.W. is fostering
its sugar-cane with duties
while Victoria is trying to
create a beet-sugar indus-
try, it is notable that beet
has now left cane far be-
hind as a means of sugar-
production. The world’s
consumption of beet-sugar
now exceeds that of cane
by fully 2,000,000 tons a
year. N.S. W. produces
what experts have declared
to be the best beet in the
world for sugar - making
purposes, and it was pro-
posed a while ago that some
one should go to England
and cry pitifully there for
the money to start a new
sugar industry. The sub-
ject, however, seems to have
dropped. Cane - growing
began in the days when
N.S. W. hadn’t yet got into
the habit of always asking
Bull to lend the money be-
fore starting anything, else
it would probably have
dropped too.
The loudest cry of
S.A. Leg. Council against
Kingston’s proposed refer-
endum re Council reform
was that “No Ministry, in
any English-speaking com-
munity, has ever so acted
before.” Which is a jibe
of much smaller tonnage
than it looks. There are
about 15 English-speaking
communities that have had
Ministries for periods, rang-
ing from a good many years
to a few centuries, and it
would take many years to
wade through all their
dusty, forgotten records
and see if any of the Minis-
tries ever acted before as
Kingston is doing. The
statement is probably a lie;
anyhow, it is a lie for all the S.A. Tories know
to the contrary. And if it is true it means
nothing. All progress consists in somebody doing
something that wasn’t done before.
So far, as the signs go, Victoria has no in-
surmountable objection to the Federal amend-
ments proposed by N.S.W. Parliament, one of
those relating to the Federal capital only ex-
cepted. It seems agreeable to have a Federal city
planted on Federal territory somewhere within
N.S.W., Riverina preferred, but it isn’t taking
any Federal city at Sydney. Also, it is solid on
the theory that the capital should be on Federal
territory, sa that the extra revenue which will be
created by the gathering of population round the
Federal centre shall be the joint property of the
provinces, and not be all snavelled by one of them.
A slab of Fysh’s banquet-speech :
The people of these colonies now numbered four mil-
lions, and it was a strange coincidence that when
the United States of America declared their inde-
pendence they numbered about four millions.
And he hoped that a generation to come
would be able to speak of another
greater consummation still—that the
example set by America would be fol-
lowed by Australia. There was
room for an immense people
in these lands. In America
in about 150 years, they had
built up 70 millions of peo-
ple, and so in Australia in
another 150 years there
would be scores of millions
of people, and who would,
perhaps, bear in mind the
efforts of her public men
who to-day were making a
commencement of great
things by striving to bring
about Federation. (Ap-
plause.) However, in using
the words he had he hoped
he would not be taken to
have spoken treason against
the work he had now under-
taken. He believed that Fed-
eration would soon be ac-
complished, and that
might be a reason why
he should make a spee-
dier return to Tasmania
than perhaps he otherwise
would. (Hear, hear.)
That P. O. Fysh, the boss Im-
perial barracker in the Southern
hemisphere should use such words
—even at a banquet—-has filled
the souls of the “Gorsave”
folks of Tasmania with a
vague, indefinite dread of
they know not what. A. I.
Clark, in his pre-Judge days,
might have whipped up the re-
publican spirit without causing
surprise; but for P. O. Fysh,
K.C.M.G., to say such things is simply
Awful.
Premier Dickson’s cold and grumpy atti-
tude towards Federation in his Melb. speech
was a surprise to many people. Queensland has
apparently so much to gain by getting its sugar
admitted free to the other provinces when inter-
provincial tariffs are abolished that more enthu-
siasm was expected from Oily James. But, on
the other hand, Federation would put an end to
the Q. duty on N.S.W. coal, and Queensland has
great coal deposits to develope when it gets rested.
And Brisbane has an idea about Federation being
a deep, dark swindle for stealing its trade away to
Sydney, just as the Sydney provincial crowd
reckon it a bottomless scheme for snatching the
Sydney trade away to Melbourne.
The old Glassey-Powers duel looks as if it
might be fought out again in Bundaberg. Against
any other opponent Glassey is quite safe —but
Powers is hugely popular in Sugar-town. The
Govt, party, recognising the hopelessness of put-
ting in any other local nominee, is prepared to
support Powers’candidature, and has requisitioned
him accordingly. If Powers should stand, the
recent agreement between Opposition and Labor
as to contesting of seats will be, of course, null
and void. It appears, however, as if the local
organisations are going to set aforesaid agreement
at defiance ; in which case Drakeite and Laborite
will do the Kilkenny-cat trick, and the Govt, can-
didate grin happily at the spectacle.
THE IDENTICAL SHEEP-DIP.
QUERIST (to German) : “ And what brand of Baptist do you belong
to —the Sprinklers , Hardshells, or Dippers? ”
THE DEUTSCHER : “ Oh, ve believe in de dip right under. Look at
de vord ‘babtise ’ — bab, down; tise, ged oop again.”
The Age, in a carefully-figured-out esti-
mate, reckons that the .£202,000 which it is pro-
posed to expend on a new Melb. railway-station
will yield 6 per cent, clear profit per annum. And
it is proposed, as usual, to borrow the money.
Why not pay it out of revenue? Isn’t a 6 per
cent, investment good enough for Victoria to put
its own money into?
Several S.A. Labor-leaders who have not
had a chance at Parliamentary life say openly
that they think some of the present members, who
have held seats for six years, should retire and
give them a show. “ Wbat’s the good of putting
us up to fight districts we can’t win while they
stick to the pocket-boroughs ? ” However, there
are very few of the present men who could be
bettered by the available talent of the party.
THE GROUND OF LOVE.
“He said he loved the ground I stood on.”
“Were you on your own property at the time? ”
Adelaide Observer, which lifts up its
voice in the interests of Proputty, declares that
the Kingston Ministry, when it proposed taking a
popular vote on the question of council-reform,
seized upon “a craven method of effecting its
object. Then it moans this sad, unreasoning
moan :
Granting for argument’s sake that the electors are in
favor of the measure lately before Parliament for the re-
form of the Council, the new Assembly will be returned
pledged thereto.
In short, if the Kingston Govt, refers the matter
to the people at a general election, mixed up with
all the other current issues so that no absolutely
certain verdict can be obtained, it isn’t craven.
And if it refers it separately so as to get an un-
mistakeable verdict it is. Which makes the
matter very lucid indeed.
Apropos the right claimed by Premier
Seddon to exclude aliens from Maoriland : It was
long ago decided by the British Privy Council, in
the case of Chinaman Ah Toy against the Yio.
Govt., that there is an absolute right to prevent
the ingress of foreigners, and Ah Toy didn’t get
in. A similar right is exercised in S.A., and the
N.S.W. Govt, excluded pertain aliens very sud-
denly only the other day when it refused to let a
band of Romany wanderers across the Murray.
The Austrian Govt, not only exercises the right
to exclude foreigners, but it sometimes decides,
after the foreigner is in, that he is undesirable,
and fires him and his other collar and shirt across
the border at very short notice. The Seddon
Govt, had precedent enough for its action—if the
precedents had been collected in a heap the top-
most one would have been visible only through a
telescope.
The man who selected Queensland’s dele-
gates to the Federal Council was indeed a humor-
ist. What a combination ! Dickson is the least-
inspired man on earth, whose greatest surprise in
life was finding himself Premier; ex - Lands
Minister Barlow—whose administration was so
foolish and blundersome that he simply had to re-
tire to save annihilation—is distinguished mainly
for his unpleasant countenance, hungry voice, and
blind and swaggering hatred of Labor politicians ;
Glassey is Barlow’s red rag and Labor’s local god,
also formal assailant of the Q.N.B. ex-directors ;
Morehead is a Q.N.B. ex-director who, in his full-
blooded days, once publicly slapped Glassey in the
eye, and whose after-dinner amusement used to
consist of mimicking, ridiculing and laughing at
the dull-witted, platitudinous, and funereal L)ick-
son. The other delegate, Foxton, is probably
present in the capacity of valet to his chief.

VITADATIO
THE GREAT HERBAL REMEDY.
Registered.
If you have suffered for 20 years VITADATIO will cure
you, and don’t forget it. VITADATIO will cure you
(without operation), even when the eminent doctors of
London, Melbourne, or any other part of the world have
failed ; be it Bright’s Disease, Cancer, Hydatids, Con-
sumption, Piles, Gall Stones, Liver or Kidney Complaint,
Gravel, Stricture, Eczema, Rheumatism, Gout, Sciatica,

TESTIMONIAL.
375 Bay St., Port Melbourne,
„ October 3rd, IS9B.
Mr. W. Webber,
Proprietor of “ Vitadatio.”
Dear Sir,—lt is with great pleasure that I write you
few lines to tell you what your great remedy,
Vitadatio,” has done for me. At the beginning of my
illness I was living in America, and was attending a ball
to see the “ New Year in,” in 1897. On my way home I
took a very severe cold, through going from the heated
ballroom out into the cold air (the snow being two or three
teet deep). I was laid up for some considerable time, and
1
BEFORE.
'
ill
i
AFTER.
treated by some of the most eminent physicians in
America, but all to no advantage. Hemorrhage of the
lung set in, and my doctors said that I had “ gone into
Consumption,” and that there was no cure for me, and
advised me to return to my native land, Australia. So
on the 4th June, 1897, I arrived back in Melbourne. I
then consulted many doctors, and tried all sorts of medi-
cines, but got no relief, so naturally began to despair.
My husband heard from our friend, Mr. H. M'Kenzie,
sewer contractor at South Yarra, how his son had been
cured of Consumption by the use of “ Vitadatio.” Mr.
M‘Kenzie urged my husband to give me a course of the
remedy, which he did ; and right glad I am that he did,
as the hemorrhages have ceased, and also the terrible
racking cough that used to keep me awake all night. I
can now attend to my duties, and can truly say that
“Vitadatio” has cured me. For the sake of other
sufferers who may be in the same plight as I was, I give
you full permission to use this letter as you may think
fit. We are well known in these colonies, as my husband
was in the furrier business for years.
I remain, Yours truly,
F. A. CLARK.
