SPORTING NOTIONS.
It is generally reckoned that the N.S.W. win
over Victoria about represents the relative strength of
the sides, the conditions of wicket and weather, even all
through, tending to the result—although the follow-on
must have slightly favored Victoria, which retained the
wickets from 3 o’clock Friday till 4 o’clock Monday. Fol-
lowing were the figuresN.S.W., Ist innings, 442; Vic.,
Ist, 215 ; 2nd, 396 ; N.S.W., 2nd, 170 for 2 wickets. The
leading scores were—N.S.W. Ist innings: Noble, 100;
Iredale, 98; Dorman, 60; 2nd innings: Mackenzie (not
out), 62. Vic., Ist innings : Giller, 87 ; Stuckey, 28 ; 2nd
innings : Graham, 124 ; Worrall, 68; Laver and Giller, 55
each.
• * • •
Sturdy Jack Worrall successfully “knocked”
the delusion prevalent in Sydney that Pye could bowl
him whenever he liked. John had evidently heard of
this belief, for when Pye was put on for his especial
benefit in Vic.’s second innings, he spat on his hands and
smacked him to the fence so often that the cricketing
apothecary was taken away and carefully kept off until
Worrall had been got rid of. Worrall is going to be the
hitter of next. Australian Eleven, and will be a worthy
successor to Lyons whenever he gets going on the srnail
’Ome grounds.
The most unpopular cricketer, if he will cease
pottering and begin to hit, can soon bang his way into
the hearts of the average crowd. A couple of years ago
Worrall couldn’t appear on Sydney ground without being
“ boo-hoo-d ” owing to his alleged remark that Corn-
stalks could be done without in an Australian XI. But
since he began to slog, no one is more popular with Syd-
neyites. Sydney is full of good, careful batsmen who
make a lot of runs in a long time every year and still are
never picked in first-class matches, whilst if a hitter
ever comes along, even if he only makes a score occasion-
ally, he is soon given a chance of distinguishing himself.
Vast Alick Mackenzie would have been picked for’Ome
long ago had he only tried to bat in “ big ” as he does in
club cricket.
Graham’s century in Victoria’s second innings
was up to his best of a few years back and will probably
just get him a trip ’Ome. He started slowly, for the
bowling was good, but after a while he seemed to tire of
steady play and stepped out in his good old style—and
then the spectators saw brilliant strokes—he went from
60 to the hundred in 20 min. “His old cheeky game
again,” said one of the Vic.’s, as “Brummy” coolly
walked up the pit'-h and pulled one of M‘K‘ibbin’s off
balls right round to leg. But he tried it too often-
missing one, Kelly stumped him just on time.
* • * *
Pye, coining out to bat on Anniversary Day in a
heavy sweater, caused some amusement amongst the
sweltering thousands who were being baked at a shilling a
head on hill and terrace. But sweaters are English, quite
English, you know.
* * » *
Newell’s hesitancy, after Crockett’s decision,
was noticed by some of the dailies. To the writer, it
seemed a bit stagey—an attempt to lessen the bitterness
of his early dismissal.
A little excitement was aroused when “Noss”
Iredale and “ Mary Ann ” Noble were running for the
first century, in the Vic.-N.S.W. match. “Noss” is the
more popular, though neither approaches Howell in public
esteem. “No frill” wins every time on Australian
grounds, and, as a striking proof, one need only mention
the popularity of Tommy Horan and Sydney’s own some-
what erratic Sammy Jones.
Why does Gregory never change ends with his
bowlers? All the other interprovincial skippers do so,
and frequently with good results, but Gregory keeps his
pegging away from the same old ends, wickets or no
wickets.
Baimerman no-balled M'Leod, and he was
transferred to the other end. Some people who saw all
the match are wondering just how far Crockett would
allow a howler to go up the pitch before he similarly
penalised him. Also, several of the N.S.W. team have
expressed themselves very opehly and strongly on some
of this umpire’s decisions.
* * • *
N.S. Wales’s two wickets for 176 in the fourth
innings of a match (Gregory scoring 44 and Iredale and
Mackenzie 49 and 62 respectively, both not out) was a
fine performance.
* * * •
As Howell holds the bowling average for this
season’s Sheffield matches, he is a pretty good certainty
for ’Ome.
* * • •
When Iredale was batting, Stuckey returned a
ball which struck “ Noss ” a blow on the neck that would
have sent to sleep half the alleged pugilists. Worrall
offered Iredale time to recover, but the batsman came to
time, and batted in fine form afterwards.
* * * *
Young Trumper’s “ cake is dough ” as regards
the English trip, but he certainly had bad luck in his only
innings in the last match. He chopped a ball down oh
Johns’ foot behind the sticks, and it bounced into Wor-
rall’s hands.
• • • •
Newell’s luck was out in both innings, but more
particularly in the second, when he sacrificed himself to
save Gregory and was run out off a “ no ball.”
* * * *
What price the extra men for England in Ire-
land, Howell, M'Kibbin and Giller—although Alex.
M’Kenzie, who has recovered his health, should come
under consideration. Mac holds the batting average
against Stoddart’s team with 80.25—composed of 80, 59,
130 and 52, made in double-quick time, the very thing to
suit English wickets and three-day matches.
* * * *
Giller seems a hard one to get out. He has no
strokes, but can keep the ball down and off his wicket;
87 and 55 were two good scores under the circumstances
of the match. He bowls a bit, too, and completely
googled Harry Donnan out when well set.
* * * *
Adelaide papers have been deluged with letters
urging Geo. Giffen’s claim to inclusion in the Australian
team. Various remedies are suggested, one being that
S.A. should decline to play the other provinces any more
if the great man is excluded, but the boldest suggestion
is thatGiffen should lead a rival Australian eleven through
England, taking the position of captain and manager him-
self. This would just suit George right down to the
ground, but a financier is needed.
• * * *
Alleged that Howell, if he goes to England, will
stay there, as Phillips guarantees the Sydney bowler a
billet with the progressive Middlesex club.
* * • •
Vanitas, the Sydney horse that has been win-
ning big races (including the Viceroy’s Cup at Calcutta)
in the East, is now owned by a Chinaman, who races
under the fraudulent alias of “Mr. Mackie.” The expor-
tation of Australian thoroughbreds is more than ever a
matter of sad regret. Fancy Vanitas carrying the colors
of Tan Poo Liat, otherwise “Mackie” 1
* * * *
A strange coincidence that those connected
with the place-getters in last Metrop. Handicap have had
no luck since. Houseman, who rode the winner, Cravat,
is dead ; young Flanagan, jockey of the second, Robin
Hood, was butchered at Caulfield, whilst Richard Craven,
who owned the other place-getter, Woodlark, has also
gone over.
* * * *
Sydney 14.1 pony, Red Rose, some months ago
disqualified for inconsistency, is now engaged in putting
up a record that should stand long. Since returning to
the track she has started 9 times—6 wins, 1 second, and 2
thirds. Five wins consecutive.
* * * *
Blair put up a big performance by carrying
13st. 21b. in the Corinthian Plate, at Randwick, on Satur-
day, winning in a canter. To carry the average amateur
and win is a big feat at any time, but when said amateur
and his gear total nearly 2cwt. it goes close to being re-
markable. With only 8.11 up, Blair, if fit, should have a
lot to say in the settlement of Newmarket Handicap.
* * * *
Kirry’s record (six furlongs) in the Challenge
Stakes, at Randwick, was a very genuine go,and all hough
he only knocked off Precaution’s best, the two per-
formances cannot be compared, as Kirry’s pace was alone
responsible for his record, whilst the flying mare had a
gale of wind to help her along. Proudfoot, who finished
second, was wound-up for this race, and the crowd behind
him went for a big win, and had bad luck in bumping
against a record-breaker. The unlucky horse of the race,
however, was Eastern King, who dead-heated for third ;
had he got a decent run it is hard to say how much would
have come off the record. Left at the post with Hop-
scotch, he had to work a rough passage through the big
field, but he ran into a place, and was galloping faster
than anything at the finish—which, with 9.3 up, is a big
performance in a record-run sprint. Eastern King is by
Sunrise, whose stock are steadily making a name for him
on both Vic. and N.S.W. courses.
* • • •
Alf Joseph (salute!) is back from Maoriland
looking all the better for his trip. When he left Mel-
bourne last year, fleshy Alfred felt almost persuaded that
his end was near, and his parting drinks with his pals
were awfully pathetic—full of gin and trouble, as Peggy
Pryde would remark. “ You may never see me again,”
said Alf to one or other. Then he raised his glass to his
pallid lips and drank to his dear departing self in solemn
silence. That’s the worst of getting into a large way of
business as a bookmaker. Your big banking account and
expensive knowledge of dead ’uns makes you afraid of
being cut down like a flower at any minute.
* * i *
Jockey Durston was scarcely laid under the turf
when the crack lightweight, D. Cook, received terrible,
probably fatal, injuries in the smash-up at Randwick on
Saturday, when five horses played sacks-on-the-mill under
the very nose of the grandstand. At the inquest on
Somerville, killed upon Sydney Cricket Ground while
cycle-racing, the jury did not go deeply into the question
of the safety of the track, nor, probably, will they dive
deep to discover, should Cook succumb, whether even the
lordly Randwick course can accommodate with safety 34
horses in a race. Yet a whole regiment of police is
present to see that no nose-blood is drawn at a spar.
• • • •
Wait-a-Bit, Cocos and Woodlark have boss
chances for the Australian Cup on paper.
* * * *
Geo. Gray has turned his chair round to see if
he can improve his luck. That is to say, he has removed
George Frederick and other costly gee-gees from Red-
fearn’s stable to the care of Harry Bellamy, who learned
his business as trainer in the employ of the Millers. A
sanguine owner like Gray couldn’t have a better adviser
than the typically cool Bellamy.
• * • •
W. R. Wilson states that Symmetry, in the
opinion of the stable, is only a speedy sprinter. And echo
answers—” Why, O why was the mare entered for the 2J-
mile Australian Cup ? ”
* * * *
Touching the victory of Homespun in the
Hunters’ Flat Race at Williamstown, last Thursday, it
was stated in Melb. Age that Mr. Albert Miller, not hav-
ing been able to give the hollow-backed gelding a satis-
factory trial, didn’t put a sixpence on. And yet this
scribe fancies that Mr. M., who was not in evidence at
the time, won £l5O at the risk of a modest “ tenner.”
True, he didn’t fancy Homespun, which accounted for
the small books laying as much as 15 to 1, but if he had
been sweet on Homespun he would have needed to out-
lay £3O to win the same amount. Snapshot, winner of
the Anniversary Stakes at the same meeting, is said to
have gone so badly in his work beforehand that the
owner, or owners, were astonished to see him get home,
although he started at B’s—in the newspaper reports.
* * * •
Mr. “ Bill” Long won two races at Randwick on
Saturday. Parthian, victor in the Nursery Handicap is
by Mr. Long’s unbeaten champion, Grand Flaneur, from
Archeress, and may later on revive some of the stable
glories. The other win was in the High Weight Handicap,
when Beachcomber beat the speedy Gozo mare, Dozon, in
1.14 J.
• * • *
Randwick, as usual, was not happy in its
solitary selection of The Barber as an example of incon-
sistent running. On Thursday The Barber ran nowhere
in the Hurdles and on Saturday won the second Hurdle
Race hands down. But why The Barber should have
been picked upon as the only culprit is one of those
things the S.T.C. alone can understand.
* * * *
There were 12 entries for the All comers’ Scull-
ing Handicap, at the Anniversary (Sydney) Regatta, for a
£5 ss. prize, which looks as if the old sport only wants a
little encouragement to make it boom again. There were
some fine specimens of men amongst them, the winner
turning up in W. Fisher, lOsec. behind. All the com-
petitors pulled in stump-outriggers, the style of craft
rowed by the “ colonials ” in England, and about the
merits of which the English waterman have grave
doubts. But then the Britishers don’t take kindly to the
Australian starting-gate, which started 30-odd horses at
Randwick on Saturday in an unbroken line. “ Colonials ”
have proved the stump-outrigger to be as great a success
as their horse-barrier. And the Englishers will find it
out later on.
The new course for the rowing races, from
Bradley’s to the High-street wharf, was an improvement,
but the quality of the amateur rowing showed sad
decadence. The Senior Fours, erstwhile the rowing
event of the year, fell through for want of entries. North
Shore R.C. won the Maiden Fours, and Balmain R.C.
beat two other crews in the Senior Fours. The contest
to the bitter end which used to take place 20 odd years
ago between the Sydney and Mercantile R.C.’s at the
premier regatta of the year is a thing of the past.
The sailing races of Sydney Anniversary
Regatta were of an interprovincial nature, as no less than
13 boats hailing from Brisbane (which did so well against
Sydney boats last year) were completely beaten off, failing
to secure a place in either the 22ft. or 18ft. classes. Effie,
with “Nick ” Johnson at the tiller, proved her title to the
championship of the 22-footers by completely outsailing
her field, and Federal, Stella and Australian, all of
Sydney, finished in the order named for the 18-footers.
The trueness of this sailing was proved on Saturday last
in the 18ft. challenge race in connection with the inter-
provincial carnival, when Federal and Stella again ran first
and second.
* * * *
The biggest item of the Sydney Sailing Carnival
was sailed last Monday afternoon, when the three Sydney
22-footers, Effie, Keribee, and Plover, sailed for the 22ft.
championship against three Queenslanders, Stanley, Vic-
toria, and Caneebie. Effie again outsailed her opponents
and won easily, the three Sydney boats finishing in front
of the Bananalanders. Joe Donnelly had luck in the
Plover winning her first face, as Mr. Sam. Hordern had
agreed to give an increased price for her if successful in
her maiden effort. Repeated trials have since shown that
last year’s champion, the Effie, still holds pride of place.
• * * *
The Cyclists’ Touring Union of England has
appointed a “ consul ”at Sydney. Presumably the gen-
tleman will wear a cocked-hat and a sword and have
entrSe at Govt. House levies, or know the reason why.
* * * *
The cyclist is no respecter of persons. Gov.
Brassey and Premier Turner were standing at the edge of
the banked track in Melb. Exhibition Ground during the
A.N.A. wheel-race. A sprinter wishing to get through
had taken the high ground, and seeing their toes and—er
waistcoats where his wheel and arms would be, yelled
out, “ Get out of that, you fat old colonials.” The last
word is reported differently by the phonograph. There
was a howl of disgust, reproof, wonder, and laughter
mixed, but the cyclist says he doesn’t “ care a ball-bear-
ing if it was the Prince of Wales ; the cows want to be
taught sense. They have no more sense than a rabbit.”
* * • •
The soldier of Pompeii was not a circumstance
to Tom Coomber, clerk of the course at Vic. League of
Wheelmen’s sports, whose Draconian rule has frequently
been resented by visitors to the arena at cycle meetings.
Coomber assumed his old position at Melb. Exhibition
track on Foundation Day, and, during the afternoon, a
select coterie of the Naicest, headed by Gov. Brassey,
Mayor M'Eacharn, and others, was led on the sward by a
kow-towing committee of the A.N.A. As they halted in
the middle of the green a heat was about to start, and
the ever-vigilant Coomber came forward. “ You
gentlemen will have to. sit down,” he remarked,
glaring at Her Majesty’s representatives with a
steely eye, and, as the A.N.A. officials shook with
fear and awaited a thunderbolt from Heaven, the clerk of
the course added, in explanation : “ The public have paid
to see the race, and they can’t see through you ! ” The
explanation was even more fierce than the demand, and
Uncle Tom sank with a dull sickening thud to the ground,
from which position he and the rest of the suite viewed
the race. All the rest of the afternoon the ruler of Vic.
strolled round the Exhibition building with two sus-
picious-looking green spots on the knees of his grey tweed
pants.
• * * *
The cycling at the A.N.A. celebration at Melb.
goes, firstly, to show what a near thing Wren Matthews
was to the last Austral ; and, to prove that the
half-caste youth, Gordon, is the coming man. Matthews
won his £250 in the big race in a style that made one
marvel that he did not carry off the Austral, and it was
apparently only the unknown powers of the big Finnegan
and his assistant pacers that stopped the speedy young-
ster from getting up. Gordon it was who won the
League Cup of £5O on Saturday week, and he followed
up this victory with another of £4O at the A.N.A. meet-
ing. Gordon has good staying powers, a rattling finish
that leaves men—now reckoned better—standing, and he
has lots of pluck to carry him through.
» * * m
At the Police Club (Syd.) Swimming Carnival
F. Lane won the 300yds. championship in 3min. 51sec. ;
but it is a pity he did not go the distance all out, for as
he finished easily in 3.51, he might have knocked big
holes in the records. Bishop, who beat Lane in the
100yds. at Bathurst, was too unwell to start.
* * * *
The two old-timers, Dooley and Fogarty, met
up Coolgardie way recently, when Michael knocked James
out in a couple of rounds. Fogarty used to have a “ dirty
right ” in the old White Horse days, provided he could
get close to his opponent. Fogarty’s chief hit was a half-
arm blow, which he got on to C. Dunn in two matches,
one breaking his jaw, and the other knocking him in-
sensible. Dooley’s long left jabs would hold Fogarty
safe. Fogarty fought Choynski in the old Darlinghurst
Hall, Sydney, but after Choynski had dropped a few of
his piston-rod lefts on to Fogarty’s nose the latter coolly
pulled off the gloves, and said it was no use standing up
to be chopped to pieces.
* * * *
The little darkie, Jerry Marshall, who made
such a good mill with Griffo years ago at Darlinghurst
Hall, Sydney, beat Billy Needham at N.Y. last Nov.
Marshall was a great disappointment to Australians by
his poor showing in the States. After his Sydney per-
formances, it was expected he would more than hold his
own with the best at his weight in America, but he was
beaten by everybody. Pluto, the clever colored lad who
went to Klondyke last year, must now be back in the
States, as a Yankee paper lately said he was on the look-
out for a match in N.Y. How clever Pluto was may be
gauged from the fact that he fought three draws with
Griffo, twice with gloves and once with knuckles. Pluto
is a stead}’, shrewd little fellow, and, as he has never
knocked himself about, should still be able to hold the
Australian end up. And Australia is surely in want of a
champion. A few years back she could name one in
every class, but to-day her fighters are all has-beens—bar
Fitzsimmons, and it remains to be seen if he has not
earned a place in the list.
• • * *
George Hamilton, the 18-year-old Sydney boy,
who won the five-mile amateur championship recently,
has every chance of being selected to represent Australia
in the World’s Championships, to be held at Montreal,
Canada. Hamilton has a wonderful jump, and, as Bill
Martin puts it, “gives his opponent several lengths of
* Red Bird tail ’ to play with.”*
■*» * *
It having come to the ears of the Dunlop Tyre
Co. that many cyclists are under the impression that a
number of cycle agents hold stocks of the ’99 Multiflex
Dunlop Tyre," but refrain from selling the same owing to
their trying to work off their old stock first, the company
therefore desire to notify the riders of their tyres that
the new Australian ’99 tyre will not be placed on the
market until the end of February. By this time, the
company expect to be able to supply the demand for the
new tyre, which will be found the tyre par excellence for
all purposes, the improvements making it so simple to
handle that the merest tyro will be able to detach and
replace the new Muliflex Dunlop with the greatest of
ease.*
* * * *
In view of the smashings-up in all sorts of sport
lately, it seemed only natural that cricket should get its
share on Saturday, when young Norman Turnbull fell
whilst chasing a ball, terribly shattering his left arm.
Mr. Norman W. Turnbull, young Turnbull’s father, one of
the most widely-known men in N.S.W., is of Herculean
physique, and a decade or more ago was a champion
amateur weight-lifter and wrestler and an authority
generally on athletics. Young Turnbull is a splendidly-
grown lad and promises to develop the physique of the
old man. Fears are entertained that amputation will be
necessary.
* * * •
Strong-man Sampson’s feats pale before the pro-
posal made some years ago by. “ Mons.” Victor Becker,
during the tug-of-war contests in the Melbourne Exhibi-
tion-building. Annoyed at a decision given against the
French team, which he had been coaching, he offered to
pull, single-handed, against the entire assemblage—“one
down, t’other come on.”
* * * •
The once-famous Surrey wicket-keeper, Pooley,
who played in Australia, some years ago, is now a pauper
inmate "of Lambeth Workhouse. But it must be ad-
mitted that poor Pooley didn’t go the right way to get
himself provided for in his old age.
T. Drew, S.A.’s sprightliest lacrosseur—and
lacrosse has “caught-on ” more in Adelaide than in any
other Australasian city—goes over to Melbourne this
month to continue his medical course, which cannot now
be completed in his home town. Drew was tried last
season in the interprovincial cricket games, too, with
but moderate success. He is a son of a Broken Hill early-
bird.

Tom Cannon went under, recently, in America,
to one Tom Jenkins. Cannon then offered to Grseco-
Romanise any man in America for £5O a side. It is 12
years or more since Cannon was in Sydney, so he must now
be getting well into years. Yankeeland is now bristling
with terrible Turks and gruesome Greeks, herculean fel-
lows who are carrying all before them, so old Tom is not
likely to be long getting a match.
* * » *
Charlie Bannerman’s idea of doing away with
the follow-on-rule altogether is deserving of consideration.
The rule was made at ’Ome where matches have to be
finished in three days. Out here all big matches can be
prolonged till the crack of doom, and it is manifestly un-
fair to handicap a successful first innings.
* * * *
“ Melb. Abgus has the gall to quote this from a
London paper “ Every racing man would welcome the
starting-machine, excepting, perhaps, those whose path
on the turf is a crooked one. As they at present
start, jockeys not on the job can get away pretty much
where they like and do what they like in the early part of
a race without the public knowing whether a bad start or
anything unusual has brought about their favorite’s
defeat ; but with a starting-machine there would be no
half-measures. If they did not try to get off they would
be left a sufficient distance that everyone could see, and
next time the horse ran it would not benefit much by
previous trickery.” Now Australia has had three or four
years’ experience of the starting-machine, and during
that time the paths of the crooked ones have undoubtedly
grown crookeder than ever. The public here see jockeys
getting away badly and doing just what they like in the
earlier part of a race ; they see the “form” of horses
improving about 3st. within the space of a week—and
what happens ? Nothing. The excuse for an animal’s
defeat is “ bad start ” more often than not, and consider-
ing that the stewards never charge a jockey with having
done pretty much as he liked to avoid getting away, the
public would like to know where the virtue of the start-
ing-machine comes in.
6 m m m *
“P.A.” : Had often heard of fish being caught
by hand, but couldn’t believe it. Was out off Eden lately,
and couldn’t get a line to bottom for leather-jackets ; but,
by drawing line up slowly, induced them to follow bait to
where the boatman was holding over a piece of ripe shark.
They gathered thick round this, and he was able to put
about 50 in the boat with his hand in a few'minutes.
Their rough skin gives good grip.
* * * *
The first electrical auto car has appeared in
Melbourne —a buggy-like arrangement, on four pneu-
matic-tyred wheels, "somewhat larger than bike wheels.
It travels easily at about 10 miles an hour, and, so far, has
not caused any undue excitement among local horses.
It is the breeders that do the shying.
• • » *
There’s a ring of manliness in the confession of
the Queensland jockey arrested for stealing a racehorse.
Drink was driving him to the devil, he said. He thought
that a couple of years in gaol might do him good and
prompt the public to raise a little money for his wife.
• ft * 9
A North Queensland paper publishes a parson’s
tale of a scrum in his corner of the vineyard:. “ It was a
horrible sight. There were two strong men in the best
possible condition, knocking one another about for 15
rounds. And only me there ! Dear me ! They opened
the first round carefully, and Tom missed with his left,
and got Jim a daisy within half a hair of the point. He
went down like a log, but I got him up and fetched him
to with a drop of water from my bag. And he sat on my
knee till time was called. ‘Who called “Time?”’en-
quired a parishioner. ‘ I did,’ replied the parson. 1 You
see, there was no one else. They sparred the next round,
and made it very willing, and I saw that no undue advan-
tage was taken on either side. Jim went down again
from a chop in the wind, and I had to give him my knee.
Ah ! they were doing wrong, but poor Jim was suffering,
and I have always a knee for a sufferer. And I advised
him to work moire about the body, which he did, and he
vanquished Tom in the fifteenth round. And now they
both have faces on them like paint-tins which a cart-
wheel has passed over.”

More Sydney Telegraph (leading article of
January 30)
The other colonies have not said “no to anything;
New South Wales has. They accepted the Convention
■ill without comment of any kind; New South Wales
rejected it, with distinct comment upon the unsuitability
of some of its provisions.
Same old lie ! Also same old liar ! ButN.S.W.’s
unqualified acceptance of the Convention Bill by
53G7 majority stands on record just the same, and
the D T. struggles to put back the universe and
make it a minority all to no purpose.
There is no lese majeste about this, any-
how When the Kaiser was nearing Berlin, en
route from Jerusalem, etc., the Berlin leaders of
finance and stock-exchange magnates desired to
solid a deputation to welcome their Wilful Wil-
liam. But the authorities thought differently;
said they—“ Indeed no; our beloved monarch, on
his return, shall at least have a decent home-
welcoming. But were you gentlemen to be first
to meet him at the city gate, the first glimpse of
you would cause him to fancy he had taken the
wrong turning and was again entering Jerusalem.”
And then the gentleman, whose surnames ended
mostly in stein, sadly returned to their money-
spinning.
After the somewhat irreverent paragraph
in another column referring to Mr. E. J. H.
Knapp’s ca didature for the Governorship of
Norfolk Island had gone to press, it was printed
in the Sydney evening papers that Mr.
Knapp, while suffering from mental depres-
sion, had attempted suicide with a razor
and was not expected to live. The Bulle-
tin is sorry, for it only wanted to get rid of poor
Knapp in his epistolatory capacity, and did not
wish him to die, but only to go to Norfolk Island.
Many others will also much regret his terrible
misfortune, for the old blue-ribbon bachelor is
generally recognised as a man who means well and
has lived a life of self-sacrifice.
Henry C. Cargill, the talented writer who
for years, with characteristic frar ‘ss, let himself
be known to old-time Bulletin readers as “ The
Dipsomaniac,” is dead at the age of 40 or there-
abouts. There are many pressmen who though
made of mere Philistine stuff take to themselves
the title of “ Bohemian,” but not of these was he
who has passed away. Never did there live a man
batter fitted by genial recklessness and erratic
talent to , typify the world of which Henri
Miirger wrote. Few more talented journalists
have adorned the Australian press, nor, when
temporarily free from the invincible thraldom
against which he so often and desperately strove,
was there ever a more industrious or honorable man.
The deceased, who passed away in Brisbane hos-
pital, an hour or two after he had been admitted,
on breaking a blood-vessel, leaves a wife but no
family.

At the Gatton inquest suspect Burgess
when asked if he had ever been “in trouble,”
drily replied that he had “never been out of it.”
A Yankee sailor of 35 is a man who may reason-
ably be expected to have seen some “life.” Under
the heading “peculiar marks” in the discharge-
sheet Burgess is described as having old wounds
on his head, his arms, his back, his chest, his legs
and other parts of his body, evidence of the knives
he has run up against. Also he has a bullet-
wound in his right shoulder and another in his left
wrist. “Never been out of it.”
Sydney Morning Herald actually pub-
lished a leading article, the other day, to demon-
strate that the new century commences at the
beginning of 1900, instead of the beginning of
1901. The Herald’s argument
It seems inconceivable that any doubt could attach to
the solution of what appears one of the simplest of all
possible arithmetical questions. What do we mean by a
century? We mean merely a hundred years. In the
present case, having in view the event from which the
computation is taken, we mean a hundred birthdays. It
means this and it means no more. Just as a man or an
era is one year old on the first birthday reached, so is he
or it 10 years old on reaching the tenth birthday, and 100
years old on reaching the hundredth. The year, the
decade, and the century are complete when the first, the
tenth, or the hundredth anniversary is reached. It
is impossible to enter into the frame of mind of
those who argue that there must be a hundred
years and one year more to complete the cen-
tury. To the passenger from Sydney to Melbourne
the moment he reaches the hundredth mile-post on his
journey his f.rst century of miles is complete, and he has
not yet to travel eight furlongs to accomplish the number.
We are bound to suppose that those who maintain that
after the hundredth anniversary or the hundredth mile-
post is reached a year or a mile has still to be traversed
have some principle of numeration in their minds, but
we must confess that we are unable to understand what
it can be. . . . Clearly the century is complete at the end
of 1899.
And yet as you go from Sydney to Melbourne, or
from any place to somewhere, you don’t come to
the hundredth mile-post till you have got to the
end of the hundredth mile, and consequently you
don’t come to the 1900th mile-post or birthday or
anniversary till you have got to the end of the
1900th mile, or year, or whatever it is. Think of
the united intellect of the Herald being unable
to see a little thing like that!

Seven or eight weeks ago, Count Thun,
Premier of Austria, declared in the Reichsrath,
apropos of complaints that Germany was expelling
Austrian subjects, that every country had an
“ undisputed right ” to expel aliens who were con-
sidered “ undesirable,” and that the action of
Germany was neither a breach of international
law nor of treaty obligations. Now he is trying
to gammon Salisbury that a British colony has no
such right. And Premier Seddon simply winks
tbe other eve.

THE ’99
The opening of the New Season has brought with it some important changes in last year's models , and especially so in the
11 Austral Swift f a light machine which may be called a Road Racer , or Light Roadster, according to fancy. The history of
the “Austral Swift ” is shortly this: —
% .
Since the commencement of what we may call the “ Swift Boom,” the Australian public have consistently shown some unwillingness to accept the
recognised English models without some modification, and the modifications called for have almost invariably tended in one direction. A careful study of
the question led us to the conclusion that it would be more satisfactory to instruct the makers in Coventry what alterations were necessary to meet Australian
views, and to ship direct the perfected machine which we called the “ Austral Swift.”
Before going into detail, it may be as well to say that the chief feature aimed at in the “ Austral Swift ”is STYLE. This we insisted upon, as we have
found it waste of time to lay any model before the Australian public that did not at first glance claim to be STYLISH. The fact that the “ Austral Swift ”
is the most stylish machine ever seen in Australia proves to us that the Swift Coventry Company have carried out the instructions we gave them both in
letter and spirit, and the first consignment we are now unpacking satisfies us that the whole of the order is to be up to sample. Among the leading
features of the new machine we may mention the following :
A SHORT HEAD, assuring rigidity and strength.
. LARGE HUBS for smoother running.
PERFECT SIMPLICITY in pedal attachments, the pedal pins being screwed into the cranks with left and right threads.
BIG GEAR WHEELS for easier propulsion.
BROOK’S SADDLES as per general desire.
PERFECT FINISH in every part of the machine.
Another feature —and an important one, too—is the price, and here again we have endeavored to meet the wishes of our Patrons,
the “AUSTRAL SWIFT ”is £2O. Terms : £2l for six months’, and £22 for twelve months’, extension.

Sept. 28, 1895, says:
“ If I were asked which single medicine I should
prefer to take abroad with me, as-likely to be
most generally useful, to the exclusion of all
others, I should say Chlorodyne. I never travel
without it, and its general applicability to the
relief of a large number of simple ailments forms
its best recommendation.”
Rev. E. D. Madgwick, who died at Cook’s
River, near Sydney, a few days ago, was known
all over Australia, but especially in Queensland
and N.S.W., as the most valiant-tongued Orange-
man of the day. Unlike the political Orangeman
who fire-eats for votes, Madgwick sincerely belie ved
Orangeism to be a divinely-revealed religion which
men should fight and die for. He was born in
England and brought up on the Hunter River.
He ministered as a Wesleyan in Queensland (no
■real all-wool Anglican clergyman ever worriesabout
Orangeism) and as a C.E. about Sydney, but the
country knew him best for his lava oratory on the
color question.
Sands and M‘Dougall have been prompt as
usual in issuing their Melbourne Directory for the
year. This is the 43rd Melb. directory published
by the old firm, and the mass of information con-
tained therein seems almost too large to talk
abouL The name of every householder within
10 miles of the G.P.O. is duly recorded ; all empty
houses are marked vacant in the book ; also, the
map of Melb. and suburbs has been carefully
brought up to date. Messrs. Sands and Mac.
are making a specialty of pictorial printing in
three colors, and a specimen picture, enclosed in
the Directory, gives a touch of sentiment to that
otherwise severely practical volume.
Another Northern correspondent to The
Bulletin :
A careful scanning of the Q. political barometer strongly
indicates a further lease of power to the gang-in-posses-
sion after the coming general elections. Of the 72 sitting
members, the seats of some dozen only are seriously
threatened, and at time of wilting all the Ministers are a
“ certainty.” The Dickson crowd will probably lose two
or, at the most, three followers ; the Labor-party get a
couple, or less ; and the somewhat tired Drake will get a
joint or two more in his “tail.” Practically, it will be
“as you were.” Several causes may be given for this
truly dreadful state of affairs —(1) the clever manipulation
of the Election Act by the Buicockers ; (2) the supine
attitude of the Drake crowd, and its failure so far to
put a programme before the electors ; (3) the unworkable
41-plank platform of Glassev and co., of which they
refuse to abate a shaving ; and, lastly, that tired feeling
which permeates all things political and social in this
sluggish piebald land.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.. ,
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir,—For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Solis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all mv friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomoliiene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAYS
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “ Eczema Capitas,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M‘Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

“ Gr.F. ” to The Bulletin :
Re your recent comment on Maoriland R.O. Bishop
Grimes. I think there must be somemistake about his
having said that “one of his missionaries went on one
"'ccasion nearly 400 miles to irive the last sacrament to a
,<tan who had not seen a priest for 40 years.” He must
have meant that the dying sinner had not for that time
seen a priest officially as an absolver of sins. I’m a heretic
myself, but have observed in many countries the earnest-
ness with which the self-sacrificing-R.C. cleric endeavours
to brighten the last hours of a repenl it sinner. In the
R.C. religion itself lies the source of the universal spirit
of self-sacrifice on the part of the priests. It is not de-
pendent upon the disposition, or left to the vagaries of
any individual priest.

Beautiful item from Adelaide :
At the Aug. sittings of the Criminal Court, John Holt
was sentenced to seven years’ imprisonment for having
garroted John Odea, who said he had no doubt the
prisoner was one of the three men who assaulted him at
night time. Holt, in vehement language, protested his
innocence, nevertheless he was sentenced and the Judge
said he narrowly escaped a whipping. Recently two men
who three months later were sentenced for burglary, con-
fessed that it was they who had attacked Odea. Inquiries
are being made, but the police are inclined to believe that
the story has been made up by the three men, who are
confined in the Stockade together.
Of course the police are much more inclined to
think the story made up than that they captured
the wrong man and got him seven years. Any
two men would naturally be so anxious to get
seven years, with a chance of a flogging as well,
that they would make up a little thing like that.
Edward Dyson’s “Below and On Top”—
which contains all his best Australian stories
and many others nearly as good—is selling like
the proverbial ripe cherries. A first edition of
2000 was soon cleared out, and the demand con-
tinues, and grows, throughout Australia and in
Maoriland. The book promises to become a per-
manent institution. “ Below and On Top ” is
published at 2s. 6d. in paper wrapper; 3s. 6d.
neatly bound in red cloth. Messrs. Geo. Robert-
son & Co., who are about to place this budget of
Dyson’s yarns on the London market, expect it to
“go big ” in Hingland.*
During the month of last Sept, the New
South Wales Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
supplied the German warship Falke with 6000
quart bottles of Sydney Lager Bier for use on the >
voyage to Samoa and Apia. During the vessel’s
stay in our harbor 600 gallons of bulk Sydney
Lager were supplied her. Another tribute to the
excellence of the local lager comes from the Ger-
man .warship Buzzard, which ordered 5500 quart
bottles of Sydney Lager for the return voyage to
Kiel. During this vessel’s stay with us she
received 50 gallons of Sydney bulk lager daily.
Both ships’ crews cannot speak too highly of the
excellence of Sydney lager. As the firemen,
officers and crews come from the country that has
always been famous for lager production, it is
clear that the Sydney Lager Bier Brewing Co.
deserves all that has been said by every con-
noisseur in favor of its lager.*

In Sydney a few days ago a young man
was killed through the overcrowding of one of the
State-owned tramcars —he was hanging on to the
footboard when a motor on the other line struck
him. The State fines ’bus-owners and ferry
steamboat captains for overcrowding ; what does
it think of itself in the same circumstances ? Of
course it is no use for the State to fine itself in
the usual way—in other words, to indignantly
order itself to forfeit to itself a sum of money ;
but it might do some good if it fined the Railway
Commissioners and gave the money to the hospi-
tals, or to those dependent on the deceased. The
State makes a good thing out of overcrowding.
It gets extra revenue out of its trams by its over-
crowding, and it also gets the fines that are levied
on other people who commit the same offence.
The man who knows his wav about in this
hard, cold, sinful world always drinks Watson’s
Whisky and Sazerac Brandy. They put soul and
energy into him, and make this ancient and
rather battered globe seem young again ; and the
faded gum-tree is bright to look upon after them,
and the brown paper that blows through the
streets at the tail of the southerly buster
is less intolerable. Watson’s Whisky is an
old-established drink that is founded on
a rock ; it is mild and rich and rare
and the fierce blast of competition blows on it in
vain. Sazerac brandy is still older ; the sunshine
that shone on the grapes of France in the days of
the Buonapartes is bottled up in it ; also, it is as
pure as the new snowball, and has a flavor which
is Paradise condensed. And the only trouble is
that man hasn’t two mouths so that he can drink
both of them at once.*

Long ’Un S.A. railway service isn’t
always so very slow. “ Are you in a hurry, sir ?”
asked the guard on S.E. train, of the only passen-
ger. Told him wasn’t particular to a dav or two,
as Govt, was paying exes. “Well, there’s a
kangaroo inside the railway fence, and we’re
going to try and nail him.” But the kangaroo,
when chased, took to the line ; so they sooled the
engine on and had him beaten in the first mile,
when the ’roo stopped and started back again!
Driver reversed engine and off again in pursuit.
And so on, backwards and forrards for half an
hour, till ’roo, getting done-up, tried to cross line,
and was mincemeated by “the catcher.”

Dear Bulletin.— Be ability to bear pain.
All your cited cases are of compulsory endurance.
Let me now mention the Dyaks, who have their
front incisors drilled through and gold or copper
studs inserted, besides undergoing an unmention-
able operation which must necessarily be most
agonising. The suffering entailed in Chinese-girls’
foot-constriction, also, is awful, yet millions sub-
mit to it; and Buddhist priests in China submit
annually to having molten lead dropped upon their
heads as a penance. In China, too, where begging
by a sound adult is punishable, men have bound
copper wire round the ankles till the feet have
mortified and dropped off, and this only that they
might beg a few years till death ensued, preferring
it to a life of toil.— Yangan.

The lending department of our Public
Library (writes a Melb. correspondent), closely
following the traditions of the Turner Govt., is of
i very grasping disposition, chasing the humble
“ sprat ” and the casual “ tray ” with great vigor.
It charges 3d. a day for all books kept over time,
including Sundays and holidays. Recently a
borrower who lost his book duly reported this and
paid the cost of a new copy, but when, a month
later, the lost volume turned up he immediately
handed it to the librarian and suggested that as it
had not yet been replaced his money might be
returned. The official agreed, but set off as contra
a bill of 7s. 6d.—3d. per day fine for 30 days,
j This being more than the cost of the new book
' the borrower “ wasn’t taking any.” He was then
j desired to hand over the lost-and-found volume, it
| being the property of the Library trustees, in con-
sideration of which they would graciously remit
the fine ! It’s not safe to talk to that man about
the financial genius of the Turner Govt.

A Melb. daily wonders how it happens that “ an
artisan visiting England as a member of an Australian
Eleven will be welcomed in that part of the pavilion at
Lord’s which is reserved for amateurs, but he must not
row at Henley.” Well, a professional cricketer is the
paid battler for a club; he draws a salary and works
under contract, and his status is clearly defined. A
cricketer’s “amateur” qualification consists in his not
being a pro. At Henley, the powers that be are “ gentle-
men ” in the Oxford, Cambridge and Eton sense, and
their law, although snobbish at . heart, is not ridiculous.
An amateur oarsman, they rule, is one who doesn’t earn
his living by any considerable exercise of his muscles
ashore, and is in no way connected with business on the
river or at the river-side. A boat-builder’s son is dis-
qualified from racing at Henley regatta. The amateur
there is understood to have no muscles worth mentioning,
except what he has developed through pure love of sport.
Physical strength is absolutely essential to success on the
river where the men compete against one another for
prizes—singly or in couples, fours, anfi eights. At
cricket they don’t compete for prizes, nor does a very
strong man possess a tremendous advantage over a slim
one, consequently pros, and amateurs meet together and
mix on fair terms, and Sampson would not be disqualified
from performing with the Gentlemen v. the Players.
But, inasmuch as he lives by his muscle, Sampson
couldn’t rank as an amateur at Henley. This is about
the best bit of special pleading for exclusiveness and
haw-hawism that Tub Bulletin has ever been guilty of.
* * * >
The two most prominent Queensland amateur
oarsmen are Denis, a blacksmith, and Slack, a butcher ;
under N.S.W. Rowing Association’s rules both these
would be debarred, unless as cast-iron professionals.
* * * *
The short wager-boats rowed by the Australians
in England do not find favor with the English critics, but
they will get there all the same. Chris. Neilseri, who,
although not a boat-builder by profession, is an artist at-
the game, gave long versus short boats several trials on
Parramatta River, and always with same result —short
boat on top. The late young Cruise (who used to show a
great burst of speed for a short distance) and Neilsen one
morning tried several quarter-mile spurts, changing boats
each time, and whoever rowed the short boat won. Neil-
sen builds his own boats, and they are works of art: some
23ft. 6in. in length, weight under 241 b., very flat in the
floor, with only l|in. spring. The skin is all one piece,
drawn tightly over the timbers, but not nailed to them
as in the ordinary outrigger.
* * * *
Strange that Queensland, which is in most
matters ahead of S. A., should have to take humble pie
before the crow-eating province at cricket. Unlike S.A.,
Queensland has never produced a champion, unless
Coningham was once in that category. Perhaps in time,
however, when the kanaka ami the Jap. put on the togs
and compete for the smile of the little brown women in
the pavilions, Queensland will be able to play the other
provinces combined.
* * • *
Clem. Hill’s cricketing record from his debut in
first-class cricket in *94 to date is one that will, perhaps,
never be beaten. In that time he his made nearly 4000
runs, and holds the remarkable average for 98 innings of
43‘13. He has knocked up eleven centuries, four against
N.S.W., four against All England, and one each against
Victoria, Derbyshire and Surrey respectively, the highest
being 206 not out against N.S.W. In interprovincial
cricket this season he has an average of something over
60. As Clem, is only 21 he should last for ten or twenty
years yet, which is something for our bowlers to look for-
ward to.

The little dears who go to Sydney Cricket
Ground to gloat over the bicycle men’s calves by electric-
light, would, no doubt, be scandalised at Spanish girls
attending a bull fight. And yet, at the present rate of
going, the odds re a fatal or permanent injury are about

ville, who fell the other night and broke his skull, is dead.
* * * *
N.S.W. cycling authorities, backed up by
several local papers, constantly abuse the Vic. wheeling
bosses for trying to ruin the sport by countenancing bet-
ting, but meanwhile their own efforts to squash the evil
at Sydney are of a very harmless character. In the stand
enclosure at the recent electric-light races writer saw
representatives from all the city betting shops openly lay-
ing the odds and doing brisk biz., and no one seemed to
object. Betting on bike-races has evidently become part
and parcel of the sport.
* * * *
The League of Vic. Wheelmen should be more
careful in its advertising methods. Announced its recent
meeting by a house-to-house delivery of bogus “ sum-
monses” (to attend the sports) properly printed in regu-
lar form on blue paper, and the sight of the familiar
document gave quite a number of pysons a shock from
which they have hardly yet recovered.
'*• * »
Advice by the South African mail, just to hand,
confirms the defeat of Van Heeden, the South African
champion, in his match race with William Martin, at dis-
tances of 1,5, and 10 miles. Van Heeden is the rider vvho
lately created a big reputation for himself, by breaking
many important unpaced world’s records, but he struck
a “hot cake ” in Martin and his “ Red Bird Special,” as
he was badly beaten in every event. “ Plugger Bill ” has
just about as good a claim to the world’s championship
as any rider we know —having now defeated acknow-
ledged champions of Australia, England, America, Italy
and South Africa.*
* * * *
The stiffened gee-gee rarely gets home ; but he
does occasionally, and then, the cronk owner and the
books in the swim get their deserts. A recent glorious
instance was the case of a hurdler who had been bottled
up for months, and at last got fairly thrown into a hurdle
race, to be immediately pounced on by the public and
backed down to a short price before the owner got a
chance. Of course, he was “ put in the bag,” and the
fact made known to sundry bookies who lay the dead ’un
till the cows come home and whack the proceeds with
the owner afterwards ; but this time the nag evidently
thought it was too good a thing to miss, and, refusing to
be pulled back, fall, or run off, he won with his head in
the air by many lengths. The horse was cheered for his
brilliant win, and the books decided they had been had,
and resolved never to put one “in the bag” for said
owner again ; whilst the punters, and they were many,
who had been told by the obliging owner to get on to his
good thing, unknowingly rubbed it in by rolling up to
thank him for the information and buy him wine.
An Acquired Knowledge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The shop was nearly empty. In fact the last
customer left on my entrance.
The barber looked up from his strop as I filled
the doorway. He was a pale man with biggish
head.
His mouth opened with a click—" Shave or
hair-cut ? ”
I pulled off my hat and coat and sat on the pre-
pared throne. In a few moments he had.my chin
lathered.
“ ’Orrible attemp’ at sooicide, that, yesterday,
sir! ’ he murmured as the razor jumped a wart on
my left cheek.
“ Yes,” said I, “ but if the man had only under-
stood a little more about physiology, he wouldn’t
have made such a mess of the job.”
“Hindeed, sir.”
He went on quietly shaving till he had half
finished, then, dipping the razor in hot water, he
asked in a peculiar tone, “ W’y, could you cut a
throat better’n ’e did ? ”
“ Certainly,” said I; “ I’m a doctor and ought
to understand a little about that sort of thing.”
Being a newly-fledged surgeon, I was naturally
a little vain and anxious to show off my know-
ledge. ‘‘You understand,” I explained, “if he
had drawn the knife from under the left ear
and so on, severing the vein you see here ”
(pointing at the big artery in my own throat),
“his recovery wouM have been hopeless. He
would have bled to death in a few minutes.”
The barber nodded his head, and his eyes
seemed to me a little queer as he bent over me
again and lifted the razor.
A horrible thought flew to my brain. . . . His
damp fingers seemed to pluck the skin and judge
the throat.
I gripped the long arms of the chair and closed
my eyes to shut out his beady blue ones.
He suddenly stopped working.
“Excuse me, sir ; f think someone calls.” And,
wiping his hands on his apron, he vanished behind
a private door.
The gas had not yet been lit, and it was getting
dark. I sat in the chair, looking at the shadowy
reflection of my scared face and white chin in the
big mirror opposite. He seemed a long time in
returning, and I felt lonely. The lamps outside
were beginning to flare, and I could hear boys
crying the news. Still he did not come.
I began to grow impatient, got off the chair,
picked up a paper and tried to read, but the light
was too dim. I went back and sat down again.
But no barber. Several customers came in and
punched at the outer counter, but getting no
response left almost immediately in a cloud of
curses. And yet he did not come. Suddenly the
private door opened, and a little figure with a
white apron darted hurriedly in. He was evi-
dently the assistant.
“Guess yer’ll ’ave ter finish yer shave some-
where else, mister.”
“ Why, this is preposterous ! Has Mr. Peters
forgotten me and gone to his tea? Confound his
impudence ! Please come here and finish the job
for me.”
“ Not much! Ole Peters’ cut ’is froat, an’ I’m
orf for the cops ! ”
EMSEY.
“Daylight Mac.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mao. was mostly sitting on his doorstep waiting
for daylight. At the first streak he called his
men, who came out all together on one pair of
legs. He, it, or they answered to the name of
Brown. Now, Brown had to work twice as hard
as anybody else’s man to keep his billet. Also,
he worked two days a day. That quadrupled
him. So Brown was Mac.’s men.
Mac. had the name of being mean. He called it
“’conomy.” The menu for breakfast at Macs
was porridge, meat and pumpkin. Each man had
two plates. One was . used for everything ; the
other for covering it afterwards —to keep the cat
and dog from licking it. At dinner-time the
cover was removed, and the plate used again.
Same at supper. Then the plate and the cover
were wiped for next day. The cloth was washed
on Sunday—weather permitting.
When Mac boiled a piece of meat he’d cut oft
enough for each, and put it back in the water.
Next meal it was hauled up through the cake of
fat, the required quantity cut off, and returned
once more to the pot-liquor, where it was kept till
finished. Kept the flies away, and saved the cost
of a dish-cover. Nothing was ever wasted at
If a tramp happened along at a critical moment
Mac would rush in to his amalgamated men.
“ Clear awa’, mon ! Clear awa’, wul ye ! ”
“ What’s up ? ” asks the amalgamation.
“ Losh, mon, there’s a tramp makin’ fo’ ’ere, an
I wadna hae the sicht o’ tucker increase his appe-
tite.”
The tucker is put out of sight, and when the
tramp arrives Mac asks him for a smoke ! . . .
Mac was economical, too, with his clothes. He
never took his hat off at table, and never lifted it
to a lady, because fingering it wore it out. There
was nothing more extravagant to his mind than
wearing a shirt in bed. He turned-in stark naked.
Consequence was his shirt lasted twice as long.
He took his boots off to go riding, and left his hat
at home on cloudy days : saved ’em a lot of wear.
In the bush, after lighting his pipe, Mae would
blow out the match and put the stem back in the
box. At night, the slush-lamp or the fire would
do as well as a head. Thus one match would light
several pipes. Once he borrowed a match from
his men, and, after using it, returned him the
stem. “I owe ye a head, me mon,” he said.
Mac was once in love, and had serious thoughts
of getting married. But when he came to size-up
his Jean, he found that it would take far too much
print and calico to cover her. So he let her slide,
and deoided to wait till a much smaller woman
should turn up.
One day I happened to call at Mac.’s about
dinner-time, and as I had tucker with me Mac.
asked me to fetch it in and eat it at his table?
Thought I’d leave some, I suppose. Failing that,
I was bound to knock off some crumbs. Anyhow,
I sat down and “ proceeded.” Mac. went to his
place —with his hat on and his boots off —and the
first thing he did before seating himself was to
take his trousers off also. I was quite astonished.
I’d never seen a man dress for dinner that way
before.
The Brown combination explained afterwards :
“ It saves the seat of his pants ! ”
EDWARD S. SORENSEN.


Burglars who broke into a Melb. pub. the
other night stole, amongst other things, the pro-
prietor’s insurance policy against loss by burglary.
Which recalls the Bendigo man who invented a
burglar-trap, and was himself caught in it when
he started after the burglar who was getting away
with his valuables.
At Heidelberg (Vic.), a few days ago, a
German musician objected to “ kiss the buke.”
P.M. Keogh suggested an affirmation if he had
any conscientious objection. “No, it’s not that,”
replied the witness, “but—so many have kissed
it. That’s all.” He was ordered to open it and
find a clean place, which was done after much
scrutinising care ; having lightly pressed his lips
thereto, he dropped the dirty volume in haste,
shudderingly. Yet this P.M. doesn’t seem to be
aware that Vic. law makes provision for taking
the oath in the Scottish manner—with right hand
upraised. “ Kiss the germ-grower ! ”
Stead, the pious, red-headed meddler of
the Review of Refuse, is selling a “Twentieth
Centum New Testament,” which is the ordinary
New Testament translated into up-to-date lan-
guage. If Stead brings the whole Bible up to
date, and his volume gets a wide circulation, he
will probably do more to kill the Christian belief
than any other man living. The hold which that
old, long-enduring volume has on the public lies
greatly in its language. It is the only book in
general circulation which is written in archaic
diction ; its stately old-world style makes it a
thing apart from the rushing, ephemeral ruck of
literature. And if it comes down from its pedes-
tal to talk in the ordinary language of the street-
corner and of Stead, then it will have to hustle
at the street-corner for its literary existence just
like any other volume. Stead, the uneasy Non-
conformist, revises the old work like this :
“Gathering the twelve round Him, Jesus said to them
— ‘ Listen ! We are going up to Jerusalem; and there
everything that is written in the Prophets will be done to
the Son of Man. For He will be handed over to the
heathen and mocked and insulted, and spat upon ; they
will flog Him and then put Him to death ; and He will
rise up again on the third day.’ The Apostles did not
take in any of this ; His meaning was unintelligible to
them, and they did not understand what He was
saying.”
“ The Apostles did not take in any of this ! ”
The next revision will probably set forth that the
Apostles didn’t drop to it, or that the twelve
failed to tumble, or that the Lord’s statement fell
flat, or that his followers “ weren’t taking any.”

A PALE, THIN GIRL MADE BRIGHT AND
SPRIGHTLY.
Miss Ada Richabds, of Karabar-street, North Auburn, near Sydney,
says“ About twelve months ago my strength seemed to fade. I suffered
most severely from the effects of the heat, although before this I had been
fairly strong. My blood became very thin and poor. I was seized with
extreme lauguor and weakness, and also severe attacks of giddiness, and I
was'often compelled to sit down that I might recover myself. My appetite
deserted me,.and I could only crawl about the house. These painful symp-
toms'were accompanied with neuralgia, and most severe pains in the chest
and sides. My spirits sank, and I became most depressed and disheartened.
I thought every fresh month might see the end of my sufferings, but relief
seemed as far off as ever.
“My system was completely debilitated ; I felt as if I could do no
work, and was drowsy and exhausted all day. I was much reduced in
figure, and my face and lips were quite bloodless. Any noise upset me.
At last I purchased two boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills at the local store.
I started taking the pills according to the directions, but experienced no
relief till I had nearly finished the second box. Then I began to notice a
change for the better, and I determined to persevere, for anything was pre-
ferable to my former statt , I procured more, and continued ; a steady
improvement now took place, much to my relief. As days passed on, and I
still progressed, it seemed as if I were gradually emerging from a dark wood
into the open sunlight.
“ Every day my daily work was less of a burden to me. I took nine
boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and they effected a
complete cure. The past seems just like a dreadful dream from which I’ve
awakened. My blood has become richer, and I’ve regained my color. The
oppressive pains in my chest and sides passed away, and I began to eat with
an appetite, Even in the hottest weather I feel strong and light-hearted.
I am stronger than ever I was in my life, and can rise early, and walk and
eat as well as anybody could wish.

The Nameless Bird.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A lonely seabird, winging
O’er wave and moaning bar,
To wild and ruined places,
Unlit by moon or star.
The gleam upon its white crest,
The light upon its wing,
Far deeper than the beauty
Of any earthly thing.
A trail of splendour bringing,
In silent ways apart,
The touch of wild desiring
Aflame to brain and heart.
Oh ! darker grew the spaces
Between the earth and sky,
And mournful on the stillness
The wailing of its cry.
A sullen light from seaward
O’er frowning headlands grew,
And every covert nestled
A foul and noisome crew.
And strangeness gathered over,
Like shadows of unrest,
The sombre city rising
Out of the doomed west.
About its spires for ever
The nameless bird shall be ;
The stain upon its white breast
To bring again to me
The long and lonesome journey,
O’er mountain ways forlorn,
With wild-eyed shadows keeping
Their vigil wild and worn ;
The cry upon the niglitwind,
The rushing sweep and swing,
And then, 0, God 1 the surging
Of drooping crest and wing.
And though by distant casement
Oft-times I sit and dream—
As ’neath the gathered starlight
Barbaric all things seem—
Of wondrous beauty trailing
The bye ways of regret;
The hopes so fairly carven,
In dreams of wisdom—yet,
A spectral light shall glimmer
On foam-tipt isles afar,
And every wind shall fathom
The moaning on the bar.
A weird and wistful crooning
Shall come from ways apart—
-01 Bird of Desolat ion 1
Engraven on my heart.
Sydney.
PATRICK CARNEY.
On Our Selection.—Casey.
[FOR THE BULLTIN.]
It was when Dad and Dave were away after kan-
garoo-scalps, and Joe was looking after the selec-
tion, minding the corn, keeping Anderson’s and
Dwyer’s and Drown’s and old mother Murphy’s
cows out of it, and chasing goannas away from
the front door, that Casey came to our place. A
starved-looking, toothless little old man with a
restless eye, talkative, ragged and grey ; and he
walked with a bend in his back (not a hump), and
carried his chin in the air. We never saw a man
like him before. He spoke rapidly, too, and
watched us all as he talked. Not exactly a
“ traveller” ; he carried no swag or billycan, and
wore a pair of boots much too large. He seemed
to have been “ well brought up ” —he took off his
hat at the door and bowed low to Mother and Sal,
who were sitting inside, sewing. They gave a
start and stared. The dog, lying at Mother’s
feet, rose and growled. Towzer wasn’t used to
the ways of people well brought up.
The world had dealt harshly with Casey, and
his story went to Mother’s heart. “ God buless
y’,” he said when she told him he could have some
dinner; “but I’ll cut y’ wood for it; oh, I’ll
cut y’ wood ! ” and he went to the wood-heap and
started work. A big heap and a blunt axe ; but
it didn’t matter to Casey. He worked hard, and
didn’t stare about, and didn’t reduce the heap
much, either; and when Sal called him to dinner
he couldn’t hear—he was too busy. Joe had to
go and bring him away.
Casey sat at the table and looked up at the
holes in the roof through which the sun was
shining.
“ Ought t’ be a cool house,” he remarked.
Mother said it was.
“ Quite a bush-house.”
“Oh, yes,” Mother said—“we’re right in the
bush here.”
He began to eat and, as he ate, talked cheerfully
of selections and crops and old times and bad
times and wire fences and dead cattle. Casey was
a versatile ancient. When he was finished he
shifted to the sofa and asked Mother how many
children she had. Mother considered and said,
“ twelve.” He thought a dozen enough for any-
one and said that his mother, when he left home,
had twenty-one. —all girls but him. That was
forty years ago and he didn’t know how many she
had since. Mother and Sal* smiled. They began
to like old Casey.
Casey took up his hat and went outside, and
didn’t say “good-day ” or “ thanks ” or anything.
He didn’t go away, either. He looked about the
yard. A panel in the fence was broken. It had
been broken for five years. Casey seemed to
know it. He started mending that panel. He
was mending it all the evening.
Mother called to Joe to bring in some wood.
Casey left the fence, hurried to the wood-heap,
carried in a armful, and asked Mother if she
wanted more. Then he returned to the fence.
“ J-oe,” mother screeehed a little later, “look
at those cows tryin’ to eat the corn.”
Casey left the fence again and drove the cows
away, and mended the wire on his way back.
At sundown Casey was cutting more wood, and
when we were at supper he brought it in and put
some on the fire, and went out again slowly.
Mother and Sal talked about him.
“Better give him his supper,” Sal said, and
Mother sent Joe to invite him in. He didn’t
come in at once. Casey wasn’t a forward man.
He stayed to throw some pumpkin to the pigs.
Casey slept in the barn that night. He slept in
it the next night, too. He didn’t believe in shift-
ing from place to place, so he stayed with us alto-
gether. He took a lively interest in the selection.
The house, he said, was in the wrong place, and
he showed Mother where it ought to have been
built. He suggested shifting it, and setting a
hedge and ornamental trees in front and fruit
trees at the back, and making a nice place of it.
Little things like that pleased Mother. Any-
wa Y’. w °md sometimes say to Sal, he s a
useful old man, and knows how to look after things
about the place.’’ Casey did. Whenever any
watermelons were ripe, he looked after them, and
hid the skins in the ground. And if a goanna or
a crow came and frighteued a hen from her nest
Casey always got the egg, and when he had
gobbled it up he would chase that crow or goanna
for its life and shout lustily.
Joe got on well with Casey. Casey’s views on
hard work were the same as Joe’s. Hard work,
Joe thought, wasn’t necessary on a selection.
Casey knew a thing or two—so he said. One
fine morning, when all the sky was blue and the
butcher-birds whistling strong, Dwyer’s cows
smashed down a lot of the fence and'dragged it
into the corn. Casey, assisted by Joe, put them
all in the yard and hammered them with sticks,
Dwyer came along.
‘ Those cattle belong to me,” he said angrily.
“ They belongs t’ me,” Casey answered, “ until
you pay damages."’ Then he put his back
to the slip-rails and looked up aggressively
into Dwyers face. Dwyer was a giant beside
Casey. Dwyer didn’t say anything—he wasn’t
a man of words—but started throwing the rails
down to let the cows out. Casey flew at him.
Dwyer quietly shoved him away with his long,
brown arm. Casey came again and fastened on
to Dwyer. Joe mounted the stockyard. Joe
seized Casey with both hands, then there was a
struggle—on Casey’s part. Dwyer lifted him up
and carried him away &nd set him down on his
back, then hastened to the rails. But before he
could throw them down Casey was upon him
again. Casey never knew when he was beaten.
Dwyer was getting annoyed. He took Casey by
the back of the neck and squeezed him. Casey
humped his shoulders and gasped. Dwyer stared
about. A plough-rein hung on the yard. Dwyer
reached for it Casev veiled “ Murder ' ” Dwver
f 1A r i!fU, I?!; ° * . Cj y
fastened one end of the rope round Casey s body
underthearms—and stared about again. Andagain
“Murder!” from Casey. Joe jumped off the
yard to get further away. A tree, with a high
horizontal limb, stood near. Dad once used it as
a butcher’s gallows. Dwyer gathered the loose
rein into a coil and heaved it over the limb, and
hauled Casey up. Then he tied the end of the
Jo.
as he walked away, ‘‘ cut the rope.'”
Casey groaned, and one of his boots dropped
off. Then he began to spin round-to wind up
and unwind and wind up again. Joe came near
and eyed the twirling form with joy.
Mother and Sal arrived, breathless and excited,
They screeched at Joe.
“Undo th’ r-r-rope,” Joe said, “an’ he’ll come
w-w -wop.”
Sal ran away and procured a sheet, and Mother
and she held it under Casey, and told Joe to un-
fasten the rope and lower him as steadily as he
could. Joe unfastened the rope, but somehow it
pinched his fingers and he let go, and Casey fell
through the sheet
* * *
For three weeks Casey was an invalid at our
place. He would have been invalided there for
the rest of his natural life only Dad came home
and persuaded him to leave.

“Go and Tell the Others.”
Theke were eleven of us in all, four women and
seven men. We were passengers on board the
sailing ship Black Squall, 900 tons, bound from
London to Calcutta. The crew had mutinied and
marooned us on Amsterdam Island—a great,
bleak rock in the Indian Ocean. Ten days passed.
We had consumed most of our provisions and all
our water. Thus far we had vainly sought water
on the island. No rain from the heavens, no sail
on the sea ! Our tongues were cracking like up-
lands in a drought. You have read of the tor-
tures of thirst. May fortune confine your know-
ledge to that.
One more attempt; the last. Two of the men—
the least feeble —started. Hours went by, hours
that smote like hammers and burned like clots of
molten lead. Exhausted and despairing the men
lighted upon a spring half hidden under a remote
cliff. Each took a few swallows. Then one of
them fell fainting on the ground, just able to say
to his companion, “ Go—go tell the others I”
People say that suffering is the mother of selfish-
ness. Often it is so; not always, thank God.
Anyway, the sense of relief ever glows with gratis
tude and pity. Read the following—a woman’s
story—and you will see why the two are paired in
my mind.
“Prom my childhood I have been subject to
headache and dizziness. At the age of seventeen
I was very weak and ill. My tongue was furred,
my appetite poor. My food seemed to give me no
strength ; I was always tired. My hands and
feet were cold and clammy, and my skin of a
creamy hue. One day, whilst in service at Mrs.
Pirth’s, Park Farm, Thornhill, I visited my home.
On arriving there I had great pain and fluttering
at the heart; I could scarcely stand. It so
frightened my mother that she got some neigh-
bours to assist me to the doctor’s. He said, ‘ Your
heart is in a shocking state.’
“ He gave me medicines, but I was not relieved
by ihem. Getting worse, I gave up my situation,
being too ill to leave home. Soon I was seized
with a nasty cough and irritation of the throat
and chest; I could get no sleep on account of it.
I would sit up in bed until daybreak coughing and
spitting, and was, of course, worse tired than
when I went to bed. My legs trembled so that I
could not stand or walk much ; I was not able to
wash or dress myself. In this condition I re-
mained month after month, growing weaker
daily. On one occasion I was so bad that mother
thought 1 was dying, and fetched Mrs. Senior, a
neighbor, saying, ‘ Do come and help me .’
“In January, 1890, I went to the Dewsbury
Infirmary, where I was under treatment for six
weeks. The doctors gave me iron mixture and
other tonics, but they had no effect. Other young
girls were there, being doctored for ailments like
mine, to as little purpose as far as I could see.
“I next tried change of air, and went to Hali-
fax, also to Batley, without benefit. I continued
to waste away, and people said I would never re-
ln May, 1891, I first read of Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, in a book that was left at our
house.
“ Mother got me a bottle from Mr. J. Day, the
chemist, Thornhill Lees, and I began taking it.
After taking only a few doses I found a,_little
relief, the heaviness of my chest and the" pain
at my heart were easier, and my food digested
and agreed with me. I now grew stronger
every day, and was soon able to go back
to iny work. But I kept on taking the
Syrup still, and, to the surprise of every
one, the color came to my cheeks, and I have since
enjoyed the best of health. • All my family were
delighted, as you may well believe. (Signed)
Miss Hannah Milnes, 18 Walker’s Buildings,
Brewery Lane, Thornhill Lees, Dewsbury, Oc-
tober 12th, 1892.
“P.S. — If we could have afforded it, we should
have published all the particulars of my case in the
newspapers, that others might know where to look
for a remedy. —H.M.”
There! that is it. She wanted to tell the
others. And by printing her story we help her to
tell them where to find a cure for indigestion and
dyspepsia and its dangerous consequences when
all hope seems to have faded away.

Adamstown, September 11th, 1896.
BONNINGTON & CO., Manufacturers of Irish Moss, &c.,
179 Harris Street, Sydney.
Dear Sirs, —Being a sutierer for years from fibroid phthisis and bronchitis, I de-
sire to add my testimony in favor of your Irish Moss. As a soother of irritating
cough, with much expectoration, I have found it to be the most effectual of anything
I have tried. It loosens the mucus, which, therefore, comes more readily away, and
the cough is thereby considerably pacified.
I remain, Yours respectfully, ’
THOMAS STOKES.
